Proving God's Existence: Two Ways and the Argument from Bodies
Outline of first two topics on proving God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methods for proving God's existence and the specific argument from the world of bodies mentioned in the chapter outline.

Core Idea
God's existence is proven through two distinct rational ways, one based on bodies.

This lesson introduces the first two topics from the author's outline on the divine essence. The author signals that there are two ways to prove God's existence, and he highlights a specific argument based on the world of bodies. These initial points set the stage for deeper discussions later in the chapter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the brief enumeration of four topics. Then read the translation.
Focus on the first two items: 'في إثباته وفيه طريقان' and 'الاستدلال بعالم الأجسام على وجود الصانع'.
2
Identify the 'two ways'
The author says 'في إثباته وفيه طريقان' (on proving Him, and in it are two ways). Consider what these two ways might be; they will be explained later in the chapter.
The text does not name the ways here; it only signals there are two.
3
Understand the argument from bodies
The second topic is 'الاستدلال بعالم الأجسام على وجود الصانع' (the argument from the world of bodies for the existence of the Maker). This is a specific argument that uses physical bodies to reach the Creator.
Note that 'عالم الأجسام' means the world of bodies, likely referring to contingent physical objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه مباحث :
الفَصْل الأوّل
في الذات
١ - في إثباته وفيه طريقان .
٢ - الاستدلال بعالم الأجسام على وجود الصانع.
٣ - ذات الواجب تخالف الممكنات .
٤ - الواجب لا يحتاج إلى إثبات كونه أزلياً أبدياً.
And in it are investigations: The first chapter: On the Essence. 1. On proving Him, and in it are two ways. 2. The argument from the world of bodies for the existence of the Maker. 3. The essence of the Necessary differs from the contingents. 4. The Necessary does not need proof of His being eternal a parte ante and eternal a parte post.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an introductory outline for the first chapter of a section on the divine essence. The author begins with 'وفيه مباحث' (and in it are investigations), indicating that the chapter will contain several distinct topics. He then lists four points: (1) Proving the existence of God, with the remark that there are two methods (طريقان) for this proof. (2) A specific argument: the argument from the world of bodies (عالم الأجسام) to establish the existence of the Creator (الصانع). (3) A doctrinal point: the essence of the Necessary Being (الواجب) is different from contingent beings (الممكنات); this touches on divine transcendence (tanzīh). (4) Another doctrinal point: the Necessary Being does not require a separate proof for His eternity; being eternal without beginning (أزلي) and without end (أبدي) is inherent. The author is providing a roadmap for the chapter, and each of these topics will be elaborated in the following pages.

Key Takeaway

Divine Transcendence: The Unique Essence and Self-Evident Eternity
Outline of last two topics on divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the essence of the Necessary Being is fundamentally different from contingent beings and that His eternity (without beginning or end) requires no separate proof.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's essence is unique and inherently eternal without need for proof.

This lesson continues with the last two points of the chapter outline. The author asserts that the essence of the Necessary Being is unlike contingent beings, and that His eternity (without beginning or end) is self-evident. These points establish divine transcendence and self-sufficiency, key concepts in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the last two items: 'ذات الواجب تخالف الممكنات' and 'الواجب لا يحتاج إلى إثبات كونه أزلياً أبدياً'.
Notice that these are declarations, not arguments.
2
Reflect on the difference between the Necessary and contingents
The author says 'ذات الواجب تخالف الممكنات' (the essence of the Necessary differs from the contingents). This means that the Necessary Being (Allah) is not like created things in His essence.
This is a principle of divine transcendence (tanzīh).
3
Understand the self-evident eternity
The fourth point states that the Necessary does not need a proof of being eternal a parte ante (azalī) and a parte post (abadī). This implies that eternity is an essential attribute of the Necessary Being.
The term 'أزلياً أبدياً' covers both beginninglessness and everlastingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Proof of God's Existence: Two Methods and the First Aspect
The author introduces the proof for God's existence and presents two methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core argument for God's existence based on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, and the first of the two aspects that demonstrate this impossibility.

Core Idea
Contingent existence demands a necessary being; otherwise, circularity or infinite regress results.

This lesson introduces the rational proof for God's existence as outlined by theologians and philosophers. The author presents two methods and begins to unpack the arguments that make the existence of a necessary being unavoidable. You will encounter the first of two 'aspects' (وجوه) that explicitly refute the possibility of a world without God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Pay attention to key terms like 'موجود واجب' and 'محدث قديم'. Notice where the text cuts off at the second aspect.
2
Identify the two methods
The author says there are two methods for theologians and philosophers. Find the summary statement that explains both: contingent existences need a necessary existent, and originated things need an ancient originator.
These two methods share the same logical foundation: impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.
3
Understand the core argument
The core argument is that without a necessary existent, we would have circularity or infinite regress, which are impossible. The author mentions that some might think they can avoid this by rejecting the 'preference without a preponderator' argument, but the correct path is to directly argue for a necessary being.
4
Analyze the first aspect
The first aspect states: if there is no necessary being, then a contingent being would have to exist from itself, which is absurd. This is a direct proof that there must be a necessary being.
This is a concise logical argument: the existence of contingency itself necessitates a necessary cause.
5
Anticipate the second aspect
The second aspect begins but is incomplete in this passage. It deals with the totality of contingents requiring an independent agent. Note how this argument differs from the first.
We will complete this in a later lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في إثباته وفيه طريقان للمتكلمين والحكماء حاصلها أنه لا بد للموجودات الممكنة من موجد واجب، والمحدثة من محدث قديم لاستحالة الدور والتسلسل، وقد يتوهم الاستغناء عن بطلان الترجيح بدون مرجح فيقال: لا بد من موجود لا يحتاج إلى الغير دفعاً للدور والتسلسل أو عن بطلان الدور والتسلسل فيذكر وجوه . الأول : لو لم يكن في الموجودات واجب لزم وجود الممكن من ذاته، وفساده بين. الثاني : مجموع الممكنات أعني المأخوذ بحيث لا يخرج عنها واحد، لا بد لها لا مكانها من مستقل بالفاعلية، وهو لا يجوز أن يكون نفسها، ولا كل جزء منها، وهو
The first investigation concerning His proof, and in it are two methods for the theologians and philosophers, the summary of which is that contingent existences necessarily require a necessary existent, and originated things require an ancient originator due to the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress. And one might imagine dispensing with the invalidation of preference without a preponderator, so it is said: there must necessarily be an existent that does not need another to avoid circularity and infinite regress; or concerning the invalidation of circularity and infinite regress, so he mentions aspects. The first: if there were no necessary being among existents, then the existence of the contingent from itself would be necessary, and its corruption is evident. The second: the totality of contingents—I mean those taken such that none of them is excluded—necessarily requires, due to its contingency, an independent agent in effectuality, and it is not permissible that it be itself, nor any part of it, and it is
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage introduces the proof for God's existence (إثباته) and outlines two methods (طريقان) used by theologians (المتكلمين) and philosophers (الحكماء). The core idea is that contingent existences (الموجودات الممكنة) necessarily require a necessary existent (موجود واجب), and originated things (المحدثة) require an ancient originator (محدث قديم). This is because circularity (الدور) and infinite regress (التسلسل) are impossible. The author then mentions that some might think they can dispense with the argument of 'preference without a preponderator' (الترجيح بدون مرجح), but the correct approach is to argue that there must be an existent that does not need another (موجود لا يحتاج إلى الغير) to avoid circularity and infinite regress. He then lists two of the 'aspects' (وجوه) that demonstrate the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress. The first aspect: if there were no necessary being, then a contingent being would have to exist from itself, which is absurd (فساده بين). The second aspect: the totality of contingents (مجموع الممكنات) as a whole, considered without exception, requires an independent agent (مستقل بالفاعلية) because it is contingent; that agent cannot be the totality itself nor any part of it. The argument is cut off at this point.

مستقل بالفاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can dispense with the argument against 'preference without a preponderator' and still prove God's existence.
Correction: The passage asserts that one must directly argue for a necessary existent to avoid circularity and infinite regress; the argument from preference without a preponderator is not a separate shortcut.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do contingent existences require a necessary existent?
A) Because circularity and infinite regress are impossible.
B) Because contingent things are created by humans.
C) Because the universe is eternal.
D) Because all existents are necessary.

Key Takeaway
The existence of contingent beings logically demands a necessary being to avoid the absurdities of circularity and infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that contingent beings cannot exist without a necessary being. Consider your own existence: do you truly live as if you are contingent upon Allah at every moment, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient? What daily habit reveals your reliance or lack thereof?
?
The passage argues that if there were no necessary being, every being would have to exist from itself—an absurdity. How does this logical necessity challenge the way you prioritize your life? If God is the only necessary being, what does that mean for the things you chase as if they were necessary?
?
The author rejects the idea that you can avoid the need for a preponderator. Have you ever tried to explain away your need for God by attributing events to chance or natural processes without acknowledging the ultimate cause? How can you guard against that temptation in your thinking?

Introduction to the Proof of the Necessary Being
Introduction to the proof with two methods and the necessity of a Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the proof of God's existence relies on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, and that theologians and philosophers agree on the necessity of a Necessary Being.

Core Idea
All contingent things need a Necessary Being; circularity and infinite regress are impossible.

This lesson begins the first chapter on proving the existence of Allah. The text introduces two main approaches used by theologians and philosophers. Both converge on the same conclusion: contingent beings require a necessary originator. This conclusion is grounded in the logical impossibility of circular reasoning and infinite chains of dependency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا بد للموجودات الممكنة من موجد واجب'.
2
Identify the two ways mentioned
The text states there are two ways (طريقان) for theologians and philosophers. Identify the shared conclusion of these two ways.
The conclusion is the same: the necessity of a Necessary Being.
3
Understand the core argument
The reasoning is based on the impossibility of circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل). Reflect on why these are logically impossible and how they necessitate a self-sufficient originator.
The text also hints that some may rely on the principle of preponderance without a preferrer, but the primary proof is via impossibility of circularity and regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه طريقان : قال : المبحث الأول في إثباته (المبحث الأول في إثباته (۱) وفيه طريقان للمتكلمين والحكماء حاصلها أنه لا بد للموجودات الممكنة من موجد واجب، والمحدثة من محدث قديم لاستحالة الدور والتسلسل، وقد يتوهم الاستغناء عن بطلان الترجيح بدون مرجح فيقال: لا بد من موجود لا يحتاج إلى الغير دفعاً للدور والتسلسل أو عن بطلان الدور والتسلسل فيذكر وجوه .
And in it are two ways: He said: The first chapter on proving Him (the first chapter on proving Him). And in it are two ways for the theologians and philosophers, the conclusion of which is that the contingent existents must have a necessary originator, and the originated beings must have an ancient originator, due to the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress. And one might imagine independence from the invalidity of preponderance without a preferrer, so it is said: There must be an existent that does not need the other, to avoid circularity and infinite regress, or regarding the invalidity of circularity and infinite regress, so he mentions aspects.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage introduces the first chapter on proving the existence of God. It states that there are two ways for the theologians and philosophers. The conclusion is that all possible existents must have a necessary originator, and all originated things must have an ancient originator, because circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل) are impossible. Then it mentions that one might think that the proof can be independent of refuting preponderance without a preferrer, but the correct method is to state that a self-sufficient existent is necessary to avoid circularity and infinite regress. This sets the stage for the enumeration of specific arguments (وجوه).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof of God's existence requires a separate refutation of the principle of preponderance without a preferrer.
Correction: The text shows that the proof can be based directly on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress, without needing to invoke that principle.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the two ways mentioned for proving the Necessary Being?
A) That all existents are possible.
B) That possible existents must have a necessary originator and originated beings an ancient originator.
C) That circularity and infinite regress are possible.
D) That no proof is needed for God's existence.

Key Takeaway
The existence of contingent beings necessitates a Necessary Being because circularity and infinite regress are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every contingent thing in your life—your health, wealth, relationships—depends entirely on a Necessary Being, why do you often act as if these things are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly lived aware of that dependence?
?
The passage says circularity and infinite regress are impossible. In your spiritual journey, have you fallen into a cycle of seeking ultimate meaning in finite things (like success or approval) without ever reaching the Source? How can you break that cycle starting today?
?
The text warns against relying on a flawed principle (preponderance without a preferrer). Are there any weak foundations you have built your faith on? Will you commit to examining the soundness of your beliefs based on this clear proof?

First Argument: Impossibility of Self-Existence for Possibles
Sanusi presents the first argument (wajh) for the existence of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the existence of the Necessary Being: a reductio that shows possibles cannot exist from themselves.

Core Idea
If no Necessary Being exists, possibles must cause themselves—and that is absurd.

This lesson introduces the first of several arguments for God's existence from the Kalam tradition. It is a crisp reductio ad absurdum: if there were no necessary being, then every possible being would have to exist from its own essence. But a possible being, by definition, cannot do that. Hence, a necessary being must exist. Let's examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the structure: 'If not X, then Y, and Y is false.'
2
Understand the definition of 'possible'
The argument hinges on the meaning of 'possible' (mumkin). A possible being is one that does not exist from itself. If it existed from itself, it would be necessary, not possible.
3
Reflect on the reductio logic
Ask yourself: What if there were no necessary being? Would all possibles just disappear? No, the argument assumes they still exist, but then they'd be self-causing, which is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لو لم يكن في الموجودات واجب لزم وجود الممكن من ذاته، وفساده بين.
First: If there were no necessary being among existents, then the existence of the possible from itself would be necessary, and its corruption is clear.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This is the first argument (وجه) for the existence of the Necessary Being. It is a brief reductio ad absurdum. The premise is: if there were no necessary being in existence, then every possible being would have to exist from its own essence (i.e., by itself). But a possible being, by definition, does not have existence from itself; it requires a cause. Therefore, the conclusion that it exists from itself is inherently corrupt (فساده بين). Hence, there must be a necessary being.

Key Terms
واجب
ممكن
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the impossibility of self-existence for possibles without evidence.
Correction: The impossibility is inherent in the definition of 'possible' (mumkin)—it means that which is not necessary from itself. The argument is a tautological reductio.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude if there were no necessary being?
A) All possibles would be necessary.
B) Possibles would exist from themselves, which is impossible.
C) Possibles would cease to exist.
D) The argument proves that all possibles are necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that the existence of possibles necessitates a self-subsisting necessary being, because possibles cannot cause themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a possible being—you exist but are not necessary. When you feel self-sufficient, are you in fact living as if you are self-causing? How will you remind yourself today that your very breath depends on Allah?
?
If your existence is entirely dependent on the Necessary Being, what does that mean for your worries and anxieties? Will you hand over your need for control to the One who truly causes all things?
?
The absurdity of a possible being causing itself is 'clear' (بين). Yet do you sometimes act as if you are the source of your own success, knowledge, or righteousness? What practical step can you take this week to consciously attribute your achievements to Allah's provision?

Second Argument: The Totality of Possibles Needs an Independent Agent
Second argument: from the totality of contingent beings to an external cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the totality of all possible beings cannot be self-sufficient and must depend on an external Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The total set of possibles can't cause itself, so its cause must be outside.

Sanusi presents his second rational argument for the existence of God. He considers the entire collection of all possible things — nothing left out. Since each possible is contingent, the whole collection is contingent too. This contingency demands an agent that is independent, and Sanusi shows why that agent cannot be part of the collection itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once through, then read the translation. Note the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
Sanusi writes in a dense style; each 'because' is a step.
2
Define the totality of possibles
Understand what 'مجموع الممكنات' means: the set of all possible beings, taken as a whole without exception. Why must this set itself be contingent?
3
Follow the elimination argument
The argument eliminates possibilities: the independent agent cannot be the totality itself, nor any part of it. Track each reason given.
4
Conclude the necessary external cause
After elimination, only one possibility remains: the agent is external, and that is the Necessary Being. Ask yourself why each elimination is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : مجموع الممكنات أعني المأخوذ بحيث لا يخرج عنها واحد، لا بد لها لا مكانها من مستقل بالفاعلية، وهو لا يجوز أن يكون نفسها، ولا كل جزء منها، وهو ظاهر، ولا بعض الأجزاء لأنه كونه علة لنفسه ولعلله ، ولأنه يفتقر إلى بعض آخر، فلا يستقل، ولأن كل جزء ففرض فعلته أولى فتعين كونه خارجاً وهو الواجب تعالى .
Second: The totality of possibles, meaning taken such that no one of them is excluded, necessarily requires, due to its contingency, an independent agent. And it is not permissible that it be that totality itself, nor any part of it, and that is evident. Nor some of the parts, because that part would be a cause of itself and of its own cause, and because it would be in need of another, so it is not independent. And because every part, if its causality is assumed, then it would be more appropriate, so it follows that it is external, and that is the Necessary, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الثاني) for the existence of the Necessary Being. It considers the total set of all possible beings (مجموع الممكنات) – the complete collection from which no possible is excluded. This totality, being composed of possibles, is itself contingent and requires an agent that is independent (مستقل بالفاعلية). The argument then shows that this independent agent cannot be the totality itself, nor any part of it: It cannot be the totality because the totality is dependent on its parts. It cannot be any part because a part would then be the cause of itself and of the other parts, which is impossible, and it would be dependent on other parts. Therefore, the independent agent must be external to the totality, and this external agent is the Necessary Being (الواجب تعالى).

Key Terms
مجموع الممكنات
مستقل بالفاعلية
علة
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the universe as a whole could be self-causing or necessary.
Correction: Sanusi shows that both the whole and any part of it are contingent and interdependent, so they cannot be the independent cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why can't an individual part of the totality be the independent agent?
A) Because it would be self-caused
B) Because it would be a cause of itself and its own cause, and would depend on other parts
C) Because the part is necessary
D) Because the part is infinite

Key Takeaway
The totality of all contingent things cannot provide its own cause; the cause must be external, and that external cause is the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument says the independent agent must be external to all created things. Has your heart been seeking ultimate security in people, wealth, or status—all of which are part of the contingent totality? How does this argument call you to redirect your trust solely to the Necessary Being?
?

How does this awareness affect your gratitude and your fear of Him? What would it look like to live each moment with this dependence felt in your heart?

Third Argument: The Totality Needs a Cause
Sanusi presents the third argument for the Necessary Being via the totality of possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that the totality of all possible beings requires a cause that necessitates its existence and renders its non-existence impossible.

Core Idea
The totality of possibles cannot be self-caused; it demands an external necessary cause.

After establishing that individual possible beings are contingent, the author presents a third argument focusing on the totality of all possibles. This argument demonstrates that even the entire set of contingents requires a cause that is not itself contingent. The passage explains why no single possible can be the cause of the whole.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the core claim: the totality of possibles needs a cause that necessitates existence.
Notice the concise structure of the argument.
2
Identify the key claim
The argument states that the totality must have a cause that makes its existence necessary and non-existence impossible.
This cause is external to the totality.
3
Understand the negation
No individual possible being can be that cause because each one is itself in need of another.
This rules out internal causation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Since the cause cannot be any member of the set, it must be something outside the totality, which is the Necessary Being.
This completes the argument for a transcendent cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لا بد لمجموع الممكنات من علة بها يجب وجوده، ويمتنع عدمه، ولا شيء من آحاد الجملة كذلك، لأن كل أحد منها محتاج إلى آخر، فلا بالنظر إليه .
Third: The totality of possibles necessarily has a cause by which its existence is necessary and its non-existence is impossible. And no one of the individuals of the set is like that, because each one of them is in need of another, so not with respect to it.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This is the third argument (الثالث). It states that the totality of all possible beings must have a cause that makes the existence of the totality necessary and its non-existence impossible. However, no single individual member of the totality can serve as that cause, because each individual possible being is contingent and depends on others. Therefore, the cause must be external to the totality, and that cause is the Necessary Being.

Key Terms

وجوب
امتناع عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that one possible being (e.g., the first cause among possibles) could cause the whole set.
Correction: The argument explicitly states that no individual possible is like that because each is in need of another. So even if there is a chain, no single possible can be the cause of the entire set.

Checkpoint

A) It needs a cause that makes its existence necessary and non-existence impossible.
B) It can be caused by one of its own members.
C) It does not need a cause because it is infinite.
D) It is itself necessary.

Key Takeaway
The totality of all possible beings requires a cause that is necessary by itself, and no part of the totality can fulfill that role.

Fourth Argument: The First Cause Must Be Necessary
Sanusi presents a logical argument for the necessity of the first cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the first cause of events must be necessary, not contingent, and how it refutes infinite regress.

Core Idea
The first cause must be necessary, or it would require an external cause or lead to absurdity.

This argument, known as the principle of determination, examines the nature of the first cause. The author presents a dilemma: if the first cause is not necessary, it either depends on something else or entails a contradiction. This concise argument is a cornerstone of kalam proofs for God's self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument progresses through conditional statements.
The passage is dense; take your time to follow each branch.
2
Trace the logical branches
Follow the reasoning: if the originator is not necessary, it either has an external cause (invalidates independence) or does not. If not, consider its non-existence before the event: if impossible, the event always exists (absurd); if possible, existence requires a preponderance without a preferrer (absurd).
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice that the argument concludes the originator must be necessary. Also consider the author's note that the sufficiency of this argument to refute infinite regress is debatable.
This connects to broader kalam discussions on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : مبدأ الحوادث بالاستقلال لو لم يكن واجباً أو مشتملا عليه، فإن كان له علة من خارج بطل الاستقلال، وإلا فإن امتنع قبل وجود الحادث لزم الانقلاب. وإن أمكن لزم الترجيح ولا مرجح وفي استغناء هذه الوجوه عن إبطال التسلسل نظر) .
Fourth: The independent originator of events, if it were not necessary or inclusive of Him, then if it has a cause from outside, independence is invalidated. Otherwise, if its non-existence before the existence of the event is impossible, then change (from non-existence to existence) would be necessary. And if it is possible, then preponderance (of existence over non-existence) would be necessary without a preferrer. And in the sufficiency of these arguments for refuting infinite regress there is consideration.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This fourth argument (الرابع) focuses on the first cause of events (مبدأ الحوادث) that is independent (بالاستقلال). It must be either necessary or contain the necessary. If it has a cause outside itself, then it is not independent. If it does not have an external cause, then consider: if it is impossible for it to be non-existent before the event, then the event would have to exist always (change impossible), which is absurd. If it is possible for it to be non-existent, then its existence requires a preponderance without a preferrer, which is also impossible. Therefore, the first independent cause must be necessary. The passage ends with a note: there is consideration (نظر) regarding whether these arguments are sufficient to refute infinite regress without a separate refutation.

Key Terms
مبدأ الحوادث
استقلال
امتناع
ترجيح
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes the impossibility of an infinite causal regress without proof.
Correction: The text itself notes that the sufficiency of these arguments to refute infinite regress is debatable; the argument does not rely on that assumption.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, if the independent originator has a cause from outside, what happens?
A) It becomes necessary.
B) Its independence is invalidated.
C) It leads to infinite regress.
D) It requires a preferrer.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument proves that the first cause must be necessary by showing that any alternative leads to an absurdity—either dependence on an external cause or a preponderance without a preferrer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself acting as if your existence is self-sufficient—as if you are the independent originator of your own success? What would it look like to truly live as if every moment of your being depends on the Necessary Being?
?
When you consider the countless causes and effects in your daily life—your health, your wealth, your relationships—do you habitually trace them back to the First Cause, or do you stop at secondary causes? How can you remind yourself today that nothing happens without the Necessary's preponderance?
?
If the first cause must be necessary, then everything contingent is utterly dependent. Does your heart feel that dependence, or do you intellectualize it without letting it transform your trust and fear? What would change if you truly believed that your existence at every instant is held by the Necessary?

The Self-Evident Nature of God’s Existence
A footnote argues that God's existence is self-evident and not in need of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view of some scholars that God’s existence is self-evident and innate, and why they consider proof unnecessary.

Core Idea
God’s existence is self-evident; debating it indicates a defect in faith.

This footnote presents a contrasting perspective to the main text’s philosophical proofs. Some scholars argue that God’s existence is so obvious that it should not be discussed in religious circles. They support their view with the Qur’an and a story about a gnostic. This lesson explores that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through directly.
Pay attention to the tone: this is a footnote presenting a minority view.
2
Identify the main claim
The scholars claim that God's existence is 'بدهي' (self-evident) and 'فطري' (innate), and that discussing it is inappropriate for believers.
Note the strong language: discussing it implies a defect in faith.
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The verse (Quran 29:61) shows that even polytheists acknowledge God as Creator. This supports the claim that belief in God is innate.
4
Reflect on the anecdote
The story of the gnostic illustrates that direct awareness of God is superior to intellectual proof. The intellect is described as incapable.
This contrasts with the main text's use of rational proofs.
5
Consider the broader implication
Ask yourself: Is this perspective compatible with the tradition of kalam? How does it relate to the purpose of Islamic theology?
The footnote is a minority opinion; the main text proceeds with proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرى بعض العلماء : أن وجود الله : إنما هو أمر بدهي لا ينبغي أن يتحدث فيها المؤمنون نفياً أو إثباتاً ولا سلباً ولا إيجاباً، ويرون أن وجود الله من القضايا المسلمة التي لا توضع في الأوساط الدينية موضع البحث، لأنها فطرية. وأن كل شخص يحاول وضعها موضع البحث إنما هو شخص في إيمانه دخل وفي دينه انحراف فما خفى الله قط حتى يحتاج إلى أن يثبته البشر تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً ومن المعروف أن الدين الإسلامي لم يجىء لإثبات وجود الله وإنما جاء لتوحيد الله . والقرآن يتحدث عن بداهة وجود الله حتى عند ذوي العقائد المنحرفة يقول سبحانه: ﴿ ولئن سألتهم من خلق السموات والأرض ليقولن الله. إنهم يقولون إن الخالق هو الله مع أنهم مشركون أو منحرفون بوجه من الوجوه في إيمانهم بالله .. سئل أحد العارفين عن الدليل على الله . فقال : الله + فقيل له فما بالعقل ..؟ فقال : العقل عاجز لا يدل إلا على عاجز مثله . راجع الإسلام والعقل ص ۱۵۰ والطريق إلى الله ص ٨ .
Some scholars hold that the existence of God is a self-evident matter; believers should not speak about it neither by negation nor affirmation, neither by denial nor affirmation. They hold that the existence of God is a conceded proposition that should not be placed as a subject of research in religious circles, because it is innate. And that every person who tries to place it as a subject of research is a person having defect in his faith and deviation in his religion. God has never been hidden such that humans need to prove Him; exalted is God above that greatly. And it is known that the Islamic religion did not come to prove the existence of God, but rather came to proclaim the Oneness of God. And the Qur'an speaks about the self-evident nature of God's existence even among those with deviant beliefs. He, glorified, says: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah.' They say that the Creator is Allah even though they are polytheists or deviant in some aspects of their faith in God. A certain gnostic was asked about the proof of God. He said: 'God.' Then it was said to him: 'What about the intellect?' He said: 'The intellect is incapable; it only points to something incapable like itself.' Reference: Islam and Reason p. 150 and The Path to God p. 8.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a footnote that presents the view of some scholars who argue that God's existence is self-evident (بدهي) and innate (فطري). They believe that discussing proofs for God's existence is inappropriate for believers, as it implies doubt. They cite the Qur'anic verse where even polytheists acknowledge God as Creator, and a story about a gnostic who said 'God' when asked for proof, dismissing the intellect as incapable. This perspective contrasts with the main text's approach of providing philosophical proofs.

The Philosophers' Proof for the Necessary Being
Definition of divine matters and the philosophers' proof for the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subject matter of the fifth chapter on divine matters and the logical structure of the philosophers' proof for the existence of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Any existing being must lead rationally to a Necessary Cause.

The scholar begins by outlining the fifth chapter's scope, which covers all aspects of God's essence, attributes, and actions. He then focuses on the first section, which presents the philosophers' core argument for God's existence: starting from any existing thing, we trace its cause until we reach a Necessary Being, as circularity and infinite regress are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scope of divine matters
Read the definition of the fifth chapter: it deals with God's essence, transcendence, attributes, permissibility, actions, and names. This sets the context for the argument.
2
Learn the six sections of the chapter
The chapter is divided into six sections. The first section is dedicated to proving the existence of the Necessary Being and examining His essence. The following steps focus on this first section.
3
Follow the philosophers' argument step by step
The argument begins with an undeniable existent. If it is necessary, the goal is reached. If it is possible, it needs a cause. The discussion then shifts to that cause, leading to a chain.
Pay attention to the logical necessity: either the chain ends in a Necessary Being or it falls into absurdity.
4
Recognize the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress
The argument concludes that circularity (دور) and infinite regress (تسلسل) are impossible, so the chain must terminate in a Necessary Being. This is the required conclusion.
This is a foundational argument in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : المقصد الخامس في الإلهيات أي المباحث المتعلقة بذات الله تعالى، وتنزيهاته وصفاته وما يجوز عليه وما لا يجوز وأفعاله وأسمائه. فلذا جعل المقصد ستة فصول. يشتمل الأول منها على تقرير الأدلة على وجود الواجب، وعلى تحقيق أن ذاته هل تخالف سائر الذوات . . ؟ وطريق إثبات الواجب عند الحكماء. انه لا شك في وجود موجود، فإن كان واجباً فهو المرام، وإن كان ممكناً فلا بد له من علة بها يترجح وجوده، وينقل الكلام إليه . فإذن يلزم الدور أو التسلسل وهو محال، أو ينتهي إلى واجب وهو المطلوب.
His statement: The fifth chapter on divine matters, meaning the investigations related to the essence of God Most High, His transcendences, His attributes, what is permissible for Him and what is not, His actions and His names. Therefore, he made the chapter contain six sections. The first of them includes the establishment of the proofs for the existence of the Necessary, and the verification of whether His essence is different from other essences. And the way of proving the Necessary according to the philosophers: that there is no doubt in the existence of an existent. If it is necessary, then that is the goal. And if it is possible, then it must have a cause by which its existence is preponderated, and the discourse is transferred to that cause. So then, either circularity or infinite regress follows, which is impossible, or it ends at a Necessary Being, which is what is sought.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage comes from the commentary (قوله) on the original text. It first defines the subject matter of the fifth chapter (المقصد الخامس) as 'divine matters' (الإلهيات), covering God's essence, transcendence, attributes, permissibility, actions, and names. It notes that the chapter is divided into six sections, the first of which is dedicated to proving the existence of the Necessary Being and investigating whether His essence differs from other essences. Then it presents the way (طريق) of the philosophers (الحكماء) to prove the Necessary Being. The argument begins with an undeniable fact: there is an existent (وجود موجود). If that existent is necessary (واجب), then the aim is achieved. If it is possible (ممكن), then it requires a cause that preponderates its existence. The discussion then shifts to that cause. This leads to either circularity (دور) or infinite regress (تسلسل), both impossible, so it must ultimately reach a Necessary Being. This is the required conclusion.

Key Terms
الإلهيات
واجب
ممكن
دور
تسلسل
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' method assumes the existence of a contingent being to start the argument.
Correction: The argument actually starts from any existing being, whether necessary or possible, and then reasons about its nature.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove the existence of a Necessary Being by showing that any existing entity must ultimately depend on a cause that is necessary, as circularity and infinite regress are rationally impossible.

The Third and Fourth Proofs for an External Necessary Cause
Presents two proofs showing the necessity of an external cause and notes their reliance on refuting infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two logical proofs: the aggregate of possible beings and the origin of temporal events both require an external necessary cause, and why these proofs depend on refuting infinite regress.

Core Idea
Two arguments show that the totality of contingent things needs a cause outside itself.

Having established that no part of a causal chain can be self-sufficient, the author now presents two further arguments. The third proof focuses on the total sum of all possible beings, while the fourth examines the independent origin of temporal events. Both arguments lead to the conclusion that an external Necessary Being must exist, though they may not be fully independent of refuting infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the passage begins with a conclusion from a previous argument, followed by 'الثالث' (third) and 'الرابع' (fourth) proofs. Identify the core claim of each proof.
The word 'وجوب' appears between the proofs; it may be a header.
2
Understand the third proof: aggregate of possibles
The third proof argues that the sum of all possible beings (al-mumkināt) cannot be self-sufficient because each individual within it depends on another. Therefore, the aggregate needs an external cause to make its existence necessary.
The proof relies on the principle that if every part is contingent, the whole cannot be necessary.
3
Understand the fourth proof: origin of temporal events
The fourth proof examines the independent origin of temporal events. If this origin is not necessary or does not contain the necessary, then either it has an external cause (undermining independence) or it does not. In the latter case, two absurdities follow: either conversion (al-inqilāb) or preponderance without a preponderator (al-tarjīḥ lā murjīḥ). Thus, the origin must be necessary.
The conclusion is that the origin must itself be necessary or contain the necessary, pointing to an external Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر، ولا بعض الأجزاء لأنه كونه علة لنفسه ولعلله ، ولأنه يفتقر إلى بعض آخر، فلا يستقل، ولأن كل جزء ففرض فعلته أولى فتعين كونه خارجاً وهو الواجب تعالى . الثالث : لا بد لمجموع الممكنات من علة بها يجب وجوده، ويمتنع عدمه، ولا شيء من آحاد الجملة كذلك، لأن كل أحد منها محتاج إلى آخر، فلا بالنظر إليه . وجوب الرابع : مبدأ الحوادث بالاستقلال لو لم يكن واجباً أو مشتملا عليه، فإن كان له علة من خارج بطل الاستقلال، وإلا فإن امتنع قبل وجود الحادث لزم الانقلاب. وإن أمكن لزم الترجيح ولا مرجح وفي استغناء هذه الوجوه عن إبطال التسلسل نظر) .
It is evident, nor any part of the parts, because it would be a cause of itself and of its own causes, and because it would need another part, so it would not be independent, and because each part, if its causality is assumed, is prior, so it is determined that it be external, and He is the Necessary (al-wājib), exalted. Third: The sum of possible things (al-mumkināt) must have a cause by which its existence is necessary and its non-existence impossible, and none of the individuals of the totality is like that, because each one of them needs another, so not with respect to it. Obligation/necessity (wujūb). Fourth: The principle of temporal events (al-ḥawādith) independently, if it were not necessary or containing the necessary, then if it has a cause from outside independence is invalidated, and if not, then if non-existence is impossible before the existence of the event, conversion (al-inqilāb) follows. And if possible, preponderance (al-tarjīḥ) follows without a preponderator (lā murjīḥ). And concerning the independence of these proofs from invalidating infinite regress (ibṭāl al-tasalsul), there is consideration (naẓar).
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct proofs (the third and fourth) for the necessity of an external cause for the cosmos. It begins by concluding from a previous argument that no part of the chain of causes can be a cause of itself or of its own causes, hence the cause must be external and is the Necessary Being. Then the third proof is stated: the aggregate of all possible beings requires a cause that makes its existence necessary; no individual within that aggregate is self-sufficient because each depends on another. A stray word 'وجوب' appears, possibly a scribal note. The fourth proof concerns the independent origin of temporal events: if this origin is not necessary or does not contain the necessary, then either it has an external cause (undermining independence) or it does not. If it does not and non-existence prior to the event is impossible, that leads to conversion (a logical impossibility); if non-existence is possible, then preponderance without a preponderator is required, which is impossible. The passage ends by noting that these proofs may not dispense with refuting infinite regress; there is reservation about their independence from that refutation.

Key Terms
الممكن
الواجب
العلة
التسلسل
الترجيح بلا مرجح
الانقلاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the sum of possible beings could cause itself, but each part is contingent and needs another.
Correction: The third proof explicitly states that no individual in the aggregate is self-sufficient because each depends on another. Therefore, the aggregate cannot be necessary by itself and requires an external cause.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why does the aggregate of possible beings require an external cause?
A) Because each individual in the aggregate is necessary.
B) Because no individual in the aggregate is self-sufficient; each depends on another.
C) Because the aggregate has no parts.
D) Because the aggregate is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The totality of possible beings and the origin of temporal events both point to an external, necessary cause, and these arguments rely on rejecting infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are one of those 'individuals of the totality' that the third proof describes as needing another. In what area of your life right now are you trying to be self-sufficient, as if you could cause your own happiness or purpose without God? What would it look like to truly depend on Him as the cause of your existence in that area today? Write down one specific action you will take to acknowledge that dependence.
?
The fourth proof argues that the origin of temporal events cannot be explained by chance or internal necessity. Think of a significant event in your life—good or bad—that you tend to attribute to luck or mere coincidence. How does this argument challenge you to see that event as caused by the only Necessary Being who acts without needing a preponderator? Commit to thanking Him for that event today.
?
The passage ends by questioning whether these proofs are independent from refuting infinite regress. If your belief in God relies on such arguments, have you personally confronted the logical necessity of an ultimate cause, or do you just accept it without thought? What step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction in the Necessary Being by studying these proofs deeply?

The Philosophers' Proof for the Necessary Being
Presents the philosophers' argument for the Necessary Being based on impossibility of circularity or regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' proof for the Necessary Being, based on the impossibility of circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Every possible existent needs a cause; the chain must end with a Necessary Being.

The philosophers approach the existence of God by starting with the undeniable fact that something exists. They then argue that if that something is not self-necessary, it must have a cause, and this reasoning cannot go on forever without reaching a self-sufficient foundation. This lesson walks you through that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the argument.
2
Identify the starting point
The argument begins with 'there is no doubt about the existence of an existent.' This is the foundation: something exists.
3
Understand the two possibilities
The existent is either necessary (self-sufficient) or possible (contingent). If necessary, the goal is reached. If possible, it needs a cause to preponderate its existence.
Possible means it could equally exist or not exist.
4
Follow the causal chain
The cause itself is examined. If it is also possible, the same question arises, leading to either circularity or infinite regress, both impossible.
Circularity is A caused by B and B caused by A; regress is an endless chain.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Since circularity and infinite regress are impossible, the chain must terminate in a Necessary Being, which is the desired conclusion.
This Necessary Being is what we understand as God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطريق إثبات الواجب عند الحكماء. انه لا شك في وجود موجود، فإن كان واجباً فهو المرام، وإن كان ممكناً فلا بد له من علة بها يترجح وجوده، وينقل الكلام إليه . فإذن يلزم الدور أو التسلسل وهو محال، أو ينتهي إلى واجب وهو المطلوب.
And the way of proving the Necessary (al-wājib) according to the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'). There is no doubt about the existence of an existent. If it is necessary, then it is the sought; and if it is possible (mumkin), then it must have a cause by which its existence is preponderated, and the discourse is transferred to it. Then circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul) follows, which is impossible, or it ends at a Necessary Being, which is the desired.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the existence of the Necessary Being. It begins by affirming that there is no doubt about the existence of some existent. Two possibilities are considered: either this existent is necessary (self-sufficient) or it is possible (contingent). If it is necessary, the goal is reached. If it is possible, it must have a cause that preponderates its existence over non-existence. The argument then examines the cause: if that cause is possible, the same question arises, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both of which are deemed impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in a Necessary Being, which is the conclusion sought.

Key Terms
الواجب
الممكن
الحكماء
الدور
التسلسل
الترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that every possible must have a cause, which is the principle of sufficient reason.
Correction: The argument does not assume this principle arbitrarily; it is a rational necessity that contingent beings require a cause to explain their existence. This is a foundational premise in the proof.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' proof, what happens if the existent under consideration is possible (mumkin)?
A) It is immediately the Necessary Being.
B) It must have a preponderating cause, leading to either circularity/infinite regress or to a Necessary Being.
C) It cannot exist because possibility requires actualization.
D) It must be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that since infinite regress and circularity are impossible, the chain of causes must end in a self-necessary being, which is what we call God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the proof shows that a Necessary Being must exist, why do you sometimes live as if He has no claim over your daily choices? Will you let this truth shape your next decision?
?
Are you willing to follow this logical chain to its end, or do you prefer to remain in comfortable uncertainty? What would change if you truly accepted that you depend on Him at every moment?
?
This argument demands an answer: either you are self-sufficient (impossible) or you need a Cause. Does your life reflect that dependence in prayer, gratitude, and humility? If not, what would it look like to start?

The Theologians' Proof from Temporality of the World
Al-Sanusi presents the theologians' proof from temporality to the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' proof for the existence of the Necessary Being based on the temporality of the world and the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Every temporal event needs a maker; the chain must end with a beginningless necessary being.

The theologians (mutakallimūn) developed a distinctive proof for God's existence starting from the evident temporality of the world. In this passage, al-Sanusi presents their core argument: since temporal events exist and each requires a maker, the sequence of causes cannot go on infinitely or in a circle—it must terminate in an eternal, self-sufficient being. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument moves from the fact of temporal events to the necessity of a first cause.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument relies on two premises: every temporal event has a maker, and an infinite or circular chain of causes is impossible. Find where these are stated.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that there must be an eternal being that requires no cause—the Necessary Being. Note how the theologians connect proving the eternal to proving the Necessary.
The final sentence highlights a key difference from the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند المتكلمين أنه قد ثبت حدوث العالم. إذ لا شك في وجود حادث، وكل حادث فيها لضرورة له محدث، فإما أن يدور أو يتسلسل وهو محال، وإما أن ينتهي إلى قديم لا يفتقر الى سبب أصلاً وهو المراد بالواجب، فكلا الطريقين مبني على امتناع وجود الممكن أو الحادث بلا موجد، وعلى استحالة الدور والتسلسل. والمتكلمون لما لم يقولوا بقدم شيء من الممكنات، كان إثبات القديم إثباتاً للواجب
And according to the theologians (al-mutakallimūn), it has been established that the world is originated (ḥudūth al-‘ālam). For there is no doubt about the existence of a temporal event (ḥādith), and every temporal event necessarily has a maker (muḥdith). Then either circularity or infinite regress follows, which is impossible, or it ends at something eternal (qadīm) that does not need any cause at all, which is what is meant by the Necessary (al-wājib). So both paths are based on the impossibility of the existence of a possible (mumkin) or a temporal event without an originate (mūjid), and on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress. And since the theologians do not affirm the eternity of any possible thing, proving the eternal (al-qadīm) is proving the Necessary.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage gives the theologians' (mutakallimūn) argument for the Necessary Being. It begins by asserting that the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world is established: there is no doubt that temporal events exist. Every temporal event must have an origination (muḥdith). The argument then proceeds: either the chain of origination leads to circularity or infinite regress (both impossible) or it ends at an eternal being (qadīm) that does not require any cause, which is the Necessary Being. The passage notes that both the philosophers' and theologians' proofs rely on the principle that a possible or temporal being cannot exist without an efficient cause, and on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress. A distinctive point is added: because theologians do not affirm the eternity of any possible being (as the philosophers do with celestial spheres), for them proving an eternal being is directly proving the Necessary Being.

Key Terms
المتكلمون
الحدوث
الحادث
المحدث
القديم
الواجب
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The theologians' proof relies on the impossibility of an infinite regress of temporal events.
Correction: The text specifically says the impossibility is of circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) of efficient causes, not merely temporal events.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians, why does the chain of makers have to end?
A) Because temporal events are finite in number.
B) Because circularity and infinite regress are impossible.
C) Because the world is eternal.
D) Because the theologians say so.

Key Takeaway
The theologians prove the Necessary Being by showing that every temporal event requires a maker, and the chain of makers must end with a beginningless, self-sufficient being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states there is 'no doubt' about the existence of temporal events. Do you actually live with that certainty, or do doubts about the universe's origin sometimes creep in? What would it take for you to remove those doubts permanently?
?
If every temporal event needs a maker, then your every action, your very existence, is contingent. Does that thought make you humble before the Self-Sufficient Creator, or do you still feel self-sufficient in your daily routines?
?
The theologians prove the Necessary Being through reason alone. Have you ever used this proof to strengthen your own faith when it wavers, or do you rely only on emotional experiences? Commit to memorizing this argument and using it.

Refuting the Philosophers' Objection on Beginningless Events
Sanusi refutes the philosophers' objection about beginningless events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' objection of beginningless temporal sequences does not undermine the proofs for the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Infinite regression of events is not the same as infinite regression of efficient causes.

Philosophers have argued that a beginningless succession of temporal events, like celestial motions, could exist without a first cause. Sanusi refutes this by distinguishing between caused effects and efficient causes. This lesson examines his two-fold response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the two 'ammā...fa...' structures.
2
Identify the two refutations
The passage gives two reasons why the objection fails. Find them and restate them in your own words.
The first refers to an earlier discussion; the second makes a key distinction.
3
Understand the distinction: caused effects vs. efficient causes
Focus on the second point: beginningless successions apply only to caused effects, not to efficient causes that must coexist with their effects. Why does this matter?
Efficient causes produce existence itself, not just a later state.
4
Connect to the broader argument for the Necessary Being
Ask yourself why this distinction defends the proofs. If efficient causes cannot form an infinite regress, then a First Cause is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يرد عليهما ما جوزه الحكماء من تعاقب الحوادث من غير بداية، كالحركات والأوضاع الفلكية أما أولاً: فلما مر في مسألة حدوث العالم. وأما ثانياً: فلأن ذلك إنما هو في المعلات دون العلل الموجدة التي لا بد من وجودها مع وجود المعلول.
And there does not apply to them (the two proofs) what the philosophers permitted of the succession of temporal events without a beginning, like celestial motions and positions. As for first: because of what has passed in the issue of the temporality of the world. And as for second: because that (succession) is only in caused effects (ma‘lūlāt) not in efficient causes (al-‘ilal al-mūjida) which must exist together with the existence of the effect.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection that philosophers might raise against both the philosophers' and theologians' proofs. The objection is that beginningless successions of temporal events, such as celestial motions and positions, could be possible, effectively avoiding the need for a first cause. The response contains two points. First, it refers back to the earlier discussion on the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-‘ālam) which already refuted this. Second, it clarifies that such beginningless successions concern caused effects (ma‘lūlāt) — i.e., the relations between events that are effects of other events — but not efficient causes (al-‘ilal al-mūjida) that produce existence ex nihilo. Efficient causes must coexist with their effects, so an infinite regress of efficient causes is impossible, whereas an infinite regress of effects (like motions) is not at issue.

Key Terms
الحوادث
المعلات
العلل الموجدة
الحدوث
البداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite regress of events (like motions) disproves the need for a first cause.
Correction: Such a regress is only of caused effects, not of efficient causes. Efficient causes must coexist with their effects, so an infinite chain of efficient causes is impossible, requiring a First Cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument not apply to the philosophers' objection about beginningless celestial motions?
A) Because celestial motions are not real.
B) Because such successions are about caused effects, not efficient causes that must coexist with their effects.
C) Because the world is not temporal.
D) Because the objection was already accepted.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it confuses causal chains of effects with the chain of efficient causes that require simultaneous existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the chain of events that led to your existence, do you tend to stop at natural causes (parents, biology) or do you seek the efficient Cause that is simultaneously present with every moment? How would your awareness of Allah change if you constantly saw Him as the immediate efficient Cause of your existence?
?
The philosophers argued that an infinite chain of events could explain the universe without God. What does this tell you about your own heart's tendency to rely on secondary causes instead of the First Cause? Are there areas in your life where you act as if the chain of effects is self-sufficient?
?
If efficient causes must exist at the same time as their effects, then Allah is not just a distant First Cause but actively sustaining you now. Does your daily life reflect this belief? What would it look like to live as if Allah is the immediate cause of every breath you take?

Critiquing Flawed Proofs for the Necessary Being
Criticizes two misguided proofs for the Necessary Being that avoid key principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why merely positing a self-sufficient being does not prove necessity unless the principle of preponderance is accepted, and see an example of an attempted proof that avoids refuting circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Independence from others does not equal necessity without rejecting preponderance without a preponderator.

Sanusi critiques two attempts to prove the Necessary Being without relying on the key principle that every contingent requires a preponderating cause. The first attempt claims that positing an independent being suffices to avoid circularity. The second claims a proof that bypasses even refuting circularity or infinite regress. Let's examine these arguments and Sanusi's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: the author presents two misguided attempts.
The passage begins with 'وتوهم بعضهم...' introducing the first attempt.
2
Identify the first attempted proof and its flaw
Find the argument that claims independence suffices to prove necessity. Then see Sanusi's refutation: independence does not imply necessity unless preponderance without a preponderator is false.
3
Understand why independence alone is insufficient
Reflect on the idea that a self-sufficient being could still be contingent without a cause for its existence or non-existence. This shows the need for the principle of preponderance.
4
Examine the second attempted proof
The second attempt claims a proof that does not require refuting circularity or infinite regress. One reason is given: the temporality of celestial bodies due to setting (ufūl). Note that the passage ends without a full critique.
There may be a textual corruption: 'الأفل' should likely be 'الأول' (the first).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتوهم بعضهم أنه يمكن الاستدلال على وجود الواجب بحيث لا يتوقف على امتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح . بأن يقال : لا بد أن يكون في الموجودات موجود لا يفتقر الى الغير، دفعاً للدور، ولا معين للواجب سوى هذا وفيه نظر لأن مجرد الاستغناء عن الغير لا يقتضي الوجوب وامتناع العدم إلا على تقدير بطلان الترجيح بلا مرجح، وإلا لجاز أن يكون المستغني عن الغير يوجد تارة ويعدم أخرى من غير أن يكون ذلك الوجود والعدم لذاته ولا لغيره بل بمجرد الاتفاق، ومنهم من توهم صحة الاستدلال بحيث لا يفتقر الى إبطال الدور أو التسلسل وذلك لوجوه : الأفل حادث لحدوث عارضه الدال على حدوثه أعني الأفول. وما هو حادث فله محدث غيره فلا يكون مبدأ الجميع الحوادث فلا يكون صانعاً للعالم.
And some mistakenly thought that one can prove the existence of the Necessary (al-wājib) in a way that does not depend on rejecting preponderance without a preponderator (imtinā‘ al-tarjīḥ bi-lā murjīḥ). By saying: there must be among existents an existent that does not need another, to avoid circularity, and no meaning for the Necessary other than this. But there is consideration in this, because mere independence from another does not necessitate necessity and impossibility of non-existence except on the assumption of the falsity of preponderance without a preponderator. Otherwise, it would be possible for the one independent of another to exist sometimes and not exist at other times without that existence and non-existence being from itself or from another, but merely by chance. And among them are those who mistakenly thought the proof valid in a way that does not require refuting circularity or infinite regress, through reasons: The first (al-awwal) is temporal because of the temporality of its accident indicating its temporality, namely setting (al-ufūl). And whatever is temporal has an originator other than it, so it cannot be the principle of all temporal events, so it cannot be the maker of the world.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage critiques two misguided attempts to prove the Necessary Being. The first attempt claims one can argue simply that there must be an existent independent of all others to avoid circularity, without needing to reject the possibility of preponderance without a preponderator (the principle that any contingent requires a preponderating cause). The author refutes this: mere independence does not entail necessity; without rejecting preponderance without a preponderator, such an independent being could come in and out of existence by mere chance. The second attempt claims a proof that does not even need to refute circularity or infinite regress. The author gives one such attempted reason (likely the first of several reasons, but only one is provided in the text): the argument from the temporality of celestial bodies, exemplified by the setting (ufūl) of a star. This argument states that the setting of a celestial body is a temporal accident, showing the body is temporal, so it cannot be the eternal originator of all events. The passage is cut off after presenting this first reason; more reasons were presumably intended.

Key Terms
الترجيح بلا مرجح
الاستغناء عن الغير
الواجب
الدور
التسلسل
الأفول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A being independent of others is automatically necessary.
Correction: The text shows that independence does not guarantee necessity; without the principle of preponderance, such a being could still be contingent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the first attempted proof that relies only on positing a being independent of others?
A) Because a being independent of others cannot exist.
B) Because independence does not imply necessity unless preponderance without a preponderator is false.
C) Because circularity is actually possible.
D) Because the necessary being must be dependent.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of the Necessary Being cannot be proven merely by independence; the principle of preponderance is indispensable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been satisfied with a shallow proof for Allah's existence—something that felt convenient but didn't hold up under scrutiny? How does this passage force you to examine the rigor of your own conviction? What will you do to ensure your faith is built on solid grounds, not flawed shortcuts?
?
The principle of preponderance without a preponderator means nothing happens by chance. In what areas of your life do you act as if things 'just happen'—your health, your provision, your successes? How does this lesson call you to see every detail as dependent on Allah? What would change if you truly lived by that?
?
Sanusi warns that a being independent of others could still be contingent without the principle of preponderance. Are there 'independent' aspects of your life—your skills, your status—that you assume make you self-sufficient? How does this passage expose that illusion? What step will you take today to acknowledge your complete need for Allah?
