Persuasive and Widely Accepted Proofs
Introduction to persuasive and widely accepted proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the proof from bodies is presented as persuasive (إقناعي) and based on well-known premises (مشهورات) that no rational person disputes.

Core Idea
The proof from bodies is persuasive, not just demonstrative, and universally acknowledged.

This lesson introduces the second investigation of the text. The author has already given demonstrative arguments, but now he turns to persuasive aspects that are widely accepted among people. We will see how this proof is grounded in common experience and Qur'anic guidance.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shift from demonstrative to persuasive
Notice that the author distinguishes between براهين (demonstrative proofs) and وجوه إقناعية (persuasive aspects). This shows a pedagogical approach: after rigorous proofs, simpler arguments are given.
Persuasive arguments are meant for a general audience.
2
Identify the claim of universal acceptance
The passage states that no one of consideration disagrees with this proof. Reflect on why it is considered among المشهورات (well-known premises).
3
Connect to the Quranic endorsement
The author later mentions that the Quran alludes to this proof in over eighty places. This lesson sets the stage for that connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : قد سبقت الدلالة على وجود الصانع بالبراهين، وها هنا يشير إلى وجوه إقناعية، وإلى كونه من المشهورات التي لم يخالف فيها أحد ممن يعتد به بذل المجهود في إثبات ما هو أعظم المطالب العالية بيان ذلك أنه لا يشك أحد في وجود عالم الأجسام من الأفلاك والكواكب، والعناصر والمركبات المعدنية والنباتية والحيوانية وفي اختلاف صفات لها وأحوال، وقد صح الاستدلال بذواتها وصفاتها لإمكانها وحدوثها على وجود صانع قديم قادر حكيم فتأتي في أربعة طرق هي الشائعة فيما بين الجمهور وأشير إليها في أكثر من ثمانين موضعاً من كتاب الله تعالى. كقوله تعالى: (إن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار والفلك التي تجري في البحر بما ينفع الناس، وما أنزل الله من السماء من ماء فأحيا به الأرض بعد موتها وبث فيها من كل دابة، وتصريف الرياح والسحاب المسخر بين السماء والأرض لآيات لقوم يعقلون) وكقوله تعالى: (ومن آياته الليل والنهار والشمس والقمر والنجوم مسخرات بأمره). وكقوله تعالى: (سنريهم آياتنا في الآفاق وفي أنفسهم) وكقوله تعالى: (ألم نخلقكم من ماء مهين) وكقوله تعالى: (ومن آياته خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف ألسنتكم وألوانكم) إلى غير ذلك من مواضع الإرشاد إلى الاستدلال بالعالم الأعلى من الأفلاك والكواكب وحركاتها، وأوضاعها، والأحوال المتعلقة بها، وبالعالم الأسفل من طبقات العناصر، ومراتب امتزاجاتها، والآثار العلوية والسفلية وأحوال المعادن والنبات والحيوانات سيما الإنسان وما أودع بدنه مما يشهد به علم التشريح وروحه بما
The Second Investigation: The proof of the existence of the Maker has already been given by demonstrative arguments, and here he points to persuasive aspects, and to its being among well-known premises that no one of consideration has disagreed with, exerting effort in establishing that which is the greatest of lofty demands. The explanation of that is that no one doubts the existence of the world of bodies—the celestial spheres, planets, elements, and compounds—mineral, plant, and animal—and in the diversity of their attributes and states. And inference has been validated from their essences and attributes, due to their contingency and temporal origination, to the existence of an Eternal, Powerful, Wise Maker. So it comes in four ways that are common among the public, and reference is made to them in more than eighty places in the Book of God Almighty. Such as His saying: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of the night and the day and the ships which sail through the sea with that which benefits people, and what Allah has sent down from the heavens of rain, giving life thereby to the earth after its lifelessness and dispersing therein every [kind of] moving creature, and [His] directing of the winds and the clouds controlled between the heaven and the earth are signs for a people who use reason.' And His saying: 'And of His signs are the night and day and the sun and moon and the stars, subject by His command.' And His saying: 'We will show them Our signs in the horizons and within themselves.' And His saying: 'Did We not create you from a despicable fluid?' And His saying: 'And of His signs is the creation of the heavens and the earth and the diversity of your languages and your colors.' And so on to other places of guidance to inference from the higher world of the celestial spheres and planets and their movements, positions, and the states pertaining to them, and from the lower world of the layers of the elements, the degrees of their mixtures, and the upper and lower effects, and the states of minerals, plants, and animals, especially man and what is deposited in his body as attested to by the science of anatomy and his spirit by what
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the second investigation of the book. The commentator begins by noting that previous proofs for the existence of the Maker were demonstrative (براهين), but here the author points to persuasive arguments (وجوه إقناعية) and to the fact that this proof is among widely accepted premises (المشهورات) that no rational person disputes. He then explains that no one doubts the existence of the physical world—celestial spheres, planets, elements, minerals, plants, animals—and their diverse attributes and states. From their essences and attributes, due to their contingency (إمكان) and temporal origination (حدوث), a valid inference is made to an Eternal, Powerful, Wise Maker. The commentator mentions that this inference is commonly presented in four ways (أربعة طرق) among the public, and the Qur'an alludes to these ways in over eighty places. He then cites several Qur'anic verses as examples of such guidance: Surah al-Baqarah 2:164, Surah al-A'raf 7:54, Surah Fussilat 41:53, Surah al-Mursalat 77:20, and Surah al-Rum 30:22. These verses point to signs in the heavens and earth, the alternation of night and day, the ships, rain, life after death, the creation of man, and the diversity of languages and colors. The passage concludes by noting that all these are references to inference from the higher world (العالم الأعلى) of celestial bodies and their motions, and from the lower world (العالم الأسفل) of elemental layers, mixtures, effects, and especially the human body and spirit as studied in anatomy. The overall aim is to show that this proof is universally accessible and Qur'anically endorsed.

Key Terms
إقناعيات
مشهورات
براهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of arguments does the author point to in this investigation?
A) Demonstrative (براهين)
B) Persuasive (إقناعية) and well-known premises (مشهورات)
C) Only philosophical proofs
D) Analogical arguments

Key Takeaway
The proof from the world of bodies is presented as persuasive and universally accepted, not merely philosophical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that no one of consideration doubts the existence of the physical world. Do you ever find yourself doubting the obvious signs of creation around you? What keeps you from fully acknowledging the Maker? Commit right now to reflect on one natural object today and see it as a sign.
?
The passage says persuasive proofs are for everyone, not just philosophers. Have you been hiding behind intellectual sophistication to avoid the simple truth? What would it mean for you to accept the Maker based on the undeniable existence of bodies?
?
The author mentions 'exerting effort in establishing the greatest of lofty demands.' Are you exerting any effort to confirm the existence of Allah in your daily life, or do you take it for granted? What one action will you take today to make this truth more real to you?

Inference from Contingency and Origination
Logical inference from contingency and origination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency (إمكان) and temporal origination (حدوث) of bodies lead to the inference of an Eternal, Powerful, Wise Maker.

Core Idea
Contingency and temporality of bodies necessitate a Maker.

This lesson focuses on the logical core of the proof: from the essences and attributes of bodies, due to their contingency and temporal origination, we infer a Maker who is Eternal, Powerful, and Wise. The author also mentions that this inference comes in four common ways.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the key concepts: imkan and huduth
The passage says الاستدلال بذواتها وصفاتها لإمكانها وحدوثها. Understand that 'imkan' means possibility (contingency) and 'huduth' means temporal origination (being created in time).
These are standard kalam terms.
2
Identify the attributes of the Maker inferred
The inference leads to a صانع قديم قادر حكيم (Eternal, Powerful, Wise Maker). Note that these attributes are directly derived from the nature of bodies.
3
Recognize the four common ways
The author says this inference comes in four ways (أربعة طرق) that are common among the public. Later lessons may detail these, but here we note their existence.
The four ways are not enumerated in this passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think contingency and temporal origination are contradictory or unrelated.
Correction: Contingency (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth) are complementary: contingency indicates dependence, and temporal origination indicates a beginning. Together they point to a Creator.

What are the two properties of bodies that form the basis of the inference?
A) Substance and accident
B) Contingency (إمكان) and temporal origination (حدوث)
C) Eternity and power
D) Matter and form

Key Takeaway
The inference from bodies to a Maker relies on their contingency and temporal origination, leading to a Maker who is Eternal, Powerful, and Wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is contingent and originated, meaning you could have not existed and you came into being after not being, how does that change the way you view your life and your purpose? What does it require of you right now?
?
The passage concludes that the Maker is not only Powerful but also Wise. In what area of your life have you been acting as if Allah is not wise in His decree? Will you submit to that wisdom today, even if you don't understand it?
?
Consider that this inference is validated from your own attributes and essence. Look at yourself: are you not dependent on forces outside yourself for every breath? What does that constant dependence demand from your heart towards the Eternal Maker?

Quranic Endorsement and the Worlds
Quranic verses and the two worlds of inference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the Quran endorses the inference from the higher and lower worlds, especially through numerous verses and the study of human anatomy.

Core Idea
The Quran repeatedly guides to the proof from creation, both celestial and terrestrial.

This lesson presents the extensive Quranic support for the proof from bodies. The author cites specific verses and notes that there are over eighty such references. He also distinguishes between the higher world (celestial spheres and planets) and the lower world (elements, compounds, minerals, plants, animals, and especially man), highlighting the depth of the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Explore the cited Quranic verses
Read the verses quoted: from surah al-Baqarah, al-A'raf, Fussilat, al-Mursalat, and al-Rum. Notice how each points to signs in creation.
These verses are all familiar and often recited.
2
Understand the two realms: higher and lower worlds
The passage mentions العالم الأعلى (higher world) and العالم الأسفل (lower world). The higher includes celestial bodies; the lower includes elements, compounds, and living beings.
3
Appreciate the emphasis on man
The author specifically highlights man, including his body as studied in anatomy and his spirit. This shows that the human self is a profound sign.
Scientific study reinforces this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own body—its complexity, the way your heart beats without your conscious command, or the fact that you are reading these words right now—do you honestly feel that this could have come about without a Creator? Or have you become numb to the wonder of your own existence? What would it take for you to genuinely see yourself as an ayat (sign) of Allah today?
?
The passage distinguishes between higher and lower worlds. In your daily life, do you spend more time pondering the heavens (sunsets, stars) or the earth (your own body, the plants and animals around you)? Which domain have you been neglecting, and why do you think that is? Commit today to reflect on one sign from the neglected world.
?
The Quran says signs are for 'a people who use reason.' Think about your own reasoning: have you ever used the evidence of your own anatomy and spirit to strengthen your certainty in Allah? If not, what holds you back? Will you take one step this week to learn about the body's design with the intention of knowing its Maker?

Allah's Existence

The Four Ways of Inferring a Creator from the World
Author states the four common ways of inferring a Creator from contingent beings, with Qur'anic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical kalam proof from contingency, its four common forms, and its foundation in rational necessity and innate disposition.

Core Idea
Evidence for God is woven into creation and the reason of every human.

This passage lays out the classical Islamic rational argument for the existence of God known as the proof from contingency (burhan al-imkan). It states that from the contingent and originated nature of the world, we can infer a necessary, powerful, and wise Maker. The author notes that this reasoning appears in four ways, referenced in over eighty Quranic verses, and he provides several examples from scripture. This lesson unpacks that argument and its scriptural roots.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through.
The passage is compact; read slowly.
2
Identify the four ways
The author says the inference comes 'in four ways.' Look for the list that follows. Notice that he does not enumerate them explicitly but implies them through the Quranic verses and the description of higher and lower worlds.
The four ways are variations of the same argument from contingency.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
The author cites five specific verses. Read each one and note how each points to a different aspect of creation (heavens, earth, night, day, etc.).
These verses are examples; the author says over eighty exist.
4
Understand the foundational premise
The author concludes with the rational foundation: every contingent and originated thing depends on a cause, and this is known innately (fitra). Understand why this premise is considered necessary.
This premise underlies all four ways.
5
Reflect on the argument's scope
Consider how the argument uses both celestial and terrestrial phenomena, including human anatomy and psychology, to demonstrate the Maker's power and wisdom.
The argument is comprehensive and interdisciplinary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد صح الاستدلال بذواتها وصفاتها لإمكانها وحدوثها على وجود صانع قديم قادر حكيم فتأتي في أربعة طرق هي الشائعة فيما بين الجمهور وأشير إليها في أكثر من ثمانين موضعاً من كتاب الله تعالى. كقوله تعالى: {إن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار والفلك التي تجري في البحر بما ينفع الناس، وما أنزل الله من السماء من ماء فأحيا به الأرض بعد موتها وبث فيها من كل دابة، وتصريف الرياح والسحاب المسخر بين السماء والأرض لآيات لقوم يعقلون} وكقوله تعالى: {ومن آياته الليل والنهار والشمس والقمر والنجوم مسخرات بأمره}. وكقوله تعالى: {سنريهم آياتنا في الآفاق وفي أنفسهم} وكقوله تعالى: {ألم نخلقكم من ماء مهين} وكقوله تعالى: {ومن آياته خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف ألسنتكم وألوانكم} إلى غير ذلك من مواضع الإرشاد إلى الاستدلال بالعالم الأعلى من الأفلاك والكواكب وحركاتها، وأوضاعها، والأحوال المتعلقة بها، وبالعالم الأسفل من طبقات العناصر، ومراتب امتزاجاتها، والآثار العلوية والسفلية وأحوال المعادن والنبات والحيوانات سيما الإنسان وما أودع بدنه مما يشهد به علم التشريح وروحه بما ذكر في علم النفس ومبنى الكل على أن افتقار الممكن إلى الموجد، والحادث إلى المحدث ضروري تشهد به الفطرة، وأن فاعل العجائب والغرائب على الوجه الأوفق الأصلح لا يكون إلا قادراً حكيماً.
And indeed, inference from their essences and attributes, due to their contingency and origination, to the existence of an eternal, powerful, wise Maker is sound. It comes in four ways that are common among the masses, and reference is made to them in more than eighty places in the Book of God Almighty: such as His saying: {Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth, and the alternation of the night and the day, and the ships which sail through the sea with that which benefits people, and what God has sent down from the sky of water, giving life thereby to the earth after its lifelessness and dispersing therein every [kind of] moving creature, and [His] directing of the winds and the clouds controlled between the heaven and the earth are signs for a people who use reason} and like His saying: {And of His signs are the night and day and the sun and moon and the stars, subjected by His command} and like His saying: {We will show them Our signs in the horizons and within themselves} and like His saying: {Did We not create you from a despised liquid?} and like His saying: {And of His signs is the creation of the heavens and the earth and the diversity of your languages and your colors} besides other places of guidance to inference from the higher world—the celestial spheres, the planets, their motions, positions, and related states—and from the lower world—the layers of elements, the degrees of their mixtures, the upper and lower effects, and the states of minerals, plants, and animals, especially the human being, and what is lodged in his body as attested by the science of anatomy, and his soul as mentioned in psychology. And the foundation of all is that the dependence of the contingent upon the bringer-into-existence, and the originated upon the originator, is necessary, attested to by innate disposition, and that the agent of wonders and marvels in the most fitting and beneficial manner cannot be other than powerful and wise.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the classical kalam proof for the existence of God from the contingency of the world. It begins by stating that inference from the essences and attributes of contingent things—their possibility and temporal origination—to a Maker who is eternal, powerful, and wise is sound. This inference is commonly presented in four ways, which are referenced in over eighty Quranic verses. The author then cites five specific verses as examples (Quran 2:164, 7:54, 41:53, 77:20, 30:22), each pointing to different aspects of creation. The argument then broadens to include both the celestial realm (spheres, planets, motions) and the terrestrial realm (elements, mixtures, minerals, plants, animals, and especially humans, with reference to anatomy and psychology). The author concludes by stating the foundational premise: the necessary dependence of every contingent being on a bringer-into-existence, and every originated thing on an originator, is a self-evident truth attested to by natural intuition (fitra). Moreover, the agent of such wonders and marvels, arranged in the most fitting and beneficial manner, must be powerful and wise. Thus, the passage is a concise yet comprehensive summary of the cosmological argument, rooted in both rational necessity and scriptural testimony.

ممكن
صانع
فطرة
أربعة طرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four ways are separate and independent proofs.
Correction: The four ways are variations of the same argument from contingency, emphasizing different aspects of creation.

Checkpoint
What is the foundational premise that underlies all four ways of reasoning to a Creator?
A) The world is eternal and uncreated.
B) The contingent needs a cause, and the originated needs an originator, as attested by innate disposition.
C) The Quran explicitly names the Creator in every verse.

Key Takeaway
The proof from contingency is foundational to Islamic theology, grounded in both rational necessity and scriptural testimony.

Why the Creator Must Be the Necessary Being
The author presents an objection and three answers proving the Creator must be the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three arguments proving that the Creator cannot be a contingent spiritual substance but must be the Necessary Being, who is absolutely independent and the endpoint of all causal chains.

Core Idea
The Creator must be absolutely independent; otherwise, the chain of causation would never end.

The objector tries to reduce the Creator to a contingent spiritual being, but the author responds with three distinct arguments. Each argument approaches the necessity of the Creator from a different angle: the first uses intuitive conjecture, the second uses logical reasoning to avoid infinite regress, and the third explains the very purpose of guiding to this conclusion. This lesson unpacks these three answers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Start by reading the objection carefully: it grants the previous argument but asks why the Maker could not be a contingent spiritual substance instead of the Necessary Being. Notice the assumption that contingency is possible.
The objector here is positing an alternative that would still require a creator further up.
2
Examine the first answer (intuitive)
Read the first answer: it claims intuition and conjecture tell us that the Maker of such an ordered universe must be absolutely independent. Note that amassing many such persuasive arguments strengthens the probability to certainty.
This is not mere guesswork—it is a cumulative argument leading to certainty.
3
Examine the second answer (logical regress)
Read the second answer: if the Maker is created, then its creator is more deserving of power and wisdom. This chain cannot go to infinity because infinite regress is invalid, so it ends with the Necessary Being.
This argument relies on the proof of the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul).
4
Examine the third answer (purpose of argument)
Read the third answer: the whole purpose of guiding people to these inferences is to alert those who deny a Maker who is the beginning, end, and ultimate refuge. This shows the teleological necessity of the Necessary Being.
This answer focuses on the goal of the argument rather than a strict logical proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : سلمنا ذلك لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك الصانع جوهراً روحانياً من جملة الممكنات دون الواجب تعالى وتقدس. فالجواب : من وجوه : الأول : أنه يعلم بالحدس والتخمين أن الصانع لمثل هذا لا يكون إلا غنياً مطلقاً يفتقر إليه في كل شيء، ولا يفتقر هو إلى شيء بل يكون وجوده لذاته فيكون الدليل من الإقناعيات والاستكثار منها كثيراً مما يقوي الظن حيث يفضي إلى اليقين . الثاني : أن ذهن العاقل ينساق إلى أن هذا الصانع إن كان هو الواجب الخالق فذاك ، وإن كان هو مخلوقاً، فخالقه أولى بأن يكون قادراً حكيماً، ولا يذهب ذلك إلى غير النهاية لظهور بعض أدلة بطلان التسلسل، فيكون المنتهى إلى الواجب تعالى وتقدس ولهذا صرح في كثير من المواضع بأن تلك الآيات إنما هي لقوم يعقلون الثالث: أن المقصود بالإرشاد إلى هذه الاستدلالات تنبيه من لم يعترف بوجود صانع يكون منه المبدأ وإليه المنتهى وله الأمر والنهي وكونه ملجأ الكل عند انقطاع
If it is said: We grant that, but why is it not possible that that Maker be a spiritual substance among contingent things, rather than the Necessary, exalted and sanctified? The answer [comes] from several aspects: First: It is known by intuition and conjecture that the Maker of something like this cannot be other than absolutely independent, to Whom everything is needy, and He is not needy of anything, rather His existence is by His essence. Thus the proof becomes from persuasive arguments, and amassing many of them greatly strengthens opinion when it leads to certainty. Second: The mind of the rational person is led to [see] that this Maker—if He is the Necessary Creator, then that is it; but if He is created, then His Creator is more deserving of being powerful and wise, and that does not go on to infinity due to the appearance of some proofs of the invalidity of regress. So the endpoint is the Necessary, exalted and sanctified. For this reason it is stated in many places that those signs are only for people who use reason. Third: The purpose of guiding to these inferences is to alert those who do not acknowledge the existence of a Maker from Whom is the beginning and to Whom is the end, and to Whom belongs command and prohibition, and Who is the refuge of all when [all] ceases.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objector grants the previous argument but proposes an alternative: why must the Maker be the Necessary Being? Could it not be a contingent spiritual substance (a jinn or angel) that itself depends on another? The author provides three answers. First answer (intuitive/conjectural): our intuitive sense and conjecture tell us that the Maker of such an immense and ordered universe must be absolutely independent—needy of nothing and the one on whom everything depends. Such reasoning is persuasive, and the accumulation of many such arguments strengthens the probability to the point of certainty. Second answer (logical): the rational mind is naturally led to realize that if the maker is the Necessary Creator, that is sufficient; but if it is created, then its own creator is even more deserving of being powerful and wise. This series cannot go on infinitely because there are clear proofs that infinite regress (tasalsul) is invalid. Therefore, the chain must terminate in the Necessary Being. The author notes that this is why the Quran often says those signs are for people who use reason—they can follow this logical chain. Third answer (purpose of the argument): the whole purpose of guiding people to these inferences is to alert those who deny the existence of a Maker who is the alpha and omega, who has command and prohibition, and who is the ultimate refuge when all else fails. The passage thus defends the necessity of the Creator's necessity, using intuitive, logical, and teleological arguments.

تسلسل
إقناعيات
حدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first answer is merely conjectural and cannot lead to certainty.
Correction: The author states that amassing many persuasive arguments strengthens opinion to the point of certainty, so it is not mere guesswork.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical argument in the second answer to the objection?
A) The Creator must be a spiritual substance.
B) If the maker is created, its creator is more deserving of being powerful and wise, and this chain ends at the Necessary Being because infinite regress is invalid.
C) The evidence from the Quran is too weak to prove necessity.
D) The objection is based on false premises about contingency.

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be the Necessary Being, as shown by intuitive, logical, and teleological arguments that each rule out the possibility of a contingent spiritual substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three answers in this passage challenges you most personally? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that the Creator is absolutely independent and you are absolutely dependent?
?
The second answer uses logic to stop an infinite regress. But in your own life, where do you find yourself assuming that things will go on forever—your health, your wealth, your relationships—without tracing them back to God? How does this passage call you to acknowledge Him as the ultimate source before it's too late?
?
The third answer says the Maker is the refuge when all ceases. Think of a moment when something you relied on completely fell apart. Did you turn to God as your refuge, or did you scramble for other supports? What concrete step can you take today to make Him your first refuge—not your last resort?

Proof that the Essence of the Necessary Being Differs from Contingents
Sanusi proves essential difference via a dichotomy and refutes a counter-view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that the essence of the Necessary Being is essentially different from contingent beings, and how the argument refutes the claim of similarity based on a common concept of 'dhat'.

Core Idea
Essential difference precedes attribution: two essences cannot share a single essence.

Sanusi presents a decisive proof that the Necessary Being's essence is fundamentally distinct from all contingent essences. Through a dichotomy involving necessity and particularity, he shows that any attempt to posit similarity leads to absurd consequences. The passage then considers and refutes an alternative view that posits similarity based on common existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and identify the author's assertion that the essence of the Necessary Being differs from contingent essences.
This is the conclusion the argument supports.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Work through the conditional: if they were similar, distinction would come from particularity. Then necessity attaches either to essence alone (making contingents necessary) or to essence-with-particularity (making the Necessary composite). Both are unacceptable.
This is a classic qiyas al-taqsim (proof by division).
3
Examine the objection and refutation
The opposing view says essences are similar but distinguished by attributes. The refutation shows that the unity of the concept of 'dhat' does not imply similarity of essences, as it applies as a necessary concomitant, not an essential.
This refines the understanding of predication in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث
ذات الواجب تخالف الممكنات
قال : ذات الواجب تخالف ذوات الممكنات وإلا لكانت امتيازه بخصوصيته وحينئذ فالوجوب إما للذات فيلزم وجوب الممكنات أو مع الخصوصية فيلزم إمكان الواجب لتركبه وقيل : بل تماثلها وتمتاز بالوجوب والحياة وكمال العلم والقدرة أو بالألوهية الموجبة للأربعة بمثل ما مرت من أدلة اشتراك الوجود ورد بأنها إنما تفيد اتحاد مفهوم الذات الصادق على الذوات لا تماثل الذوات لأن وقوعه عليها وقوع اللازم لا لذاتي كما مر في الوجود) .
The third chapter: The essence of the Necessary Being differs from contingent beings. He said: The essence of the Necessary Being differs from the essences of contingent beings; otherwise its distinction would be by its particularity. Then necessity is either for the essence, which necessitates necessity for contingent beings, or with the particularity, which necessitates possibility for the Necessary Being due to its composition. And it was said: Rather they are similar and distinguished by necessity, life, perfect knowledge, and complete power, or by a state called divinity necessitating these four, based on the arguments mentioned for the commonality of existence. And it was refuted that these only indicate the unity of the concept of essence (dhat) applying to essences, not the similarity of essences, because its occurrence upon them is the occurrence of a necessary concomitant, not as an essential, as has passed in the discussion of existence.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and refutation. The author first asserts that the essence of the Necessary Being (al-wajib) differs from the essences of contingent beings (al-mumkinat). The proof is via a conditional: if they were similar, the distinction would come from a particularity (khussusiyya). Then necessity (wujub) would either attach to the essence itself, implying that contingent essences would also be necessary, which is false; or it would attach to the essence plus the particularity, making the Necessary Being composite (murakkab), which contradicts its necessary existence. The passage then reports a contrary view (qila): the essences are similar and distinguished by four states (wujub, life, knowledge, power) or a fifth state (ilahiyya). This is refuted by noting that the arguments for commonality of existence only prove unity of the concept of 'dhat' (self-subsisting entity), not similarity of the essences themselves, because the concept applies as a necessary concomitant (lazim), not as an essential (dhati).

Key Terms
الواجب
الممكنات
الذات
الخصوصية
الوجوب
الإمكان
التركيب
العارض
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the unity of the concept of 'dhat' implies the essences of the Necessary and contingents are similar in reality.
Correction: The concept 'dhat' applies as a necessary concomitant (lazim) to each essence, not as an essential constituent (dhati), so conceptual unity does not entail real essential similarity.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what would follow if the essence of the Necessary Being were similar to contingent essences?
A) Necessity would be for the essence alone, making contingents necessary.
B) Necessity would attach to the particularity, making the Necessary Being composite.
C) Both A and B are possible consequences.
D) The essences would share all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's essence is fundamentally distinct from all contingent essences, and any claim of similarity based on a common concept is refuted by the nature of predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever thought that God's essence is 'like' something you know, how does Sanusi's proof challenge that assumption? What does this mean for how you conceive of your Lord?
?
The dilemma shows that claiming similarity leads to absurd consequences. Are there areas in your belief where you unconsciously attribute human-like limitations to Allah? What would it take to purify your tawheed?
?
The refutation explains that a shared term does not imply shared reality. In your own worship, do you ever confuse the concept of 'existence' as applied to Allah and to creation? How can you deepen your sense of His absolute uniqueness?

The True Position: Real Difference but Conceptual Commonality
The author affirms real difference while explaining conceptual commonality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that the Necessary Being and contingent beings are really different yet share concepts of essence and existence as accidental commonalities.

Core Idea
Essential difference, conceptual commonality.

This passage presents the author's own position after reviewing other views. He insists on a real difference between the Necessary and contingents, but also explains how they share the concept of 'dhat' and existence—not as constitutive parts, but as external concomitants. Pay close attention to the nuance: sharing a concept does not imply sharing reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a thesis, a proof by contradiction, then an explanation of commonality.
2
Identify the main thesis
The author states that the Necessary differs from contingents in essence and reality. Underline that claim.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
If they were similar, then necessity and possibility would have to be either concomitants of essence (leading to sharing) or essence-with-particularity (leading to composition). See how both options are impossible.
4
Grasp the nuance about commonality
The author then explains that 'dhat' and 'wujud' are common as concepts, but only as accidental (arid) or external necessary concomitant (lazim khariji), not constitutive (muqawwim).
This is the key point to avoid misunderstanding.
5
Review the arguments for commonality
Three arguments are cited: division of existence, affirmation of absolute together with hesitation about particularity, and the argument from the opposite. Reflect on how they support commonality without implying sameness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : الحق أن الواجب تعالى يخالف الممكنات في الذات والحقيقة، إذ لو تماثلا ، وامتاز كل عن الآخر بخصوصه، فمثل الوجوب والإمكان إما أن يكون من لوازم الذات، فيلزم اشتراك الكل فيه، أو الذات مع الخصوصية، فيلزم التركيب المنافي للوجوب الذاتي نعم تشارك ذاته ذات الممكنات، بمعنى أن مفهوم الذات أعني ما يقوم بنفسه، ويقوم به غيره صادق على الكل، صدق العارض على المعروض، كما أن وجود الواجب، ووجود الممكن مع اختلافهما بالحقيقة يشتركان في مطلق الوجود الواقع عليهما ، وقوع لازم خارجي غير مقوم بالأدلة المذكورة في اشتراك الوجود من صحة القسمة إلى الواجب والممكن، ومن الجزم بالمطلق مع التردد في اتحاد الخصوصية، ومن المقابل، بل لا يتغير إلا الاشتراك في مفهوم الذات ، وصدقه على جميع الذوات من غير دلالة على تماثل الذوات وتشاركها في الحقيقة .
The third chapter: The truth is that the Necessary, exalted is He, differs from contingent beings in essence and reality. For if they were similar, each distinguished from the other by its particularity, then like necessity and possibility would either be among the necessary concomitants of the essence, which would necessitate the sharing of all in it, or (they would be) the essence along with the particularity, which would necessitate composition that contradicts essential necessity. Indeed, His essence shares (in the concept) with the essence of contingent beings, in the sense that the concept of essence (dhat), i.e., what is self-subsisting and that by which other things subsist, applies to all, as an accident applies to its subject. Just as the existence of the Necessary and the existence of the contingent, with their difference in reality, share in absolute existence that occurs upon them, (they share) as a necessary external concomitant not constitutive, based on the arguments mentioned in the commonality of existence: the validity of division into the Necessary and the contingent, and the affirmation of the absolute while hesitating about the unity of particularity, and from the opposite. Rather, there is no change except the commonality in the concept of essence and its truthfulness upon all essences without indicating the similarity of essences and their sharing in reality.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage states the author's own position. It begins by reiterating that the Necessary Being (al-wajib ta'ala) differs from contingents (al-mumkinat) in essence and reality. The proof is similar to the first: if they were similar, necessity and possibility must be either concomitants of the essence (leading to sharing) or attached to essence-with-particularity (leading to composition). The author then introduces a nuance: the essence of the Necessary shares the concept of 'dhat' with contingents. 'Dhat' here is defined as 'what is self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi) and that by which others subsist (yaqumu bihi ghayruhu).' This concept applies to all, but as an accident (arid) applies to its subject, not as a constitutive genus. Similarly, existence (wujud) of the Necessary and existence of the contingent share the absolute concept of existence, but as an external necessary concomitant (lazim khariji), not as constitutive (muqawwim). The arguments for this commonality are listed: the validity of dividing existence into necessary and possible, affirming the absolute while being uncertain about the particularity, and the argument from the opposite. The conclusion: only the concept of 'dhat' is common; it does not indicate real similarity or sharing in reality.

Key Terms
الذات
الحقيقة
العارض
المعروض
الوجود
اللازم
المقوم
القسمة
المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the Necessary and contingents share the concept of 'dhat', then they must be similar in reality.
Correction: The author clarifies that the concept of 'dhat' applies as an accident (arid) to its subject, not as a constitutive genus (muqawwim), so it does not imply real similarity.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the commonality of the concept of 'dhat' between the Necessary Being and contingent beings?
A) As a constitutive genus that applies to both.
B) As an accident (arid) that applies to its subject.
C) As a necessary concomitant that is part of the essence.
D) As a predicate that indicates real similarity.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being and contingent beings are different in essence and reality, but share the concepts of 'dhat' and existence as accidental commonalities—not constitutive ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists' and 'I exist', is there any hidden assumption in your heart that existence is the same 'thing' for both? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it demand of your understanding of Tawhid?
?
The lesson shows that conceptual commonality can exist without real similarity. Think of a time you assumed that because you and someone else shared a label (like 'Muslim' or 'human'), you shared the same reality or worth. How does this passage discipline how you think about God's uniqueness compared to everything else?
?
If God's essence is utterly different from yours, does that make you feel distant? Or does it magnify His majesty and make your worship more serious? How will you actively remind yourself of this difference when you perform any act of worship today?

Objection and Answer on Composition of the Necessary Existent
The author answers an objection about composition in the necessary existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun's assertion that both the necessary and possible have complete existence does not lead to composition in the necessary existent.

Core Idea
Necessity flows from the unique quiddity, not from existence itself.

An objector argues that if both the necessary and possible have complete existence, then the necessary would have both necessity and existence, implying composition. The author responds by clarifying that necessity is a property of the quiddity's difference from others, not a component added by existence. This short but crucial exchange defends divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection (فإن قيل) which questions how the mutakallimun avoid composition. Note the assumption that both necessary and possible have complete existence.
The objection presumes that existence is a shared attribute.
2
Read the answer
Read the author's reply (قلنا). Notice the key phrase: 'المقتضي للوجوب هو المخالفة الماهية لسائر الماهيات' — the cause of necessity is the quiddity's difference.
Existence (الوجود) is described as additional (زائد) to the quiddity.
3
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms: واجب (necessary), ممكن (possible), تركيب (composition), وجوب (necessity), ماهية (quiddity), وجود زائد (additional existence). Understand how they relate.
4
Analyze the logic
Why does the answer prevent composition? Because necessity is not a part of the essence; it is a concomitant of the quiddity's uniqueness. Existence is an extra attribute, but it does not cause necessity. Therefore, the necessary remains simple.
Composition would only occur if necessity and existence were both essential constituents.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this answer upholds divine simplicity (tawhid) in kalam. The necessary existent is one in essence, with no internal parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فكيف لم يلزم المتكلمين القائلين بتمام وجود الواجب والممكن تركب الواجب . . ؟ قلنا : لأن المتصف بالوجوب، والمقتضي للوجوب هو المخالفة الماهية لسائر الماهيات، والوجود زائد عليها.
If it is said: How did it not entail for the mutakallimun who assert the completeness of existence of the necessary and the possible the composition of the necessary? We say: Because that which is described with necessity, and the necessitating of necessity, is the quiddity's difference from other quiddities, and existence is additional to it.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The objector asks why the mutakallimun who hold that both the necessary and the possible have complete existence (تام الوجود) are not led to the conclusion that the necessary existent is composite (تركب). The author replies that the attribute of necessity (الوجوب) belongs to the quiddity (الماهية) because of its being different from all other quiddities; existence (الوجود) is an extra attribute added to the quiddity. Therefore, the necessary existent remains simple: necessity is not a constituent of its essence but a concomitant of its unique quiddity.

Correction: The passage clarifies that necessity is not a part of the essence; it is a consequence of the quiddity's uniqueness, while existence is an additional attribute. Thus, God remains simple.

Why the Maker Must Be Eternal and Everlasting
Sanusi explains why some mutakallimun must prove the Maker's eternity and everlastingness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun needed to prove the Maker's eternity and everlastingness separately, and the two rational proofs they used.

Core Idea
Proving a Maker is not enough; you must also prove He is eternal and everlasting.

This passage from al-Maqasid explains a subtle point in theology. While the Necessary Existent's eternity follows directly from its necessity, some theologians only proved a Maker for the world without establishing His necessity. For them, additional arguments were needed to demonstrate that the Maker is without beginning and without end. The author presents two such arguments, one for each attribute.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Read the opening lines to grasp why some mutakallimun needed separate proofs for eternity and everlastingness, unlike those who prove a Necessary Existent.
The key distinction is whether one proves a Maker as necessary or merely as a cause.
2
Study the first proof for eternality
The first argument shows that if the Maker were originated, He would need an originator, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible.
This proof refutes the chain (tasalsul) and establishes beginninglessness.
3
Study the second proof for everlastingness
The second argument demonstrates that non-existence is impossible for the Eternal, either because He is necessary in Himself or because the causal chain ends with Him by necessitation.
The note about choice vs. necessitation clarifies that a necessary being cannot be preceded by non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع في أن الصانع أزلي أبدي لما كان الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه لم يحتج بعد إثباته كونه أزلياً أبدياً . والمتكلمون لما اقتصروا على إثبات صانع للعالم افتقروا ببيانهم إلى إثبات ذلك فالأزلية لابطال التسلسل ونما سيجيء من أن الكل بقدرة القديم والأبدية لما مر من ستلزام القديم امتناع العدم. قال : المبحث الرابع : قد يجعل من مطالب هذا الباب أن الصانع أزلي أبدي ولا حاجة إليه، بعد إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته لأن من ضرورة وجوب الوجود امتناع العدم أزلاً وأبداً. وبعض المتكلمين لما افتقروا في البيان على أن لهذا العالم صانعاً من غير بيان كونه واجباً أو ممكناً ، افتقروا إلى إثبات كونه أزلياً أبدياً فبينوا : الأول : بأنه لو كان حادثاً لكان له محدث ويتسلسل، وبأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن المؤثر في وجود هذا العالم هو الله تعالى، من غير واسطة الثاني : بأن القديم يمتنع عليه العدم لكونه واجباً أو منتهياً إليه بطريق الإيجاب . لأن الصادر بطريق الاختيار يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم . وقد سبق بيان ذلك .
The fourth investigation: On the Maker being eternal and everlasting. Since the necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible, after establishing it there is no need to prove it is eternal and everlasting. And the mutakallimun, since they confined themselves to proving a Maker of the world, needed in their demonstration to prove that. So the eternality (al-azaliyya) is to refute the chain (tasalsul), and similarly what will come that all is by the power of the Eternal; and the everlastingness (al-azaliyya?) because of what has passed that the Eternal entails the impossibility of non-existence. He said: The fourth investigation: It may be made among the demands of this chapter that the Maker is eternal and everlasting, and there is no need for it after establishing the Necessary of Existence in itself, because from the necessity of existence comes the impossibility of non-existence eternally and everlastingly. And some mutakallimun, since they lacked in demonstration that this world has a Maker without demonstrating whether He is necessary or possible, they needed to prove that He is eternal and everlasting. So they demonstrated: First: that if He were originated (hadith), He would have an originator, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul), and we will establish the proof that the effecting agent in the existence of this world is God Almighty, without intermediary. Second: that non-existence is impossible for the Eternal because He is necessary or [because] it ends to Him by way of necessitation (al-ijab). For what issues by way of choice (al-ikhtiyar) is preceded by non-existence. This has been explained before.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a new section (المبحث الرابع) dealing with the Maker's eternality (أزلية) and everlastingness (أبدية). The author first states the general principle: if one has already proven the necessary existent (واجب الوجود) whose non-existence is impossible, then its eternity and everlastingness follow directly. However, some mutakallimun did not prove a necessary existent; they only proved a Maker (صانع) for the world without determining whether that Maker is necessary or possible. For them, separate arguments are needed. The author gives two proofs: The first proof (الأول) establishes eternality: if the Maker were originated (حادث), He would need an originator, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). They also claim that the true immediate cause of the world is God. The second proof (الثاني) establishes everlastingness: non-existence is impossible for the Eternal (القديم) either because He is necessary in Himself or because the chain of causes culminates in Him by necessitation (إيجاب). An additional note distinguishes between necessitation and choice: what is produced by choice is preceded by non-existence, implying that a necessary being cannot be preceded by non-existence. The author concludes by referring to a previous explanation.

The mutakallimun's proof of a Maker required separate arguments for eternality and everlastingness, demonstrating that the Maker is beginningless and endless.

The Creator's Eternality: Thesis and Two Proofs
Al-Taftazani states the fourth section on the Creator's eternality and gives two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proving the Creator's eternality (azali and abadi) is necessary for those who only prove a maker for the world, and the two proofs used to establish that eternality.

Core Idea
Eternality follows from necessary existence, but if you only prove a maker, you need separate proofs.

Al-Taftazani here turns to a crucial attribute of the Creator: His eternity without beginning and without end. While this is a necessary consequence of being the Necessary Existence, many theologians who only demonstrate that the world has a maker must go further to establish that this maker is eternal. The passage presents two classic proofs drawn from the impossibility of infinite regress and the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal. Let's read the source and see how these arguments unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
The passage begins by stating that if we already prove a Necessary Existence, eternality is automatic. But some mutakallimun only prove a maker, so they need separate proofs. Find that distinction.
2
Understand the first proof: argument from infinite regress
The first proof says if the maker were originated, it would need an originator, leading to an infinite chain which is impossible. Also, they will later establish that God is the direct effecter without intermediaries.
3
Understand the second proof: impossibility of cessation for the eternal
The second proof argues that the eternal cannot cease to exist because it is either necessary in itself or its existence ends in a necessary cause. And what comes into being by choice is preceded by non-existence, so the eternal cannot be by choice.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This lesson shows that proving God's existence is not the same as proving His attributes; additional reasoning is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
في أن الصانع أزلي أبدي

لما كان الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه لم يحتج بعد إثباته كونه أزلياً أبدياً . والمتكلمون لما اقتصروا على إثبات صانع للعالم افتقروا ببيانهم إلى إثبات ذلك فالأزلية لابطال التسلسل وما سيجيء من أن الكل بقدرة القديم والأبدية لما مر من استلزام القديم امتناع العدم.

قال : المبحث الرابع : قد يجعل من مطالب هذا الباب أن الصانع أزلي أبدي ولا حاجة إليه، بعد إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته لأن من ضرورة وجوب الوجود امتناع العدم أزلاً وأبداً.

وبعض المتكلمين لما افتقروا في البيان على أن لهذا العالم صانعاً من غير بيان كونه واجباً أو ممكناً، افتقروا إلى إثبات كونه أزلياً أبدياً فبينوا : الأول : بأنه لو كان حادثاً لكان له محدث ويتسلسل، وبأنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن المؤثر في وجود هذا العالم هو الله تعالى، من غير واسطة

الثاني : بأن القديم يمتنع عليه العدم لكونه واجباً أو منتهياً إليه بطريق الإيجاب . لأن الصادر بطريق الاختيار يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم . وقد سبق بيان ذلك .
The Fourth Section: On the Creator being eternal without beginning and eternal without end.

Since the necessary being is that whose non-existence is impossible, after proving Him, His being eternal without beginning and without end does not need [proof]. But the mutakallimun, when they confined themselves to proving a maker for the world, needed by their demonstration to prove that. So the eternal without beginning is for the invalidation of infinite regress, and what will come that all things are by the power of the Eternal; and the eternal without end is because of what has passed of the implication of the Eternal's impossibility of non-existence.

He said: The fourth section: It may be made among the demands of this chapter that the Creator is eternal without beginning and eternal without end, and there is no need for it after proving a Necessary Existence in itself, because from the necessity of necessary existence follows the impossibility of non-existence eternally without beginning and without end.

And some mutakallimun, when they needed in the demonstration that this world has a maker without demonstrating whether He is necessary or possible, they needed to prove that He is eternal without beginning and without end. So they clarified: The first: that if He were originated, He would have an originator, and [that would lead to] infinite regress, and that we will establish the proof that the effecter in the existence of this world is God Most High, without any intermediary.

The second: that the eternal is impossible to cease to exist because He is necessary or because He ends in [a necessary cause] by way of necessitation. For the emanation by way of choice is preceded by non-existence. And that has been explained before.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth section of the chapter, which addresses the eternality (azali and abadi) of the Creator. The author first states the principle: once we have proven a wājib al-wujūd (Necessary Existence), the attributes of being eternal without beginning (azalī) and eternal without end (abadī) follow necessarily, since impossibility of non-existence is essential to necessary existence. Therefore, for one who proves the Necessary Existence directly, no separate proof of eternality is needed. However, the mutakallimun typically only prove that the world has a maker (ṣāniʿ) without establishing whether that maker is necessary or contingent. Consequently, they must separately prove that the maker is eternal, because from merely showing that a contingent world requires a maker, it does not follow that the maker itself is eternal. The author then recounts their two proofs. The first proof is based on the impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul): if the maker were originated (ḥādith), it would require another originator, leading to an infinite chain. Since infinite regress is impossible, the maker must be eternal. Moreover, they will later establish that the direct effecter of the world's existence is God without any intermediary, reinforcing that no chain of originated beings exists. The second proof: the eternal (al-qadīm) is impossible to cease to exist, either because it is necessary in itself (wājib bi-dhātih) or because it is an effect that ends in a necessary cause by way of necessitation (ījāb). The author notes that emanation by choice (ikhtiyār) is preceded by non-existence, so if something comes into existence through choice, it is not eternal. Thus, the eternal cannot be produced by choice; it must be necessary. The author references that this was explained earlier.

أبدي
واجب الوجود
تسلسل
قديم
حادث
إيجاب
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proving that the world has a Creator automatically proves that the Creator is eternal.
Correction: The passage explains that if one only proves a maker (ṣāniʿ) without establishing that the maker is the Necessary Existence, additional arguments are needed to show that the maker is eternal without beginning and without end.

Checkpoint
Why do some mutakallimun need separate proofs for the Creator's eternality?
A) Because they deny that God is eternal.
B) Because they only prove that the world has a maker, not whether that maker is necessary or contingent.
C) Because they reject the concept of necessary existence.
D) Because they accept infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The Creator's eternality is a necessary consequence of being the Necessary Existence, but those who only prove a maker must employ arguments from infinite regress and the impossibility of cessation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

But do you live as if He is the only necessary being in your life, or do you act as if other things are necessary for your happiness? What would change if you internalized that He alone is eternal and all else is contingent?

The passage says that some only prove a maker and then need extra arguments for eternality. Have you ever reduced your faith to just believing in a Creator without grasping His absolute necessity? In what ways does your worship reflect that He is not just a maker but the Eternal One who cannot cease to exist?

The Four Pillars of Divine Transcendence
Lists the four main topics of the second chapter on tawhid and divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four foundational topics that establish God's transcendence in Islamic theology, including the denial of anthropomorphism, incarnation, and temporal attributes.

Core Idea
God is absolutely unique: not body, not substance, not united, not temporal.

After establishing the general framework, the author now introduces the specific inquiries of the second chapter. These four topics form the core of tawhid concerning divine transcendence. They systematically negate any resemblance between God and creation, whether in essence, composition, or attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own enumeration come through before you interpret.
Notice the chapter heading and the four numbered items.
2
Identify the four topics
List each of the four topics in your own words. Note how each one negates a specific attribute or relationship from God.
The first topic is about transcendence in general; the next three are specific denials.
3
Understand the theological significance
For each negation, ask yourself: what concept of God does this protect? For example, denying body and substance guards against anthropomorphism.
Think about how each denial safeguards God's uniqueness.
4
Connect to common misunderstandings
Some might think 'tawhid' only means oneness of God. Reflect on how the passage shows tawhid also includes transcendence above all created attributes.
This is a key nuance in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
وفيه مباحث :
الأول : في التوحيد في التنزيهات
الثاني : الله تعالى ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض
الثالث : الله تعالى لا يتحد بغيره
الرابع : امتناع اتصاف الله تعالى بالحوادث
The second chapter, containing investigations: The first: On tawhid concerning transcendences. The second: God Most High is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident. The third: God Most High does not unite with another. The fourth: The impossibility of God Most High being described with temporal attributes.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and outline. It introduces the second chapter of the work, which will cover four key topics in Islamic theology. The first topic is 'tawhid' (divine unity) specifically regarding 'transcendences' (tanzeehat), meaning God's absolute transcendence above any resemblance to creation. The second topic asserts that God is neither a body (jism), nor a substance (jawhar), nor an accident ('arad). This refutes anthropomorphic and philosophical conceptions that limit God to physical or categorical descriptions. The third topic states that God does not unite (ittihad) with anything else, rejecting any notion of incarnation or pantheistic union. The fourth topic denies the possibility of God being described with temporal attributes (hawadith), meaning attributes that come into existence in time, affirming His eternal and unchanging nature. These four topics lay the foundations for a proper understanding of God's essence and attributes.

Key Terms
توحيد
تنزيه
جسم
جوهر
عرض
اتحاد
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'tawhid' only means the numerical oneness of God.
Correction: The first topic clarifies that tawhid also encompasses God's transcendence above all created attributes (tanzeehat).

Checkpoint

A) God is not a body
B) God does not unite with another
C) The impossibility of God being described with temporal attributes
D) On tawhid concerning transcendences

Key Takeaway
The second chapter lays the groundwork for understanding God's transcendence by denying four specific categories: physicality, substantiality, union, and temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The fourth pillar says God cannot acquire new attributes over time. This means He has always been exactly as He is—perfect, complete, unchanging. Does your trust in God waver when your circumstances change? How does His immutability demand that you anchor your hope in Him alone, not in your changing feelings or situations?

Three Proofs Against Multiplicity of the Necessary Being
The author enumerates three proofs against the existence of two necessary beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs that demonstrate the impossibility of multiple necessary beings, and how each proof approaches the issue from a distinct logical angle.

Core Idea
Three distinct logical arguments converge to prove that God's oneness is necessary and cannot admit any multiplicity.

The author presents three proofs (wujuh) against the possibility of multiple necessary beings in the context of affirming God's transcendence. These arguments approach the issue from different angles: quiddity, determination, and the relationship between necessity and determination. Together, they form a robust defense of tawhid. As we explore each proof, notice how they rely on fundamental principles of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its structure
Read the Arabic passage once to see its overall organization: a claim followed by three numbered proofs. Then read the translation to grasp the gist.
The passage begins by negating multiplicity in both parts (composition) and individuals (multiple beings).
2
Analyze the first proof: quiddity transfer
Understand how the first proof argues that two necessary beings would have identical quiddities (transfer) or distinct ones. Both possibilities lead to absurdities, such as self-precendence or contingency.
Key terms: الماهية (quiddity), التعين (determination).
3
Analyze the second proof: determination and quiddity
The second proof focuses on the determination (taʻayyun) that distinguishes two beings. If it is inseparable from the quiddity, multiplicity is impossible; if separate, the being loses necessity.
Notice how this proof relies on the definition of necessary being as independent.
4
Analyze the third proof: necessity and determination
The third proof examines all possible relationships between necessity (wujub) and determination. Each scenario leads to an impossibility, confirming that only one necessary being can exist.
This is the most detailed proof, covering cases like circularity and separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه مباحث : الفصل الثاني في التنزيهات الله تعالى (الأول) : في التوحيد الواجب لا كثرة فيه أجزاء، لأن المركب ممكن، ولا أفراد لوجوه :
الأول : لو وجد واجبان لزم نقل الماهية وإلا لكان ممكناً، يعلل إما بها فيتقدم على نفسه، ضرورة تقدم العلة بالوجوب، وإما بغيرها فلا يكون ذاتياً، فكان تمايزها يتعين، وهو ثبوتي فيه تركب الواجب .
الثاني : لو تعدد الواجب فالتعين الذي به الامتياز إما نفس الماهية الواجبة أو بها، ويلازمها فلا تعدد وينفصل فلا وجوب.
الثالث : لو تعدد فالوجوب والتعين إن جاز إهلاكها (١) لزم الوجوب بلا تعين وهو محال والتعين بلا وجوب ،وهو إمكان وإن لم يجز كان الوجوب بالتعين فيدور، أو بالعكس أو كلاهما بالذات فلا تعدد، أو ينفصل فلا وجوب).
And in it are investigations: The second chapter on the transcendences of Allah Almighty. (The first): On the tawhid (oneness) of the necessary being: there is no multiplicity in it in terms of parts, because the composite is possible, nor in terms of individuals, due to proofs:
First: If two necessary beings existed, then the quiddity would have to be transferred; otherwise it would be possible. It is caused either by it, then it would precede itself, because the cause must precede in necessity, or by something else, then it would not be essential. So their distinction would be determined, and it is positive, involving composition in the necessary being.
Second: If the necessary being were multiple, then the determination by which distinction occurs is either the same as the necessary quiddity or by it; if it is inseparable from it, then there is no multiplicity; if it is separate, then there is no necessity.
Third: If it were multiple, then necessity and determination: if their separation is possible, then necessity without determination follows, which is impossible, and determination without necessity, which is contingency; if separation is not possible, then either necessity is by determination, leading to circularity, or the reverse, or both are essential, then there is no multiplicity, or they are separate, then there is no necessity.)
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct proofs (wujuh) against the possibility of multiple necessary beings, as part of the discussion on divine transcendence (tanzeehat). The author first states the first research topic: affirming the oneness of the necessary being by negating any multiplicity in both parts (composition) and individuals (multiple necessary beings). The first proof argues that if two necessary beings existed, their quiddities would either be identical or distinct. If identical, there is no multiplicity; if distinct, the distinction must be either essential or accidental. If essential, then each is composite (having quiddity plus distinction), contradicting necessity; if accidental, then each depends on distinction, implying contingency. The second proof focuses on the determination (taʻayyun) that distinguishes two beings. If this determination is inherent to the quiddity, then it is inseparable, so there cannot be two distinct necessary beings; if it is separate, then the being would lack necessity because it depends on an external determination. The third proof examines the relationship between necessity (wujub) and determination. If they can be separated, then necessity without determination (which is impossible) or determination without necessity (contingency) results. If they cannot be separated, then either necessity is caused by determination (circular), determination by necessity (also circular), both are essential (leading to multiple essential aspects and thus multiplicity), or they are separate entities (again contradicting necessity). Each scenario leads to an impossibility, thus proving that only one necessary being can exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three proofs are independent and unrelated.
Correction: While each proof approaches the issue from a different angle, they collectively reinforce the same conclusion: multiplicity of the necessary being is logically impossible. They are complementary, not isolated.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the third proof regarding the relationship between necessity (wujub) and determination (taʻayyun)?
A) They are always inseparable in the necessary being.
B) They can be separated, leading to necessity without determination.
C) Every possible scenario leads to an impossibility, proving that only one necessary being exists.
D) Both are essential, resulting in composition.

Key Takeaway
The three proofs collectively demonstrate that the necessary being must be absolutely one, as any multiplicity leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
These logical proofs show that God must be absolutely one, with no possibility of multiplicity. Do you hold this belief with the same certainty as you hold that two plus two equals four? If not, what is one step you can take today to move from intellectual assent to heartfelt conviction?
?
The proofs reveal that any form of distinction in God would lead to dependence and contingency. In your own life, have you unknowingly treated some created thing as an independent source of good or harm, thus implicitly denying God's absolute oneness in practice? Make a specific commitment to remove that false reliance.
?
If the Necessary Being is indivisible and unique, then your entire existence is dependent on Him alone. What is one area of your life—your time, your relationships, your career—where you are acting as if there are multiple ultimate authorities? How will you realign that area to reflect God's exclusive necessity?

First Proof Against Multiplicity of the Necessary Being
Al-Taftazani presents the first proof against multiple necessary beings with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument against the possibility of multiple necessary beings, including the objection and response.

Core Idea
Shared necessity with distinct particulars would make the Necessary Being composite, which is impossible.

After establishing the impossibility of multiple necessary beings, al-Taftazani presents his first proof. He constructs a logical argument based on the concepts of commonality and distinction, anticipating and refuting objections. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the initial argument
Identify the premise: if two necessary beings, there must be commonality and distinction, leading to composition.
3
Grasp the objection
The objection claims that commonality could be accidental, not constitutive, avoiding composition.
This is a key philosophical move.
4
Follow the response
Al-Taftazani argues that necessity is identical to essence, so commonality is essential, leading to composition.
5
Appreciate the independent proof
The final part strengthens the argument, showing it can stand alone as a proof.
This shows the depth of the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على امتناع تعدد الواجب بوجوه: الأول: لو كان الواجب مشتركاً بين اثنين لكان بينهما تمايز لامتناع الاثنينية بدون التمايز وما به التمايز غير ما به الاشتراك ضرورة. فيلزم تركب كل من الواجبين مما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز وهو محال. لا يقال هذا إنما يلزم لو كان الوجوب المشترك مقوماً وهو ممنوع الجواز أن يكون عارضاً والاشتراك في العارض مع الامتياز بخصوصه لا يوجب التركب. لأنا نقول وجوب الواجب نفس ماهيته إذ لو كان عارضاً لها كان ممكناً معللاً بها إذ لو علل بغيرها لم يكن ذاتياً وإذا علل بها يلزم تقدمها على نفسه لأن العلة متقدمة على المعلول بالوجود والوجوب. وإذا كان الوجوب نفس الماهية كان الاشتراك فيها اشتراكاً في الماهية والماهية مع الخصوصية مركبة قطعاً. فإن قيل لم لا يجوز أن تكون الخصوصية من العوارض. قلنا لأنها تكون معللة بالماهية أو بما تقوم بها من الصفات وهو ينافي التعدد المفروض إذ الواجب لا يكون بدون تلك الخصوصية أو بأمر منفصل فيلزم الاحتياج المنافي للوجوب الوجود. وهذا يصلح أن يجعل دليلاً مستقلاً.
And he adduced evidence for the impossibility of multiplicity of the Necessary Being by aspects: The first: If the Necessary Being were common between two, there would be between them distinction (tamayyuz) because the impossibility of duality without distinction, and that by which distinction occurs is necessarily other than that by which commonality (ishtirak) occurs. Then it follows that each of the two necessary beings is composed of that by which commonality occurs and that by which distinction occurs, and that is impossible. It cannot be said: this only follows if the common necessity were constitutive (muqawwim), but it is not allowed that it be accidental ('arid), and commonality in the accidental with distinction by its particularity does not necessitate composition. For we say: the necessity (wujub) of the necessary being is its very essence (māhiyyah); for if it were accidental to it, it would be possible (mumkin) and caused by it, for if it were caused by something else it would not be essential, and if caused by it, there would follow its priority over itself, because the cause is prior to the effect in existence and necessity. And if necessity is the essence itself, then commonality in it is commonality in the essence, and the essence with particularity is unequivocally composed. And if it is said: why is it not allowed that the particularity be among the accidents? We say: because it would be caused by the essence, or by the attributes subsisting in it, and that contradicts the assumed multiplicity, since the Necessary Being cannot be without that particularity, or by a separate thing; then there follows neediness contradicting necessity of existence. And this is suitable to be made an independent proof.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the multiplicity of the Necessary Being. Al-Taftazani begins by stating a proof based on composition. If there are two necessary beings, they must share the property of necessity (ishtirak) and also have distinction (tamayyuz) to be two. That which distinguishes them must be different from that which they share. Thus each would be composed of a common element (necessity) and a distinguishing element. Composition is impossible for the Necessary Being because it would entail need and contingency. An objection is then raised: the commonality might be accidental, not constitutive, so composition would not follow. Al-Taftazani replies that the necessity of the Necessary Being is identical to its essence, not accidental. If it were accidental, it would be contingent and caused, leading to circularity. Since necessity is the essence itself, commonality in it is commonality in essence, and essence with particularity is necessarily composed. A further objection suggests the particularity could be accidental. The response: if so, it would be caused by the essence or its attributes, contradicting the very multiplicity assumed, because the Necessary Being cannot exist without that particularity. Alternatively, if caused by an external thing, that entails need. Thus the argument stands and can serve as an independent proof.

اشتراک
ترکیب

مقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that commonality could be accidental successfully refutes the proof.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that necessity is identical to essence for the Necessary Being, so commonality cannot be accidental.

Checkpoint
In the first proof against multiple necessary beings, why does Al-Taftazani argue that composition would result?
A) Because each necessary being would have both commonality and distinction
B) Because the necessary being must be simple
C) Because the proof assumes composition is impossible
D) Because the objection shows it is not composition

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that multiplicity of the Necessary Being would entail composition, which is impossible because the Necessary Being's necessity is identical to its essence.

?
This proof shows that neediness is incompatible with necessity. Where in your life do you cling to a sense of self-sufficiency? How does recognizing that only Allah is truly independent expose the illusion of your own independence?
?
The proof refutes the possibility of multiple necessary beings. What 'gods'—such as your career, your intellect, or your reputation—do you functionally treat as independent sources of security? Are you willing to dethrone them in your heart, affirming that Allah alone is necessary and everything else is contingent?
