Second Proof: Determination Cannot Be from an External Cause
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof based on determination of the necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the determination of the Necessary Being cannot depend on an external cause, and how this proves the impossibility of multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
The distinction between necessary beings must be internal; otherwise, they would not be self-sufficient.

This lesson presents the second proof against the multiplicity of the Necessary Being. It focuses on the concept of determination (ta'ayyun). If there were two necessary beings, each must have a distinct determination. The proof shows that this determination cannot come from an external cause, because that would undermine self-necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Note the logical flow: it begins with a conditional statement and then examines two possibilities.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the two branches of the dichotomy
The proof presents two scenarios for determination: either it is from the essence itself (or its concomitant) or from an external cause. Find these in the text and understand each consequence.
3
Understand the conclusion
Observe that if determination is internal, multiplicity is impossible; if external, necessity by essence is negated. Hence, multiplicity cannot occur. This is the proof's conclusion.
This shows that the Necessary Being must be unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن يقال: لو تعدد الواجب فالتعين الذي به الامتياز إن كان نفس الماهية الواجبة أو معللاً بها أو بلازمها فلا تعدد وإن كان معللاً بأمر منفصل فلا وجوب بالذات لامتناع احتياج الواجب في تعينه إلى أمر منفصل فلهذا جعل في المتن دليلاً ثانياً.
That it is said: If the Necessary Being were multiple, then the determination (ta'ayyun) by which distinction occurs: if it is the essence itself of the necessary being, or caused by it or by its necessary concomitant, then there is no multiplicity; and if it is caused by a separate thing, then there is no necessity by essence due to the impossibility of the Necessary Being needing in its determination a separate thing. For this reason it was made in the text a second proof.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is a concise argument based on determination (ta'ayyun). Al-Taftazani states that if there were two necessary beings, each must have a determination that distinguishes it. This determination must either be identical to the necessary being's essence, caused by the essence, or caused by its necessary concomitant—in which case there would be no actual multiplicity, because the distinction is not separate. Or the determination could be caused by an external thing. But if it is caused externally, then the necessary being would depend on something else for its determination, contradicting its self-sufficiency. Therefore, multiplicity is impossible. This argument is considered an independent proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think determination is an accidental attribute that does not affect the necessity of the being.
Correction: The proof shows that if determination were caused externally, the being would depend on that cause, negating its necessity by essence.

Checkpoint

B) It would not be necessary by essence
C) Multiplicity is still possible
D) Determination would be part of the essence

Key Takeaway
The determination of a necessary being cannot be caused externally, because that would imply dependence and negate necessity.

Third Proof: Impossibility of Multiple Necessary Beings
Al-Taftazani presents a proof by cases against multiple necessary beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relationship between necessity and determination logically precludes the existence of more than one necessary being.

Core Idea
Necessity and determination are inseparable; one cannot have necessity without determination.

This lesson unpacks the third and most intricate proof from al-Taftazani. He examines whether necessity (wujub) and determination (ta'ayyun) are necessarily connected or not. By tracing all logical possibilities, he shows that each leads to contradiction, confirming that only one necessary being can exist. Follow the reasoning carefully: it is a model of rigorous kalam argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption and question
Begin by reading the Arabic passage with its translation. The proof starts by assuming more than one necessary being, then asks whether necessity and determination are necessarily connected.
Notice the key terms: wujub (necessity), imkan (possibility), ta'ayyun (determination).
2
Analyze the first case: no connection
If there is no necessary connection, necessity could exist without determination (impossible) or determination without necessity (makes the necessary being possible). Both are absurd.
3
Analyze the second case: connection exists
If there is a connection, three subcases arise: necessity through determination (circularity and dependence), determination through necessity or both essential (determination becomes inseparable, contradicting multiplicity), or both from an external cause (loss of necessity by essence). Each is impossible.
Focus on the logical contradictions in each subcase.
4
Synthesize the overall conclusion
Since all possibilities lead to contradiction, the initial assumption of multiple necessary beings is false. Thus, the necessary being is necessarily one.
This proof complements the earlier two proofs (not covered here) reinforcing tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: لو كان الواجب أكثر من واحد لكان لكل منهما تعين وهوية ضرورة وحينئذ إما أن يكون بين الوجوب والإمكان والتعين لزوم أولا، فإن لم يكن جاز انفكاكهما لزم جواز الوجوب بدون التعين وهو محال لأن كل موجود متعين، وجواز التعين بدون الوجوب وهو منافي لكون الوجوب ذاتياً، بل يستلزم كون الواجب ممكناً حيث تعين بلا وجوب وإن كان بين الوجوب والتعين لزوم، فإن كان الوجوب بالتعين لزم تقدم الوجوب على نفسه ضرورة تقدم العلة على المعلول بالوجود والوجوب مع محال آخر وهو كون الوجوب الذاتي بالغير إن جعل التعين زائداً، وإن كان التعين بالوجوب أو كلاهما بالذات لزم خلاف المفروض وهو تعدد الواجب لأن تعين المعلول لازم غير متخلف فلا يوجد الواجب بدونه، وإن كان التعين والوجود بأمر منفصل لم يكن الواجب واجباً بالذات لاستحالة احتياجه في الوجوب والتعين بل في أحدهما إلى أمر منفصل وهو ظاهر.
Third: If the Necessary Being were more than one, then each of them would necessarily have determination (ta'ayyun) and identity. Then either there is a necessary connection (luzūm) between necessity (wujūb) and possibility (imkān) and determination, or not. If not, then it would be possible for them to separate, leading to the possibility of necessity without determination — which is impossible because every existent is determined — and the possibility of determination without necessity, which contradicts the necessity being essential, rather it entails that the necessary being is possible, since it is determined without necessity. And if there is a connection between necessity and determination: if necessity is through determination, then there would be priority of necessity over itself due to the priority of the cause over the effect in existence and necessity, along with another impossibility, namely that essential necessity would be through another, if determination is considered extra. And if determination is through necessity, or both are by essence, then it would contradict the assumption, namely the multiplicity of the necessary being, because the determination of the effect is a necessary concomitant that is not absent, so the necessary being would not exist without it. And if determination and existence are through a separate thing, then the necessary being would not be necessary by essence because of the impossibility of its need in necessity and determination, or in one of them, for a separate thing — this is evident.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This is the third and most elaborate proof. Al-Taftazani examines the relationship between necessity (wujub), possibility, and determination. He considers two cases: either there is a necessary connection between them or not. If there is no connection, then it would be possible for necessity to exist without determination, which is impossible because every existent is determined. Also, determination could exist without necessity, which would make the necessary being possible. If there is a connection, three subcases arise. If necessity is caused by determination, then necessity would precede itself (circularity) and essential necessity would depend on another. If determination is caused by necessity, or both are essential, then the determination of the effect is necessary and inseparable, so the necessary being would not exist without it, implying multiple distinct necessary beings would depend on their determinations, which contradicts the assumption that there are multiple. If both determination and existence depend on an external cause, then the supposed necessary being would not be necessary by essence because it needs that cause. Thus all possibilities lead to contradiction, proving multiplicity is impossible.

Key Terms
تعین
وجوب
امكان
لزوم
انفكاك
علة
معلول
وجوب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple necessary beings could coexist if each is necessary but distinct in determination.
Correction: The proof shows that determination and necessity are inseparably linked. If multiple existed, each would have a unique determination that is necessary for its existence, implying dependence and thus contradicting necessity by essence.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what does the subcase where 'determination is through necessity or both are by essence' lead to?
A) Circularity of necessity
B) The necessary being being dependent on an external cause
C) Contradiction of the assumption of multiple necessary beings
D) Possibility of necessity without determination

Key Takeaway
The necessary connection between necessity and determination proves that only one necessary being can exist, as any multiplicity leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that multiplicity leads to contradiction. Is there any area of your life where you are trying to serve two masters, thinking both can be ultimate? What must you do to resolve that contradiction?
?
Do you live as though only Allah is truly necessary, or do your actions suggest you treat other things as necessary alongside Him? What specific step will you take today to align your heart with this truth?

The Third Proof: Logical Impossibility of Multiple Necessary Beings
Sanusi presents a dilemma argument against multiplicity using necessity and specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third kalam argument for the oneness of God, which demonstrates that multiplicity of necessary beings is impossible due to the necessary connection between necessity and specification.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of the Necessary Being leads to absurdity because necessity and specification are inseparably linked.

This lesson presents the third proof against multiplicity from Sharh al-Maqasid. The argument uses a logical dilemma to show that if there were two necessary beings, their relationship with necessity and specification would lead to contradictions. Understanding this argument strengthens the Islamic doctrine of tawheed by demonstrating that only one Necessary Being can exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument's structure.
The argument is dense; focus on understanding the overall flow before diving into details.
2
Identify the initial conditional
The argument begins with 'If the Necessary Being were more than one, each would have specification and identity.' Recognize this as the starting assumption that leads to a dilemma.
Specification (التعين) here means the distinct identity that makes one being different from another.
3
Follow the first branch: no necessary connection
The first option is that there is no necessary connection between necessity and specification. Consider the absurd consequences: necessity without specification (impossible) and specification without necessity (makes the being contingent).
This branch shows that necessity and specification cannot be independent.
4
Follow the second branch: necessary connection
Now assume there is a necessary connection. Examine the four sub-branches about which depends on which. Each leads to a contradiction or makes the necessary being dependent.
The key is to see that every possible relationship leads to an absurdity.
5
Conclude the impossibility of multiplicity
Since all possibilities lead to absurdity, the original assumption of multiple necessary beings must be false. Only one Necessary Being can exist.
This argument is a classic kalam proof for tawheed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الواجب أكثر من واحد لكان لكل منهما تعين وهوية ضرورة وحينئذ إما (۲) أن يكون بين الوجوب والإمكان (۳) والتعين لزوم أولا ، فإن لم يكن جاز انفكاكهما لزم جواز الوجوب بدون التعين وهو محال لأن كل موجود متعين، وجواز التعين بدون الوجوب وهو ينا في كون الوجوب ذاتياً، بل يستلزم كون الواجب ممكناً، حيث تعين بلا وجوب وإن كان بين الوجوب والتعين لزوم، فإن كان الوجوب بالتعين لزم تقدم الوجوب على نفسه ضرورة تقدم العلة على المعلول بالوجود والوجوب مع محال آخر، وهو كون الوجوب الذاتي بالغير، إن جعل التعين زائداً، وإن كان التعين بالوجوب أو كلاهما بالذات لزم خلاف المفروض، وهو تعدد الواجب، لأن تعين المعلول لازم غير متخلف فلا يوجد الواجب بدونه، وإن كان التعين والوجود بأمر منفصل، لم يكن الواجب واجباً بالذات لاستحالة احتياجه في الوجوب والتعين بل في أحدهما إلى أمر منفصل وهو ظاهر.
The third: If the Necessary Being were more than one, each would have specification and identity necessarily. Then, either there is a necessary connection (2) between necessity, contingency (3), and specification or not. If not, and it is possible for them to separate, then it would necessitate the possibility of necessity without specification, which is impossible because every existent is specified; and the possibility of specification without necessity, which contradicts [the fact] that necessity is essential; rather, it implies that the necessary being is contingent, since it is specified without necessity. And if there is a necessary connection between necessity and specification, then if necessity is by specification, it would necessitate the priority of necessity over itself by virtue of the priority of cause over effect in existence, and another absurdity, namely that the essential necessity is by another, if specification is considered additional. And if specification is by necessity, or both are by essence, it would contradict the hypothesis, which is the multiplicity of the necessary being, because the specification of the effect is a non-deferrable concomitant, so the necessary being cannot exist without it. And if specification and existence are by a separate thing, then the necessary being is not necessary by essence, due to the impossibility of its need for a separate thing in necessity and specification, or even in one of them, and this is clear.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the third argument) for the oneness of God (tawheed). The author sets up a dilemma regarding the relationship between necessity (الوجوب), contingency (الإمكان), and specification (التعين) for a supposed multiplicity of necessary beings. He first considers whether there is a necessary connection (لزوم) between necessity and specification. If there is no connection, then it would be possible to have necessity without specification (impossible) or specification without necessity (which would make the necessary being contingent). If there is a connection, then he examines the direction: if necessity depends on specification, that leads to circularity (priority of necessity over itself) and makes necessary essence dependent on another; if specification depends on necessity, then the specification of the effect is necessary and cannot be absent, so only one can have that specification; if both are essential, then each would have its own specification, contradicting the hypothesis of unity. The final option is that specification and existence come from an external cause, which would make the necessary being dependent and thus not necessary by essence. All possibilities lead to absurdity, proving that multiplicity is impossible.

Key Terms
الواجب
التعين
الوجوب
الإمكان
اللزوم
المعلول
العلة
الذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument assumes that specification is an attribute, but it actually deals with the logical necessity of distinct identity.
Correction: Specification here refers to the necessary distinctness of any existent; without it, two beings would be indistinguishable and thus identical.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility examined regarding the relationship between necessity and specification?
A) There is a necessary connection between them.
B) There is no necessary connection between them.
C) They are identical.
D) They are mutually exclusive.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that the very concept of a necessary being requires uniqueness; any multiplicity leads to logical contradictions.

Are there 'necessary beings' in your life—things you treat as indispensable—that contradict this truth? What will you do about them this week?
?
This proof shows that multiplicity leads to logical absurdity. When you consider your own life, where have you allowed a mental 'multiplicity'—dividing your devotion or fear between Allah and created things? How does this argument expose that as not just wrong but rationally impossible? Will you let this truth reshape your attachments?
?
The argument hinges on the connection between necessity and specification. Do you live as if Allah's necessity is the only thing that specifies your identity? Or do you let other specifications—your job, your status, your relationships—define who you are, as if they were necessary for your existence? What would it mean to let go of those false 'necessary' specifications today?

The Fourth Proof: Argument from Shared Objects of Power
Presents the fourth kalam argument against two gods using a single object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' argument that a single object of power cannot be intended by two gods, leading to the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot both intend the same thing without logical contradiction.

The theologians developed several arguments to prove God's oneness. One powerful argument focuses on a single object of power that two gods might both intend. The argument explores three possibilities and shows that each leads to an impossibility, thereby proving that multiple gods cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a heading introducing the theologians' approach.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage presents three possibilities for what happens when a single object of power is intended by two gods. Find each one and notice how each leads to a contradiction.
The first possibility is unclear ('as it should be for inference'), but the second and third are clearer.
3
Understand preponderance without a preponderator
Focus on the third possibility: only one god produces the object. Why does this lead to preponderance without a preponderator? Because both gods are equally omnipotent and the object is equally possible for both, so there is no reason for one to create it rather than the other.
This is a key logical principle in kalam.
4
Reflect on the premises
The passage ends by stating that omnipotence is essential and being an object of power is contingent. This means no inherent feature gives one god priority over another, strengthening the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرق المتكلمين في نفي التعدد والكثرة
الرابع) : لو وجد إلهان فوقع المقدور الذي قصداه . إما أن يكون بما ينبغي أن يكون للاستدلال أو بكل منهما فيلزم مقدورين قادرين . أو بأحدهما فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح لأن نسبة المقدورات إليهما على السواء. لأن القادرية بالذات والمقدورية بالإمكان .
The ways of the theologians in negating multiplicity and plurality.
The fourth: If two gods existed, and the intended object of power occurred, either it occurs as it should be for inference, or by both, then it necessitates two omnipotent beings; or by one of them, then it necessitates preponderance without a preponderator, because the relation of the objects of power to both is equal. Because omnipotence is essential and being an object of power is contingent.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the fourth argument from the theologians to refute the possibility of two gods. It begins with a heading: 'The ways of the theologians in negating multiplicity and plurality.' The argument focuses on a single object of power (مقدور) that both gods intend. There are three possibilities: (1) the object occurs 'as it should be for inference' (this phrase is ambiguous; possibly referring to a natural or logical manner), (2) it occurs by both gods together, which would imply two omnipotent beings causing the same effect (problematic), (3) it occurs by only one god. The third option leads to preponderance without a preponderator (ترجيح بلا مرجح) because both gods have equal essential omnipotence, and the object is equally possible for both. There is no reason for one to create it rather than the other. Thus multiplicity is impossible. The argument relies on the premise that omnipotence (القادرية) is essential (بالذات) and being an object of power (المقدورية) is contingent (بالإمكان), so no inherent distinction gives priority.

Key Terms
التعدد
الكثرة
المقدور
الترجيح بلا مرجح
القادرية
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument assumes that two gods would necessarily conflict or disagree about the object.
Correction: The argument does not assume conflict; it examines the logical consequences even if both gods intend the same object. The impossibility arises from the lack of a reason for one god to act rather than the other.

Checkpoint
What follows if only one god produces the object of power in the fourth argument?
A) Both gods are omnipotent.
B) It necessitates preponderance without a preponderator.
C) The object is impossible.
D) The other god is incapable.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods is demonstrated by the logical consequences of a shared object of power: any attempt to assign the act to one god leads to preponderance without a preponderator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you sometimes act as if there are multiple forces determining the outcome, even though you profess belief in one God? How does this argument confront that tendency?
?
If you truly believe that only one God has essential omnipotence, why do you still fear or rely on created things as if they have independent power? What would change if you internalized the logical impossibility of any other power?
?
This argument shows that any supposed second god would be either redundant or powerless. In your life, what 'second gods'—like wealth, status, or people—do you treat as if they have ultimate control? Are you willing to remove them from that position today?

The Fifth Proof: Argument from Conflicting Wills
Sanusi presents the fifth argument against multiple gods via conflicting wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical impossibility of multiple gods based on conflicting wills and the inevitability of incapability.

Core Idea
Two omnipotent gods with conflicting wills lead to logical impossibility.

This passage presents the fifth argument against polytheism. It considers two gods who will contradictory things and examines all possible outcomes. The analysis shows that any scenario inevitably leads to incapability in at least one god, which contradicts the nature of a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: it presents a conditional argument with two main branches.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the two branches of the argument
The first branch considers the possibility that the second god cannot will the opposite; the second branch considers that he can. Each leads to a problem.
3
Analyze the three outcomes under the second branch
If the second god can will the opposite, then either both wills occur (impossible), neither occurs (both incapable), or only one occurs (preponderance without a preponderator). Notice how each outcome proves incapability.
This is the heart of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إذا أراد أحدهما أمراً فإن لم يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده فعاجز . إذ لا مانع سوى تعلق قدرة الأول . وإن تمكن لزم من فرض وقوعها إما وقوع الضدين وهو محال أو لا وقوعهما . وهو عجز لهما مع الاستحالة في مثل حركة جسم وسكونه أو وقوع أحدهما فقط وهو ترجيح بلا مرجح مع عجز من لم يقع مراده .
The fifth: If one of them wills a thing, then if the other is not able to will its opposite, then he is incapable, since there is no obstacle except the attachment of the power of the first. And if he is able, then from the assumption of its occurrence follows either the occurrence of two opposites, which is impossible, or the non-occurrence of both, which is incapability for both, along with the impossibility in the case of motion and rest of a body, or the occurrence of only one, which is preponderance without a preponderator along with the incapability of the one whose will did not occur.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the fifth argument against two gods, focusing on conflicting wills. If one god wills something (e.g., a body to move), the other could will its opposite (e.g., rest). Two cases: either the second cannot will the opposite (then he is incapable, contradicting omnipotence) or he can. If he can, we consider the actual outcome of their wills. There are three scenarios: (1) both opposites occur together—impossible; (2) neither occurs—both are incapable; (3) only one occurs—this involves preponderance without a preponderator (since the gods are equal) and the other god's will is thwarted, making him incapable. In all possibilities, at least one god is shown to be incapable, which contradicts the definition of a necessary being. The example of motion and rest of a body illustrates the impossibility of simultaneous opposites.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الضد
القدرة
العجز
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if the gods are in conflict; if they cooperate, multiple gods could exist.
Correction: The argument does not require actual conflict; it demonstrates that any difference in will or lack of coordination leads to logical impossibility, because the mere possibility of conflicting wills implies incapability in at least one god.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if both gods are able to will opposites and both wills are actualized?
A) Both opposites occur, which is impossible.
B) Neither occurs, so both are incapable.
C) Only one occurs, showing preponderance.
D) They reconcile and produce a third state.

Key Takeaway
The argument from conflicting wills shows that multiple gods cannot coexist because any conflict leads to incapability in at least one of them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah is omnipotent, why do you sometimes place your ultimate trust in your own plans or in other people, as if they have independent power?
?
This proof shows that any conflict between two gods leads to logical absurdity. In your life, when you experience inner conflict or hesitation about obeying Allah, are you not effectively acting as if there is a second 'will' competing with His? How can you train your heart to submit entirely to His will as the only one that truly prevails?
?
The proof concludes that only one God can exist. Does your daily life reflect this certainty? Where do you find yourself attributing 'power' to causes other than Allah, and what step will you take this week to redirect your trust back to Him alone?

The Sixth Proof: Tawārud and Tamānu‘
Presents the sixth argument using concurrence and mutual prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma posed by the possibility of two gods either agreeing or disagreeing on an object of power.

Core Idea
Two gods lead to either concurrence or conflict, both impossible.

This lesson presents the sixth proof for the oneness of God, using the concepts of tawārud (concurrence) and tamānu‘ (mutual prevention). The argument shows that whether two hypothetical gods agree or disagree on a single created act, an absurdity follows, proving that only one God can exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the Concise Passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Notice the two conditional scenarios introduced by 'if they agree' and 'if not'.
The passage is extremely concise; each term carries deep theological weight.
2
Understand Tawārud (التوارد)
Tawārud means 'concurrence' — two independent agents both being the direct cause of the same effect, which leads to a logical contradiction because each would claim exclusive causation.
It does not mean cooperation; rather, it points to a problem in attributing a single effect to two independent causes.
3
Understand Tamānu‘ (التمانع)
Tamānu‘ means 'mutual prevention' — if the two gods disagree, each would prevent the other from causing its effect, resulting in a deadlock that proves their inability.
This shows that disagreement implies weakness, which is impossible for a true deity.
4
Analyze the Logical Structure
The argument presents a dilemma: if the two gods agree on an object of power, tawārud arises (impossible); if they disagree, tamānu‘ arises (also impossible). Both outcomes are absurd, so the assumption of two gods is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument for divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن اتفقا على مقدور لزم التوارد وإلا فالتمانع .
The sixth: If they agree upon an object of power, then concurrence (tawārud) is necessary, and if not, then mutual prevention (tamānu‘).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the sixth argument, presented very concisely. It introduces two technical terms in kalam: التوارد (tawārud) and التمانع (tamānu‘). 'Tawārud' refers to two independent agents both being the cause of the same effect, which is problematic because each would claim the effect exclusively, leading to contradiction. 'Tamānu‘' refers to each agent preventing the other from causing its effect, which implies mutual obstruction and incapability. Thus, whether the two hypothetical gods agree on an object of power or disagree, an absurdity follows. If they agree, tawārud arises; if they disagree, tamānu‘ arises. Either way, the existence of two omnipotent beings is impossible.

Key Terms
التوارد
التمانع
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawārud means cooperation or that two gods could work together harmoniously.
Correction: In kalam, tawārud specifically refers to the logical problem of two independent agents both being the sole cause of the same effect, leading to a contradiction. It does not imply cooperation but rather impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the technical term for two agents both causing the same effect?
A) التمانع
B) التوارد
C) الترجيح
D) الإمكان

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of both tawārud and tamānu‘ forces the acceptance of a single divine creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever catch yourself attributing outcomes to multiple 'causes'—luck, your own effort, or other people—while claiming to believe in one all-powerful Creator? How does the concept of tawārud challenge you to see His exclusive hand in every event?
?
Is there a conflict within you—a tamānu‘—where you try to serve both God and your own desires, thinking you can balance them? What would it mean to surrender that struggle and acknowledge that only one will should rule your life?

The Seventh Proof: Argument from Differentiation
Presents the seventh argument based on the logical dilemma of differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that undermines any claim of multiple distinct gods by analyzing the nature of their differentiation.

Core Idea
Any supposed differentiator between gods either fails to distinguish or is dispensable, collapsing duality.

The seventh proof in the series demonstrates that the very idea of multiple distinct gods is self-defeating. It examines what would make two gods different, and shows that any candidate for differentiation leads to contradiction or redundancy. Here, the author presents a concise logical dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the terse, logical style. The passage is a single sentence containing a conditional structure.
The phrase 'ما به التمايز' means 'that by which they are differentiated'.
2
Understand the two cases
Identify the two possible scenarios: either the differentiating feature is a necessary concomitant of divinity, or it is not. Consider the implication of each case.
A necessary concomitant is an inseparable attribute of divinity.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the logic: In the first case, the feature cannot distinguish because both gods would share it. In the second, the feature can be removed, making the gods identical and hence one. Both paths lead to the elimination of duality.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum against polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : ما به التمايز إن كان من لوازم الألوهية فباطل وإلا فيمكن ارتفاعه فترتفع الاثنينية .
The seventh: That by which they are differentiated, if it is from the necessary concomitants of divinity, then it is false; otherwise, its removal is possible, and then duality is removed.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the seventh argument against multiple gods. It focuses on the criterion of differentiation (التمايز) between two hypothetical gods. The argument considers whether the distinguishing feature is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of divinity (الألوهية). If it is a necessary concomitant, then both gods must possess it, so it cannot be a distinguishing feature (it would be common to both, making them identical). If it is not a necessary concomitant, then it is possible for that feature to be absent; if it is absent, the two gods become identical and thus one, removing the duality. In either case, multiplicity collapses. Therefore, there cannot be two distinct gods.

Key Terms
التمايز
لوازم الألوهية
الاثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes gods must have different attributes to be distinct.
Correction: The argument does not assume that; it shows that any possible differentiator leads to unity, so distinction is impossible.

Checkpoint
What happens if the differentiating feature is not a necessary concomitant of divinity?
A) It is impossible for it to be absent.
B) Its removal is possible, leading to the removal of duality.
C) The gods become more distinct.
D) The feature becomes essential.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that any proposed differentiating feature between two gods either cannot actually differentiate them or is eliminable, leading to the necessary conclusion of divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the logical necessity of God's oneness, do you truly internalize that any other belief is rationally impossible, or do you still harbor vague doubts? What step in this argument has the most power to uproot those doubts from your heart?
?
If the differentiator between two hypothetical gods must be either essential or accidental, and both options lead to the same unity, what does this say about the absolute simplicity of the true God? How does this press you to reject not just polytheism but also any subtle associations in your life?
?
This argument eliminates any possibility of multiplicity in divinity. Are you willing to let it eliminate multiplicity in your loyalties? Is there any area of your life where you act as if there is more than one ultimate authority?

The Eighth Proof: Argument from Absence of Evidence
Sanusi presents the eighth argument: from absence of evidence to necessary negation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absence of proof for a second god necessitates its negation and why this is not an argument from ignorance.

Core Idea
No proof means no claim — the rational necessity of evidence.

Sanusi presents a concise but powerful argument: if there is no evidence for the existence of a second god, then that claim must be rejected. This is not a fallacious argument from ignorance but a fundamental principle of rational inquiry, especially in matters of divinity. Let's examine this brief passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the translation. Notice how the argument is structured in a single, tight logical step.
The Arabic uses three key terms: دليل (proof), نفي (negation), محالات (absurdities).
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the premise (no proof), the conclusion (negation is obligatory), and the consequence (otherwise absurdities). This is a conditional argument.
The footnote indicates a variant reading: محال (singular) instead of محالات (plural), but the meaning remains.
3
Reflect on the principle
Why is this not an argument from ignorance? Because it relies on the necessity of evidence for any claim about existence, not on a mere lack of proof.
This argument is foundational in kalam for establishing divine uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : لا دليل على الثاني فيجب نفيه وإلا لزم محالات (۱) .
The eighth: There is no proof for the second, so its negation is obligatory; otherwise, absurdities would follow (1).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the eighth argument, a brief but powerful principle: there is no proof (دليل) for the existence of a second god. Therefore, we must negate (نفي) it. If we do not negate it, we would be accepting something without evidence, which leads to absurdities (محالات). This argument relies on the principle that claims without evidence are invalid, especially concerning matters of divinity. The footnote indicates a textual variant where 'محالات' (plural) may be read as 'محال' (singular), but the meaning remains that absurdities result from affirming the second without proof.

Key Terms
الدليل
النفي
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is just an argument from ignorance (appeal to ignorance).
Correction: It is not an argument from ignorance because it does not claim that the lack of proof proves nonexistence; rather, it asserts that without proof, affirmation is irrational, and negation is rationally obligatory to avoid absurdity. This is a foundational principle in rational theology.

Checkpoint
What must be done according to the eighth argument because there is no proof for a second god?
A) Accept its possibility.
B) Negate it.
C) Seek more proof.
D) Assume it is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The absence of evidence for a claim about existence, especially in divinity, rationally necessitates its negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever internally affirmed something about God without a clear proof, just because it felt comfortable? What does this passage demand from you regarding that belief?
?
Your claim that 'there is no god but Allah'—do you have proof as solid as this argument? Or are you relying on imitation? What would it mean for you to truly negate every unproven claim about God?
?
When you encounter a claim about religion that has no evidence, do you suspend judgment or do you actively negate it? What is the rational obligation of negation asking you to do today in your faith journey?

The Ninth Proof: Argument from Infinite Regress
Presents the ninth argument: multiplicity would lead to an unlimited chain without priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth proof for the oneness of the Necessary Being: that multiplicity would result in an unlimited chain due to the absence of a priority principle.

Core Idea
Without a limiting principle, multiplicity leads to an infinite regress.

This lesson presents the ninth argument from the Sharh al-Maqasid against the possibility of multiple necessary beings. It is a concise but powerful argument that relies on the concept of priority to show that any multiplicity would be unlimited. Let us read the original text and unpack its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice that the Arabic is very short and dense.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the three key terms: التعدد, التناهي, الأولوية. Understand their meanings and how they relate.
التعدد means multiplicity, التناهي means being limited/finite, الأولوية means priority.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The argument has a conditional statement: if multiplicity, then non-limitedness, because of no priority. Explain this in your own words.
This is a simple cause-effect reasoning.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this argument support the uniqueness of the Necessary Being? Think about what would happen if there were two or more necessary beings.
The conclusion is that the Necessary Being must be one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : لو تعدد لم يتناه إذ لا أولوية .
The ninth: If it were multiple, it would not be limited, as there is no priority.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the ninth argument against multiple necessary beings. It argues that if there were more than one necessary being, there would be no principle to limit the number to two or any finite number. Since each would be equally necessary and independent, there is no priority (أولوية) among them that would determine a specific count. Therefore, an infinite regress would follow, which is absurd. This is a common kalam argument that a necessary being must be unique, otherwise a vicious infinite series would be required.

Key Terms
التعدد
التناهي
الأولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is against an actual infinite existing.
Correction: The argument is against an unordered multiplicity without a limiting principle; it assumes that if there were multiple necessary beings, they would be equally independent, leading to an unlimited chain without a reason to stop at any finite number.

Checkpoint
Why would multiplicity not be limited according to the ninth argument?
A) Because each god is finite.
B) Because there is no priority among them.
C) Because they are all the same.
D) Because of divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The ninth proof demonstrates that multiplicity among necessary beings would lead to an unlimited regress, because without a priority principle, there is no reason to stop at any finite number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does your heart rely on multiple sources of hope and security besides Allah, implicitly denying the logical necessity of His absolute oneness? What would it take for you to truly prioritize Him above all else?
?
If you believe that no priority exists among multiple necessary beings, how does that challenge your tendency to see some created things as equally 'necessary' in your daily life? Will you commit to tracing every need back to the One who alone has no need of anything?

The Tenth Proof: Scriptural Evidence and Acknowledged Weakness
Presents the tenth argument: scriptural proofs from consensus and definitive texts, with a note on weaknesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of scriptural evidence (consensus and definitive texts) in establishing tawheed, and why the author candidly admits the weakness of some rational arguments.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs, though necessary, are accompanied by an honest admission of rational shortcomings.

The final argument in this series shifts from pure reason to revelation. The author cites consensus (ijma') and definitive texts (nusus qat'iyya) as supporting tawheed. But he also acknowledges that some earlier rational arguments have weaknesses, showing scholarly integrity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this is the tenth argument, concluding the series.
2
Identify the two types of scriptural evidence
The passage mentions 'الإجماع' (consensus) and 'النصوص القطعية' (definitive texts). Understand what these are and how they support tawheed.
3
Reflect on the admission of weakness
The author says 'وفي بعض ما سبق ضعف لا يخفى' (and some of the previous have a weakness that is not hidden). Consider why he includes this candid note.
This shows that not all rational arguments are equally strong, but the cumulative case stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : الأدلة السمعية من الإجماع والنصوص القطعية وفي بعض ما سبق ضعف لا يخفى).
The tenth: The scriptural proofs from consensus and definitive texts; and some of the previous have a weakness that is not hidden.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is the tenth and final argument in this series. It points to the scriptural (سمعية) proofs, namely consensus (الإجماع) and definitive texts (النصوص القطعية) such as Quranic verses, which establish the oneness of God. The author also acknowledges that some of the preceding rational arguments have a weakness (ضعف) that is not hidden (لا يخفى). This may be a candid admission that not all rational proofs are equally strong, but the cumulative case and the scriptural evidence are sufficient. The argument serves as a reminder that revelation also affirms tawheed, and that rational arguments, though valuable, may have limitations.

Key Terms
الأدلة السمعية
الإجماع
النصوص القطعية
الضعف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's admission of weakness means the entire case for tawheed is weak.
Correction: The author acknowledges that some rational arguments have limitations, but the scriptural evidence provides a solid foundation. The admission shows scholarly honesty, not a denial of truth.

Checkpoint
What types of scriptural evidence does the tenth argument cite?
A) Analogical reasoning and intuition.
B) Consensus and definitive texts.
C) Philosophical speculation and mysticism.
D) Personal experience and tradition.

Key Takeaway
The tenth argument for tawheed relies on scriptural proofs (consensus and definitive texts) and honestly notes some weaknesses in earlier rational arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author honestly admits that some rational arguments have weakness. Are you willing to admit when your own reasoning about faith is weak, or do you cling to arguments you know are flawed? What would it look like to humbly submit to the definitive texts instead?
?
You have now seen that scriptural proofs—consensus and definitive texts—are the final authority for tawheed. When your desires or doubts conflict with these texts, do you adjust your life to them or try to twist them? Ask yourself honestly: what is the ultimate authority in your heart?
?
If some of the previous arguments for God's existence had weaknesses, what does that say about depending solely on your own intellect? Does this lesson challenge you to rely more on revelation and less on your own reasoning? Commit to one concrete step to shift that balance today.

Fourth Argument: Preponderance Without a Preponderator
Al-Taftazani presents the fourth argument against multiple gods: the problem of preponderance without a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two equal gods logically necessitates an impossible preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
Two equal gods cannot both cause the same act without a reason for one to prevail.

This argument tackles the scenario where two gods both intend the same action. Since they are equal in power, any outcome would be arbitrary unless one has a preponderating factor. Al-Taftazani shows this leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the either/or structure.
2
Identify the impossibility
The argument says if both gods cause the act, it becomes a single act by two agents (impossible). If one causes it, there must be a reason why that one rather than the other.
This is the principle of preponderance without a preponderator
3
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect: if both are truly equal, why would one act rather than the other? This shows the need for a single, unique necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرق المتكلمين في نفي التعدد والكثرة
الرابع: لو وجد إلهان فوقع المقدور الذي قصداه. إما أن يكون بما ينبغي أن يكون للاستدلال أو بكل منهما فيلزم مقدورين قادرين. أو بأحدهما فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح لأن نسبة المقدورات إليهما على السواء. لأن القادرية بالذات والمقدورية بالإمكان.
الخامس: إذا أراد أحدهما أمراً فإن لم يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده فعاجز. إذ لا مانع سوى تعلق قدرة الأول. وإن تمكن لزم من فرض وقوعها إما وقوع الضدين وهو محال أو لا وقوعهما. وهو عجز لهما مع الاستحالة في مثل حركة جسم وسكونه أو وقوع أحدهما فقط وهو ترجيح بلا مرجح مع عجز من لم يقع مراده.
السادس: إن اتفقا على مقدور لزم التوارد وإلا فالتمانع.
السابع: ما به التمايز إن كان من لوازم الألوهية فباطل وإلا فيمكن ارتفاعه فترتفع الاثنينية.
الثامن: لا دليل على الثاني فيجب نفيه وإلا لزم محالات.
التاسع: لو تعدد لم يتناه إذ لا أولوية.
العاشر: الأدلة السمعية من الإجماع والنصوص القطعية وفي بعض ما سبق ضعف لا يخفى.
The ways of the theologians in negating multiplicity and plurality.
Fourth: If there were two gods and the intended action occurred. Either it must be as it ought to be for inference, or by both of them, necessitating [an act] between two capable beings; or by one of them, necessitating preponderance without a preponderator, because the relationship of possible acts to them is equal. For capability is by essence and possibility is by [the thing's] possibility.
Fifth: If one of them willed a thing, then if the other is unable to will its opposite, he is incapable, since there is no obstacle except the attachment of the first's power. But if he is able, then from the assumption of its occurrence either the occurrence of the two opposites follows, which is impossible, or their non-occurrence, which is an incapacity for both, along with impossibility in such a case as the motion of a body and its rest; or the occurrence of only one of them, which is a preponderance without a preponderator, along with the incapacity of the one whose will did not occur.
Sixth: If they agree upon an action, coordination follows; otherwise, mutual impediment.
Seventh: What distinguishes them: if it is among the necessary concomitants of divinity, it is false; otherwise, it is possible for it to be removed, and then the duality is removed.
Eighth: There is no proof for the second [god], so its negation is necessary, otherwise impossibilities would follow.
Ninth: If there were multiple, they would not be finite, since there is no priority.
Tenth: The scriptural evidences from consensus and definitive texts, and in some of what preceded there is weakness not hidden.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage enumerates ten arguments (طرق) used by theologians to negate the possibility of multiple gods. It begins with the heading 'The ways of the theologians in negating multiplicity and plurality.' Then each argument is presented as a numbered point:

1. Fourth argument: If two gods existed, for any intended action, either it is performed by both, which would entail a single act being between two independent agents (impossible), or by one, which would require a preponderator without any preponderating factor, since both are equal in capability and the act is equally possible for both.

2. Fifth argument: If one god wills something, the other either cannot will the opposite (then he is incapable) or can. If he can, then assuming both wills occur, either both opposites occur (impossible) or neither occurs (incapacity for both, impossible), or only one occurs (preponderance without preponderator).

3. Sixth argument: If they agree on an action, it would be a single act by two agents (coordinated); otherwise, mutual impediment.

4. Seventh argument: Whatever differentiates them must be either a necessary concomitant of divinity (which would be false, as divinity is one) or accidental, in which case it can be removed, and thus duality is removed.

5. Eighth argument: There is no proof for a second god, so it must be negated, otherwise impossibilities follow.

6. Ninth argument: If there were multiple, they would be infinite, as no priority exists.

7. Tenth argument: Scriptural evidences from consensus and definitive texts also refute polytheism, though some of the earlier rational arguments have weaknesses.

Key Terms
الترجيح بلا مرجح
مقدور بين قادرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what is impossible if two gods cause the same intended action?
A) That they cooperate
B) That the action would be between two capable beings
C) That they would be equal
D) That there would be a preponderator

Key Takeaway
The existence of two equal gods leads to the impossible scenario of an action being caused by both or one arbitrarily, which proves monotheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but do your thoughts and actions reflect a recognition that only He deserves ultimate trust? Think of a recent moment when you panicked and turned to other means before prayer. What does that reveal about your heart's true belief in His sole power? Commit to turning to Him first next time.
?
This argument shows that two equal gods lead to logical absurdity. How often do you mentally create 'gods' by giving ultimate authority to money, status, or relationships? Name one such false god you need to dethrone today and write a concrete step to do so.
?
If you truly accept that nothing can happen without God's will, why do you still feel anxious about outcomes? Pick one specific worry, then consciously remind yourself that no other power can intervene without His permission. How does that change your perspective?

Fifth Argument: Conflict of Wills
Al-Taftazani presents the fifth argument against multiple gods: conflicting wills lead to impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conflicting wills between two supposed gods lead to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
If one god wills and the other cannot oppose, he is weak; if he can, chaos follows.

This argument examines a scenario where two gods have opposing wills. It shows that any outcome leads to either weakness, impossibility, or arbitrariness, thereby disproving the possibility of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the if-then structure.
2
Analyze the branches
Identify the three possibilities: the second god cannot will the opposite (incapable), both wills occur (impossible), or only one occurs (preponderance without preponderator).
3
Reflect on divine perfection
Contemplate that any deficiency (incapacity) or arbitrariness is incompatible with divinity.

Source Text

Sixth Argument: Agreement or Impediment
Al-Taftazani presents the sixth argument against multiple gods: agreement leads to coordination, disagreement leads to mutual impediment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two gods cannot coexist either in agreement or disagreement.

Core Idea
Whether they agree or disagree, the existence of two gods leads to incoherence.

This brief but powerful argument presents a dilemma: if two gods agree on an action, you have two agents on one act (coordination, which is impossible); if they disagree, they hinder each other, showing imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the short Arabic and translation. Notice it presents two options.
2
Examine 'agreement'
Understand why two agents performing the same act is impossible (it would be a single act with two independent causes).
This is the 'توارد' problem.
3
Examine 'disagreement'
Mutual impediment means each god can hinder the other, implying imperfection.

Seventh Argument: What Distinguishes Them
Al-Taftazani presents the seventh argument against multiple gods: the distinguishing feature leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinguishing factor between multiple gods cannot be essential or accidental.

Core Idea
Whatever distinguishes two gods either undermines their divinity or their duality.

This argument looks at the basis of differentiation. If two gods are distinct, there must be a distinguishing feature. If that feature is essential to divinity, then each would be different, contradicting the concept of divinity. If accidental, it can be removed, and then they become one.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. It is a short conditional argument.
2
Understand the two possibilities
The distinguishing feature is either a necessary concomitant of divinity or not.
3
Analyze each possibility
If it is a necessary concomitant, then each god would have a different essence, but divinity is one. If it is accidental, it can be removed, and duality disappears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرق المتكلمين في نفي التعدد والكثرة
الرابع: لو وجد إلهان فوقع المقدور الذي قصداه. إما أن يكون بما ينبغي أن يكون للاستدلال أو بكل منهما فيلزم مقدورين قادرين. أو بأحدهما فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح لأن نسبة المقدورات إليهما على السواء. لأن القادرية بالذات والمقدورية بالإمكان.
الخامس: إذا أراد أحدهما أمراً فإن لم يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده فعاجز. إذ لا مانع سوى تعلق قدرة الأول. وإن تمكن لزم من فرض وقوعها إما وقوع الضدين وهو محال أو لا وقوعهما. وهو عجز لهما مع الاستحالة في مثل حركة جسم وسكونه أو وقوع أحدهما فقط وهو ترجيح بلا مرجح مع عجز من لم يقع مراده.
السادس: إن اتفقا على مقدور لزم التوارد وإلا فالتمانع.
السابع: ما به التمايز إن كان من لوازم الألوهية فباطل وإلا فيمكن ارتفاعه فترتفع الاثنينية.
الثامن: لا دليل على الثاني فيجب نفيه وإلا لزم محالات.
التاسع: لو تعدد لم يتناه إذ لا أولوية.
العاشر: الأدلة السمعية من الإجماع والنصوص القطعية وفي بعض ما سبق ضعف لا يخفى.
The ways of the theologians in negating multiplicity and plurality.
Fourth: If there were two gods and the intended action occurred. Either it must be as it ought to be for inference, or by both of them, necessitating [an act] between two capable beings; or by one of them, necessitating preponderance without a preponderator, because the relationship of possible acts to them is equal. For capability is by essence and possibility is by [the thing's] possibility.
Fifth: If one of them willed a thing, then if the other is unable to will its opposite, he is incapable, since there is no obstacle except the attachment of the first's power. But if he is able, then from the assumption of its occurrence either the occurrence of the two opposites follows, which is impossible, or their non-occurrence, which is an incapacity for both, along with impossibility in such a case as the motion of a body and its rest; or the occurrence of only one of them, which is a preponderance without a preponderator, along with the incapacity of the one whose will did not occur.
Sixth: If they agree upon an action, coordination follows; otherwise, mutual impediment.
Seventh: What distinguishes them: if it is among the necessary concomitants of divinity, it is false; otherwise, it is possible for it to be removed, and then the duality is removed.
Eighth: There is no proof for the second [god], so its negation is necessary, otherwise impossibilities would follow.
Ninth: If there were multiple, they would not be finite, since there is no priority.
Tenth: The scriptural evidences from consensus and definitive texts, and in some of what preceded there is weakness not hidden.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage enumerates ten arguments (طرق) used by theologians to negate the possibility of multiple gods. It begins with the heading 'The ways of the theologians in negating multiplicity and plurality.' Then each argument is presented as a numbered point:

1. Fourth argument: If two gods existed, for any intended action, either it is performed by both, which would entail a single act being between two independent agents (impossible), or by one, which would require a preponderator without any preponderating factor, since both are equal in capability and the act is equally possible for both.

2. Fifth argument: If one god wills something, the other either cannot will the opposite (then he is incapable) or can. If he can, then assuming both wills occur, either both opposites occur (impossible) or neither occurs (incapacity for both, impossible), or only one occurs (preponderance without preponderator).

3. Sixth argument: If they agree on an action, it would be a single act by two agents (coordinated); otherwise, mutual impediment.

4. Seventh argument: Whatever differentiates them must be either a necessary concomitant of divinity (which would be false, as divinity is one) or accidental, in which case it can be removed, and thus duality is removed.

5. Eighth argument: There is no proof for a second god, so it must be negated, otherwise impossibilities follow.

6. Ninth argument: If there were multiple, they would be infinite, as no priority exists.

7. Tenth argument: Scriptural evidences from consensus and definitive texts also refute polytheism, though some of the earlier rational arguments have weaknesses.

Key Terms
التمايز
اللوازم
الاثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the distinguishing feature is accidental, what happens according to the argument?
A) The gods become different in essence
B) The distinguishing feature cannot be removed
C) Duality is removed when the feature is removed
D) The gods remain distinct

Key Takeaway
Any distinguishing feature between gods either makes them essentially different (contradicting oneness) or accidental (removable, thus not truly separate).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that any distinguishing feature between two supposed gods either makes divinity not one (if essential) or is removable (if accidental), proving that multiple gods are impossible. Have you ever, even in a moment of difficulty, acted as if there is another power that can bring benefit or harm besides Allah? If so, how does this logical proof challenge you to repent and reaffirm your tawheed right now?
?
The source text uses a simple yet powerful syllogism to break the illusion of multiple gods. Do you truly rely on your intellect to affirm Allah's oneness, or do you merely repeat the words without letting the reasoning penetrate your heart? What one step can you take today to internalize this proof so that it strengthens your certainty in times of doubt?
?
Consider the phrase 'if the distinguishing feature can be removed, then duality vanishes.' In your own life, are there 'distinguishing features' you have added to your worship that are not essential to your relationship with Allah? Are there practices or beliefs that you hold onto that, if removed, would reveal that your devotion is actually directed elsewhere? This argument calls you to strip away anything accidental that competes with pure tawheed. What will you remove today?

Eighth Argument: No Proof for a Second
Al-Taftazani presents the eighth argument against multiple gods: absence of proof necessitates negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from lack of evidence for multiple gods.

Core Idea
If there were a second god, we would have evidence; absence of proof indicates impossibility.

This is an argument from the principle of sufficient reason. If a second god existed, there must be some indication of its existence. Since there is none, it cannot exist. Any attempt to assume otherwise leads to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. It is a straightforward claim.
2
Understand the necessity of proof
For the existence of a second god, there must be a proof (دليل). Without proof, we must negate it.
3
Consider the consequence
If we do not negate it, impossibilities would follow, meaning the assumption itself is impossible.

Source Text

Ninth Argument: Infinite Regress
Al-Taftazani presents the ninth argument against multiple gods: no priority leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that multiple gods lead to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Multiple gods would have no ordering principle, leading to an infinite chain.

This argument states that if gods are multiple, there is no reason for any one to be first or prior. Hence they would be infinite, which is impossible for necessary beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. It is very brief.
2
Understand 'no priority'
If multiple gods exist, each is equally necessary; there is no reason for one to be prior to another. Therefore, the set would have no first element, implying infinity.
3
Why infinity is impossible
An actual infinite of necessary beings is absurd because necessary beings must be uncaused and non-composite; an infinite set would violate the principle of causality.

Source Text

Tenth Argument: Scriptural Evidences
Al-Taftazani presents the tenth argument against multiple gods: scriptural evidences from consensus and definitive texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of scriptural arguments and the acknowledgment of weaknesses in some rational arguments.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs decisively reject polytheism, even if some rational arguments have flaws.

This final argument shifts from reason to revelation. It cites consensus of Muslims and definitive Quranic verses as conclusive proof against multiple gods. It also honestly notes that some earlier rational arguments have weaknesses.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the mention of two types of evidence.
2
Identify the scriptural evidences
The argument mentions الإجماع (consensus) and النصوص القطعية (definitive texts).
3
Reflect on the acknowledgment of weakness
Al-Taftazani admits that some earlier rational arguments have weakness, showing intellectual honesty.

Take a concrete step today: read a classical tafsir on a verse of tawhid and see how the scholars understood it.

The Fourth Argument: A Single Act Cannot Be from Two Gods
Al-Taftazani presents the two possibilities for a single act under two gods and shows their impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single act cannot be produced by two independent gods, based on the argument of preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
Two independent gods cannot produce a single act without contradiction.

In this argument, al-Taftazani lays out the rational proof that the existence of two gods leads to an impossibility. He assumes two gods with all divine attributes and examines what happens when they intend to create a specific possible act. The analysis reveals that such an act would have to be produced either by both or by one, and both options are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses a very systematic approach.
2
Identify the two options
The passage presents two possibilities for the occurrence of the act: by both gods or by one. Find each and note the reason why each is impossible.
3
Understand the logic of impossibility
Ask yourself why each option fails. The first fails due to causal overdetermination, the second due to lack of preponderator.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: شروع في طرق المتكلمين فمنها أنه لو وجد إلهان، ويتصفان لا محالة بصفات الألوهية من العلم والقدرة والإرادة وغير ذلك. فإذا قصد إلى إيجاد مقدور معين كحركة جسم معين في زمان معين فوقوعه إما أن يكون بهما فيلزم مقدور بين قادرين مستقلين بمعنى استقلال كل منهما بإيجاده وقد سبق في بحث العلة امتناع ذلك. وإما أن يكون بأحدهما، فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، لأن المقتضى للقادرية ذات الإله، وللمقدورية إمكان الممكن فنسبة الممكنات إلى الإلهين المفروضين على السوية من غير رجحان. لا يقال يجوز أن لا يقع مثل هذا المقدور للزوم المحال، أو يقع بهما جميعاً لا بكل منهما ليلزم المحال. لأنا نقول للأول باطل للزوم عجزهما، ولأن المانع عند وقوعه بأحدهما ليس إلا وقوعه بالآخر، فيلزم من عدم وقوعه بهما وقوعه بهما. وكذا الثاني لأن التقدير استقلال كل منهما بالقدرة والإرادة.
Fourth: A commencement of the ways of the theologians. Among them is that if there were two gods, and they are necessarily qualified with the attributes of divinity—knowledge, power, will, and others—and if they intend to bring into existence a specific possible act, such as the motion of a specific body at a specific time, then its occurrence is either by both, necessitating an act between two independent agents, meaning each is independent in its production, and this was already shown impossible in the discussion of causality; or it is by one of them, necessitating preponderance without a preponderator, because the necessitating factor for capability is the essence of the god, and for possibility it is the possibility of the possible, so the relationship of possible things to the two supposed gods is equal without preponderance. It cannot be said: 'It is possible that such an act does not occur due to the impossibility [that would follow], or that it occurs by both together, not by each, to avoid impossibility.' For we say: The first is false because it necessitates their incapacity, and because the preventer when it occurs by one is only its occurrence by the other, so from its non-occurrence by both, its occurrence by both follows. Likewise the second, because the assumption is that each is independent in power and will.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument in detail. It begins by stating that this is the start of the theologians' ways. The argument assumes two gods with all divine attributes. They intend to create a specific act (e.g., motion of a body at a specific time). The occurrence of that act must be either by both gods or by one. If by both, it becomes a single act between two independent agents, which was already proven impossible in the discussion of causality (causal overdetermination). If by one, then that one is chosen without any preponderating factor, because the grounds for capability (essence) and possibility (intrinsic possibility) are equal for both. An objection is then raised: perhaps the act does not occur at all (to avoid impossibility), or it occurs by the two together but not by each individually (so no single agent). The answer refutes the first: if the act did not occur, both would be incapable, and the only obstacle to one producing it is the other's production, so if neither produces it, the obstacle is removed and both produce it (contradiction). The second: the assumption is that each is independent, so if they produce together, each independently produces the same effect, which is the same impossibility as before.

Key Terms
مقدور بين قادرين مستقلين
الترجيح بلا مرجح
استقلال
قادرية بالذات
مقدورية بالإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if two gods exist, they could cooperate to produce an act, but the argument shows that cooperation would require each to be independent, leading to overdetermination.
Correction: Cooperation would still involve each god's independent causation of the same effect, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why is it impossible for a single act to be produced by both gods?
A) Because they would need a preponderator to decide which one acts.
B) Because it would require each god to be independent in causing the same effect, which is impossible.
C) Because the act would not occur at all.
D) Because the gods would be incapable.

Key Takeaway
A single act cannot be produced by two independent gods without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone is the independent Creator of every single act, then where do you look for the causes of your successes and failures? Do you attribute your achievements to your own effort, or to the One who alone can produce them? This lesson demands that you examine your heart: in whom or what do you truly place your trust for even the smallest outcome?
?
Consider a recent situation where you felt torn between two equal options. How did you decide? The argument shows that without a preponderator, preponderance is impossible. Does your life reflect a belief that Allah is the only One who tips the scales? Or do you act as if chance or your own whim determines choices? Let this question unsettle you until you repent of any hidden shirk in your decision-making.
?
The impossibility of two gods means that absolutely every event—from the fall of a leaf to the beat of your heart—is under the sole control of Allah. Does this truth make you feel secure or terrified? Be honest: does your daily worry reveal that you act as if other powers (people, money, health) have independent influence? Commit today to recognizing Allah's sole agency in everything, and let that reshape your fears and hopes.

Refuting the Objection: The Act Could Be Avoided or Produced Jointly
Al-Taftazani refutes two objections against the fourth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that the act could not occur or be produced jointly by both gods fails to rescue the hypothesis of two gods.

Core Idea
Objections that seem to avoid the contradiction actually lead to greater absurdities.

After presenting the main argument, al-Taftazani anticipates an objection. The objector suggests two possible escapes: either the act does not occur at all (to avoid the impossible conclusion), or it occurs by both together but not by each individually. The theologian's response shows that both suggestions are self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. The objection is introduced with 'la yuqalu' (it cannot be said).
Notice the structure: objection then answer.
2
Analyze the first objection
The objector suggests the act might not occur. Understand why al-Taftazani refutes this: it implies incapacity and leads to contradiction.
3
Analyze the second objection
The objector suggests joint production without individual causation. See why the refutation uses the assumption of independence.

Source Text

?

?

?
