Fifth Argument: Conflicting Wills Lead to Absurdity
Al-Taftazani presents the fifth argument against multiple gods based on conflicting wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that multiple gods would lead to contradictory wills, resulting in absurdities such as the coexistence of opposites or the incapacity of omnipotent beings.

Core Idea
Two omnipotent gods cannot coexist because their wills would conflict, leading to impossible outcomes.

Al-Taftazani presents the fifth rational argument against the existence of multiple gods. He demonstrates that if two gods each possess complete power, their potential conflicting wills create a series of impossible scenarios. This lesson walks through the logical dilemma and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the structure of the argument. Notice how al-Taftazani sets up a dilemma and then explores the consequences.
The passage is incomplete at the end; focus on the core argument.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument begins by asking whether the second god can will the opposite of what the first wills. Both answers (yes or no) lead to impossibility. Trace each horn.
3
Study the three sub-scenarios under the first horn
If the second god can will the opposite, three outcomes are possible: both intended objects occur, neither occurs, or only one occurs. Each is shown to be impossible. Understand why each is impossible.
These are classic contradictions: conjunction of opposites, absence of both, or preponderance without a preponderator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: أنه لو وجد إلهان بصفة الألوهية، فإذا أراد أحدهما أمراً كحركة جسم مثلاً فإما أن يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده أو لا؟ وكلاهما محال. أما الأول: فلانه لو فرض تعلق إرادته بذلك الضد. فإما أن يقع مرادهما وهو محال لاستلزام اجتماع الضدين، أو لا يقع مراد واحد منهما وهو محال لاستلزامه عجز الإلهين الموصوفين بكمال القدرة على ما هو المفروض ولاستلزامه ارتفاع الضدين المفروض امتناع خلو المحل عنهما كحركة جسم وسكونه في زمان واحد معين؛ أو يقع مراد أحدهما دون الآخر وهو محال لاستلزامه الترجيح بلا مرجح، وعجز من فرض قادراً حيث لم يقع مراده. وأما الثاني: فلأنه يستلزم عجز الآخر حيث لم يقدر على ما هو ممكن في نفسه أعني إرادة الضد، والمقدمات كلها بينة سوى هذه، فإنها ربما تمنع ويقال لا ...
The fifth aspect: If there were two gods with the attribute of divinity, and if one of them willed a thing, such as the motion of a body, then either the other is able to will its opposite or not? Both are impossible. As for the first: because if one assumes the attachment of his will to that opposite, then either both of their intended objects occur, which is impossible because it necessitates the conjunction of opposites; or neither of their intended objects occurs, which is impossible because it necessitates the incapacity of the two gods described as possessing complete power, as is assumed, and because it necessitates the absence of the two opposites, assuming that the locus cannot be devoid of them, such as the motion of a body and its rest in one specific time; or the intended object of one occurs without the other, which is impossible because it necessitates preponderance without a preponderator, and the incapacity of the one assumed to be powerful, since his intended object did not occur. As for the second: because it necessitates the incapacity of the other, since he was not able to do what is possible in itself, namely the willing of the opposite. All the premises are evident except this one, for it may be objected and it is said no...
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, focusing on the problem of conflicting wills. It assumes two gods with divine attributes. If one wills something (e.g., motion), the other either can will the opposite (e.g., rest) or cannot. Both alternatives lead to impossibility. If he can will the opposite, then several scenarios follow: (a) both intended objects occur—impossible because opposites would coexist; (b) neither occurs—impossible because then both gods are incapable, and the locus would be empty of both opposites, which is also impossible (since a body cannot be neither moving nor at rest at a given time); (c) only one occurs—impossible because it requires a preponderator without cause, and the other god fails to achieve his will, implying incapacity. If the other cannot will the opposite, then he is incapable of willing something possible, which contradicts the attribute of complete power. The passage notes that all premises are evident except the claim that willing the opposite is possible in itself, which may be objected to. The text cuts off during the response to this objection.

Key Terms
إرادة الضد
اجتماع الضدين
ارتفاع الضدين
عجز
الترجيح بلا مرجح
كمال القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes the two gods are unequal in power, so one can override the other.
Correction: The argument assumes both gods have complete power (kāmil al-qudra) equally. The impossibility arises precisely because both are omnipotent, making any conflict unresolvable.

Checkpoint
In the fifth argument, if one god wills motion and the other is able to will the opposite, which of the following is NOT presented as an impossible outcome?
A) Both intended objects occur.
B) Neither intended object occurs.
C) Only the motion occurs.
D) Both gods change their wills.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of conflicting wills among omnipotent beings logically necessitates the oneness of God.

The Core Structure of the Proof of Mutual Hindrance
Sanusi presents the first part of the proof of mutual hindrance, outlining the logical alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of mutual hindrance establishes the impossibility of two gods by showing that both possibilities (the other god being able or unable to will the opposite) lead to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot coexist because their wills inevitably lead to logical contradictions.

Sanusi presents the fifth proof for the impossibility of more than one god, known as the proof of mutual hindrance (burhan al-tamanu'). The argument proceeds by assuming two gods and then examining the logical consequences of their wills. This passage walks through the core reasoning, showing that any scenario leads to an impossibility. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping the rational basis for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's opening assumption
Start by reading the Arabic passage that sets up the hypothesis: two gods exist, one wills something, and we ask whether the other can will the opposite. This is the foundation of the entire argument.
Note the phrase 'fima an yatamakkan al-akhar min iradat diddihi aw la' – this is the key branching point.
2
Follow the first branch: the other god can will the opposite
If the second god is able to will the opposite, then three sub-cases are examined. Work through each sub-case and see why it is impossible. Pay attention to the terms 'social all-diddain' (conjunction of opposites) and 'tarji bil la murajjih' (preference without a preponderant).
The first sub-case (both wills realized) leads to conjunction of opposites; the second (neither realized) leads to incapacity; the third (only one realized) leads to preference without cause.
3
Follow the second branch: the other god cannot will the opposite
If the second god is unable to will the opposite, then this implies incapacity on his part, contradicting the assumed perfection of divine power.
Note that the premise 'the second god is unable to will the opposite' is later denied by an objection, which will be addressed in the next lesson.
4
Understand the conclusion of the proof
Since both possibilities lead to impossibility, the original assumption of two gods must be false. Reflect on how this proof uses pure logic without reliance on scripture.
The proof is called 'burhan al-tamanu' because of the mutual hindrance (tamanu) between the wills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: أنه لو وجد إلهان بصفة الألوهية، فإذا أراد أحدهما أمراً كحركة جسم مثلاً فإما أن يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده أو لا؟ وكلاهما محال. أما الأول: فلانه لو فرض تعلق إرادته بذلك الضد. فإما أن يقع مرادهما وهو محال لاستلزام اجتماع الضدين، أولا يقع مراد واحد منهما وهو محال لاستلزامه عجز الإلهين الموصوفين بكمال القدرة على ما هو المفروض ولاستلزامه ارتفاع الضدين المفروض امتناع خلو المحل عنهما كحركة جسم وسكونه في زمان واحد معين؛ أو يقع مراد أحدهما دون الآخر وهو محال لاستلزامه الترجيح بلا مرجح، وعجز من فرض قادراً حيث لم يقع مراده. وأما الثاني: فلأنه يستلزم عجز الآخر حيث لم يقدر على ما هو ممكن في نفسه أعني إرادة الضد، والمقدمات كلها بينة سوى هذه، فإنها ربما تمنع ويقال لا نسلم أن مخالفة أحدهما للآخر، وإرادة ضد ما أراده ممكنة حتى يكون عدم القدرة عليها عجزاً! وذلك أن الممكن في نفسه ربما يصير ممتنعاً بحسب شرط، ككون الجسم في هذا الحيز حال الكون في حيز آخر. والجواب: أن الممكن في ذاته ممكن على كل حال ضرورة امتناع الانقلاب، والممتنع فيما ذكرتم من تحيز الجسم هو الاجتماع أعني كونه في آن واحد، في حيزين فكذا ها هنا يمتنع اجتماع الإرادتين، وهو لا ينافي إمكان كل منهما، فتعين أن لزوم المحال، إنما هو من وجود الإلهين. فإن قيل: كل منهما عالم بوجوه المصالح والمفاسد. فإذا على المصلحة في أحد الضدين، امتنع إرادة الآخر. قلنا: لو سلم كون الإرادة تابعة للمصلحة، ففرض الكلام فيما إذا استوت في الضدين وجوه المصالح. فإن قيل: ما ذكرتم لازم في الواحد إذا وجد المقدور؛ فإنه لا يبقى قادراً عليه، ضرورة امتناع إيجاد الموجود فيلزم أن لا يصلح للألوهية. قلنا: عدم القدرة منا على تنفيذ القدرة ليس عجزاً بل كمالاً للقدرة بخلاف عدم القدرة بناء على سد الغير طريق القدرة عليه، فإنه عجز بتعجيز الغير إياه. وهذا البرهان يسمى برهان التمانع وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى: (لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا).
The fifth proof: That if there were two gods with the attribute of divinity, then if one of them willed something, such as the movement of a body, then either the other is able to will its opposite or not? Both are impossible. As for the first: because if his will were attached to that opposite, then either both of their objects of will occur, which is impossible because it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites; or neither of their objects of will occurs, which is impossible because it necessitates the incapacity of the two gods described with perfect power, as is assumed, and because it necessitates the non-existence of both opposites, whereas it is impossible for a locus to be devoid of them, like the movement of a body and its rest in one specific time; or the object of will of one of them occurs without the other, which is impossible because it necessitates preference without a preponderant and the incapacity of the one assumed to be powerful when his object of will did not occur. As for the second: because it necessitates the incapacity of the other, as he was not able to do what is possible in itself, namely the will of the opposite. All the premises are evident except this one, for it might be denied and it might be said: we do not accept that the opposition of one to the other and the willing of the opposite of what he willed is possible such that inability to do it constitutes incapacity! For the possible in itself may become impossible due to a condition, like the body's being in this location while it is in another location. The answer: That the possible in itself is possible in every case, due to the necessity of the impossibility of change, and the impossible in what you mentioned of the body's being in a place is the conjunction, meaning its being at one moment in two places. So likewise here, the conjunction of the two wills is impossible, and that does not negate the possibility of each of them. So it is established that the occurrence of the impossible is only from the existence of two gods. If it is said: Each of them knows the aspects of benefits and harms. So if the benefit is in one of the two opposites, the will of the other is impossible. We say: Even if it is granted that the will follows benefit, the hypothesis of the discussion is when the aspects of benefits are equal in the two opposites. If it is said: What you mentioned applies to the One when the object of power exists; for He does not remain powerful over it, due to the impossibility of creating the already existing, and it would then follow that He is not fit for divinity. We say: The inability (from us) to execute power is not incapacity but perfection of power, unlike the inability based on someone else blocking the path of power over it, for that is incapacity caused by another's disabling. This proof is called the proof of mutual hindrance, and it is indicated by His saying, Exalted: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-wajh al-khamis) for the impossibility of multiple gods, known as the proof of mutual hindrance (burhan al-tamanu'). The author assumes two gods possessing divinity. If one wills a specific action, such as the movement of a body, the other may either be able to will its opposite (e.g., rest) or not. Both options lead to absurdity, thereby demonstrating the impossibility of two gods. The author then addresses three objections and provides answers. The first objection argues that the possible may become impossible due to a condition, similar to a body being unable to be in two places at once. The answer clarifies that the impossibility lies in the conjunction, not in the individual possibility. The second objection posits that if each god knows the best interest, contradictory wills would not occur. The author responds by assuming equal benefit in both options. The third objection contends that even a single god cannot create what already exists, so this proof would apply to Him as well. The author distinguishes between inability due to perfection (completion of power) and inability due to external obstruction. The passage concludes by naming this argument 'burhan al-tamanu' and citing Quran 21:22.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist if they never will conflicting things.
Correction: The proof shows that even the mere possibility of conflict (the ability to will the opposite) leads to logical absurdity, so actual conflict is not required.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what happens if both gods' wills are realized when they will opposite things?
A) The universe is destroyed.
B) It leads to the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible.
C) One god overpowers the other.
D) The universe remains unchanged.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance demonstrates that the existence of two gods logically leads to absurdities, thereby proving that only one God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have now seen that the mere possibility of conflicting wills between two gods leads to logical absurdity. But in your own life, do you ever act as if there are multiple ultimate authorities—seeking approval from people, fearing their judgment more than Allah's? This proof exposes the rational necessity of exclusive devotion. Examine your heart: have you given anyone or anything a share of the reverence that belongs only to Allah? What concrete step will you take today to uproot that hidden shirk?
?
The proof shows that if two gods existed, their wills would inevitably conflict, leading to impossibility. Yet you, a servant of the One God, often experience inner conflict when you try to please both Allah and your own desires simultaneously. How does this lesson's logic expose the futility of serving multiple masters? When you feel that tension, will you pause and ask: 'Which will am I prioritizing right now?' Commit to one action this week where you consciously choose Allah's will over your own.
?
The third sub-case in the proof hinges on the impossibility of preference without a preponderant. In your daily choices—what you eat, how you spend your time, whom you obey—do you act with intention and purpose, or are you driven by whim and habit? The proof demands that every effect has a sufficient reason. How can you bring this rational awareness into your worship, ensuring that your actions are motivated by a clear desire for Allah's pleasure rather than empty routine? What is one worship act you will perform today with renewed intentionality?

Responding to Objections on the Proof of Mutual Hindrance
Sanusi addresses three objections to the proof of mutual hindrance and concludes with the Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's responses to three key objections that challenge the proof of mutual hindrance, and how the proof is preserved.

Core Idea
Objections based on possibility, divine wisdom, and divine power are answered without undermining the proof.

After presenting the core argument of mutual hindrance, Sanusi anticipates and refutes several objections. These objections attempt to show that the proof's premises might be flawed or that the same reasoning could apply to the One God. By addressing them, Sanusi strengthens the proof and clarifies important aspects of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and its answer
The first objection denies that the other god can will the opposite, arguing that a possible thing may become impossible due to a condition (like a body being unable to be in two places at once). Read Sanusi's answer carefully.
Note the distinction between the impossibility of conjunction and the possibility of each individual will.
2
Read the second objection and its answer
The second objection claims that if each god knows the best interest, they would never will conflicting things. Examine Sanusi's response about equal benefit.
Sanusi concedes the premise for argument but restricts the scenario to cases where benefits are equal.
3
Read the third objection and its answer
The third objection argues that the same reasoning would apply to the One God (e.g., He cannot create what already exists). Understand Sanusi's distinction between two types of inability.
The key is that inability due to completion of power is not a deficiency, unlike inability caused by another's obstruction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and Quranic citation
Sanusi names the proof 'burhan al-tamanu' and cites Quran 21:22. Consider how this proof integrates rational and scriptural evidence.
The verse 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted' succinctly echoes the logical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس: أنه لو وجد إلهان بصفة الألوهية، فإذا أراد أحدهما أمراً كحركة جسم مثلاً فإما أن يتمكن الآخر من إرادة ضده أو لا؟ وكلاهما محال. أما الأول: فلانه لو فرض تعلق إرادته بذلك الضد. فإما أن يقع مرادهما وهو محال لاستلزام اجتماع الضدين، أولا يقع مراد واحد منهما وهو محال لاستلزامه عجز الإلهين الموصوفين بكمال القدرة على ما هو المفروض ولاستلزامه ارتفاع الضدين المفروض امتناع خلو المحل عنهما كحركة جسم وسكونه في زمان واحد معين؛ أو يقع مراد أحدهما دون الآخر وهو محال لاستلزامه الترجيح بلا مرجح، وعجز من فرض قادراً حيث لم يقع مراده. وأما الثاني: فلأنه يستلزم عجز الآخر حيث لم يقدر على ما هو ممكن في نفسه أعني إرادة الضد، والمقدمات كلها بينة سوى هذه، فإنها ربما تمنع ويقال لا نسلم أن مخالفة أحدهما للآخر، وإرادة ضد ما أراده ممكنة حتى يكون عدم القدرة عليها عجزاً! وذلك أن الممكن في نفسه ربما يصير ممتنعاً بحسب شرط، ككون الجسم في هذا الحيز حال الكون في حيز آخر. والجواب: أن الممكن في ذاته ممكن على كل حال ضرورة امتناع الانقلاب، والممتنع فيما ذكرتم من تحيز الجسم هو الاجتماع أعني كونه في آن واحد، في حيزين فكذا ها هنا يمتنع اجتماع الإرادتين، وهو لا ينافي إمكان كل منهما، فتعين أن لزوم المحال، إنما هو من وجود الإلهين. فإن قيل: كل منهما عالم بوجوه المصالح والمفاسد. فإذا على المصلحة في أحد الضدين، امتنع إرادة الآخر. قلنا: لو سلم كون الإرادة تابعة للمصلحة، ففرض الكلام فيما إذا استوت في الضدين وجوه المصالح. فإن قيل: ما ذكرتم لازم في الواحد إذا وجد المقدور؛ فإنه لا يبقى قادراً عليه، ضرورة امتناع إيجاد الموجود فيلزم أن لا يصلح للألوهية. قلنا: عدم القدرة منا على تنفيذ القدرة ليس عجزاً بل كمالاً للقدرة بخلاف عدم القدرة بناء على سد الغير طريق القدرة عليه، فإنه عجز بتعجيز الغير إياه. وهذا البرهان يسمى برهان التمانع وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى: (لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا).
The fifth proof: That if there were two gods with the attribute of divinity, then if one of them willed something, such as the movement of a body, then either the other is able to will its opposite or not? Both are impossible. As for the first: because if his will were attached to that opposite, then either both of their objects of will occur, which is impossible because it necessitates the conjunction of two opposites; or neither of their objects of will occurs, which is impossible because it necessitates the incapacity of the two gods described with perfect power, as is assumed, and because it necessitates the non-existence of both opposites, whereas it is impossible for a locus to be devoid of them, like the movement of a body and its rest in one specific time; or the object of will of one of them occurs without the other, which is impossible because it necessitates preference without a preponderant and the incapacity of the one assumed to be powerful when his object of will did not occur. As for the second: because it necessitates the incapacity of the other, as he was not able to do what is possible in itself, namely the will of the opposite. All the premises are evident except this one, for it might be denied and it might be said: we do not accept that the opposition of one to the other and the willing of the opposite of what he willed is possible such that inability to do it constitutes incapacity! For the possible in itself may become impossible due to a condition, like the body's being in this location while it is in another location. The answer: That the possible in itself is possible in every case, due to the necessity of the impossibility of change, and the impossible in what you mentioned of the body's being in a place is the conjunction, meaning its being at one moment in two places. So likewise here, the conjunction of the two wills is impossible, and that does not negate the possibility of each of them. So it is established that the occurrence of the impossible is only from the existence of two gods. If it is said: Each of them knows the aspects of benefits and harms. So if the benefit is in one of the two opposites, the will of the other is impossible. We say: Even if it is granted that the will follows benefit, the hypothesis of the discussion is when the aspects of benefits are equal in the two opposites. If it is said: What you mentioned applies to the One when the object of power exists; for He does not remain powerful over it, due to the impossibility of creating the already existing, and it would then follow that He is not fit for divinity. We say: The inability (from us) to execute power is not incapacity but perfection of power, unlike the inability based on someone else blocking the path of power over it, for that is incapacity caused by another's disabling. This proof is called the proof of mutual hindrance, and it is indicated by His saying, Exalted: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-wajh al-khamis) for the impossibility of multiple gods, known as the proof of mutual hindrance (burhan al-tamanu'). The author assumes two gods possessing divinity. If one wills a specific action, such as the movement of a body, the other may either be able to will its opposite (e.g., rest) or not. Both options lead to absurdity, thereby demonstrating the impossibility of two gods. The author then addresses three objections and provides answers. The first objection argues that the possible may become impossible due to a condition, similar to a body being unable to be in two places at once. The answer clarifies that the impossibility lies in the conjunction, not in the individual possibility. The second objection posits that if each god knows the best interest, contradictory wills would not occur. The author responds by assuming equal benefit in both options. The third objection contends that even a single god cannot create what already exists, so this proof would apply to Him as well. The author distinguishes between inability due to perfection (completion of power) and inability due to external obstruction. The passage concludes by naming this argument 'burhan al-tamanu' and citing Quran 21:22.

Misconception: The proof of mutual hindrance also applies to the One God, making it invalid.
Correction: Inability to create what already exists is not a sign of weakness but perfection of power, unlike inability caused by another god's interference.

Answering the Objection of Mutual Hindrance
The author answers the objection that two gods could will opposite things without contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to the proof of divine unity based on mutual hindrance is answered, and why the impossibility of two gods arises from the conjunction of opposing wills.

Core Idea
Two gods can each will opposites, but their wills cannot meet.

The objection suggests that if two gods existed, each could will opposite things, and lacking power to do so would be impotence. The answer clarifies that the impossibility is not of each individual will but of their conjunction at one instant. This lesson unpacks that logical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's concession: each god could will the opposite, and lacking ability would be impotence. Note the example of a body in two places.
The objector uses the idea that a possible thing can become impossible due to a condition.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the difference between 'possible in itself' and 'possible under a condition'. The author insists that a thing possible in itself remains possible always.
This distinction is crucial for the answer.
3
See the answer's structure
The author shows that the impossibility is of the conjunction (social) of two conflicting wills, not of each will individually. The absurdity results from the existence of two gods, not from one god's action.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this preserves the proof of divine unity (tawhid). The proof from mutual hindrance remains valid because the contradiction arises only when two gods coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسلم (۱) أن مخالفة أحدهما للآخر، وإرادة ضد ما أراده ممكنة حتى يكون عدم القدرة عليها عجزاً! وذلك أن الممكن في نفسه ربما يصير ممتنعاً بحسب شرط، ككون الجسم في هذا الحيز حال الكون في حيز آخر. والجواب : أن الممكن في ذاته ممكن على كل حال ضرورة امتناع الانقلاب ، والممتنع فيما ذكرتم من تحيز الجسم هو الاجتماع أعني كونه في آن واحد، في حيزين فكذا ها هنا يمتنع اجتماع الإرادتين، وهو لا ينافي إمكان كل منهما ، فتعين أن لزوم المحال، إنما هو من وجود الإلهين.
We concede that opposition of one to the other, and willing the opposite of what the other wills, is possible, so that lack of ability to do so would be impotence! For a thing possible in itself may become impossible due to a condition, like a body being in this space while being in another space. And the answer: that a thing possible in itself is possible in every case due to the impossibility of change, and the impossible in what you mentioned of the body's spatiality is the conjunction, i.e., its being at one instant in two spaces; so likewise here, the conjunction of the two wills is impossible, and this does not negate the possibility of each one. So it is established that the necessary entailment of falsity is only from the existence of two gods.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector concedes that two gods could will opposite things, and that lacking power to do so would be impotence. They argue that a thing possible in itself can become impossible due to a condition (like a body being in two places at once). The author answers that the thing possible in itself remains possible; the impossibility is specifically of the conjunction of two opposite wills in one instant, not of each will individually. Thus the absurd consequence (luzum al-muhal) arises only from the existence of two gods, not from each one alone.

Key Terms
التمانع (al-tamānuʿ)
الاجتماع (al-ijtimāʿ)
المحال (al-muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection shows that two gods can each will opposite things, so there is no contradiction.
Correction: The contradiction arises not from each will individually but from the conjunction of both wills at the same time.

Checkpoint

A) Each god's power to act
B) The conjunction of the two wills at one time
C) The existence of any possible thing
D) The creation of the universe

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods is due to the necessary conjunction of opposing wills, not from each god's individual capabilities.

Objection on Knowledge of Benefits and Answer
Objection and answer about knowledge of benefits and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the proof of multiple gods by limiting the objection to cases of equal benefit.

Core Idea
The objection only works when benefits are unequal, not when they are equal.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a potential objection to his proof of tawḥīd: if each god knows all benefits, why would they ever will different things? He then gives a concise answer that restricts the scope of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice that the objector assumes each god knows all benefits and harms, so each will always choose the beneficial action. Ask yourself: does this make conflict impossible?
This is the core attack on the earlier proof.
2
Read the author's answer
The author first concedes the assumption for the sake of argument, then refocuses on the specific scenario where benefits are exactly equal. What happens then?
This is a classic mutakallimūn strategy of restriction (takhṣīṣ).
3
Reflect on the logical structure
See how the author does not deny the objector's premise but instead shifts the burden to show that the objection only applies in a limited case.
Consider why this defense is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : كل منهما عالم بوجوه المصالح والمفاسد . فإذا (۲) على المصلحة في أحد الضدين، امتنع إرادة الآخر. قلنا : لو سلم كون الإرادة تابعة للمصلحة، ففرض الكلام فيما إذا استوت في الضدين وجوه المصالح .
For it is said: Each of them knows the aspects of benefits and harms. So when the benefit is in one of the two opposites, the other's will is impossible. We say: Even if we concede that the will follows benefit, the discussion is only about when the aspects of benefit are equal in the two opposites.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector argues: If both gods know all benefits and harms, then whenever one sees benefit in an act, the other would also see the harm in the opposite, so both would will the same thing and conflict would not occur. The author responds by setting aside the case where benefits are unequal; the argument assumes interests are equal. In that hypothetical case, the will is not determined by benefit, leaving room for conflict. Thus the objection only works if benefits are always unequal, which need not be true.

Key Terms
المصالح (al-maṣāliḥ)
المفاسد (al-mafāsid)
الإرادة (al-irāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objector's point disproves the proof.
Correction: The author shows it only applies when benefits are unequal, which need not be the case.

Checkpoint
What does the author assume to answer the objection about each god knowing benefits?
A) That will does not follow benefit at all
B) That both gods are ignorant of some benefits
C) That the discussion is about when benefits are equal in both opposites
D) That only one god can act

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it assumes unequal benefit; when benefits are equal, will is not determined by benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that what benefits you must be God's will, and what harms you cannot be His will? Are you willing to accept that God's will is not always determined by your perception of benefit?
?

?
If God's will is not constrained by benefit, how should that change the way you pray? Do you ever pray as if God must grant your request because it seems beneficial? How can you submit more fully to His will even when you cannot see the benefit?

The Objection on Power After Creation and Its Answer
Objection on divine power after creation and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between voluntary cessation of divine power and forced inability, and why the former does not contradict divine perfection.

Core Idea
God's voluntary cessation of power is perfection, not impotence.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection: if God cannot recreate what already exists, doesn't that imply a lack of power? He responds by distinguishing two types of inability, showing that God's case is one of perfection, not defect. The key is whether the inability arises from choice or from being obstructed by another.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (the objector's claim) in Arabic and translation. Note that they argue that even a single God would not remain able to create something already existing.
The objector assumes that any lack of ability to act is a defect.
2
Identify the two scenarios in the answer
The author presents two cases: (1) not exercising power due to one's own will, and (2) being prevented by another. Identify the words that mark this distinction.
Pay attention to the phrases 'ليس عجزاً' and 'بل كمالاً'.
3
Reflect on why the objection fails
Consider why the first type (voluntary cessation) is not impotence. How does this apply to God after creation?
The objector's error is conflating the two types of inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : ما ذكرتم لازم في الواحد إذا وجد المقدور؛ فإنه لا يبقى قادراً عليه، ضرورة امتناع إيجاد الموجود فيلزم أن لا يصلح للألوهية . قلنا : عدم القدرة منا على تنفيذ القدرة ليس عجزاً بل كمالاً للقدرة بخلاف عدم القدرة بناء على سد الغير طريق القدرة عليه، فإنه عجز بتعجيز الغير إياه .
For it is said: What you mentioned is necessary in the case of the One God when the determined thing exists; for He does not remain able to do it, due to the impossibility of bringing into existence what already exists, so it follows that He is not fit for divinity. We say: The lack of ability from us to execute the power is not impotence but rather perfection of power, unlike the lack of ability due to another blocking the way of power over it, for that is impotence caused by the other incapacitating him.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector argues that the same problem occurs even with one God: after creating something, God cannot create it again, so He would lack power, and thus be unfit for divinity. The author distinguishes two types of inability: (1) voluntarily not exercising power – this is not impotence but perfection (since it is by choice); (2) inability due to being prevented by another – this is real impotence. The case of one God is of the first type, while the case with two gods would involve the second type if one prevents the other. Hence the objection fails.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
العجز (al-ʿajz)
التعجيز (al-taʿjīz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any inability to act negates divinity.
Correction: Voluntary cessation of power is not a defect but perfection; only forced inability due to obstruction is impotence.

Checkpoint
How does the author distinguish the inability in the case of one God from that of two gods?
A) One God is never unable; two gods are
B) Inability in one God is due to choice and is perfection; inability in two gods is due to obstruction and is impotence
C) Both are equally impotent
D) The inability in one God is also due to being blocked

Key Takeaway
The objector's fallacy is conflating voluntary non-exercise of power with forced inability; the former is perfection, the latter is impotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you chose not to use your ability, and someone accused you of weakness. How does the distinction between voluntary cessation and forced inability help you see that moment differently? Will you now view your own restraint as strength?
?
When you pray for something and it doesn't happen, do you ever feel that God is somehow limited or indifferent? How does this lesson’s teaching that God's inability to do the impossible is a perfection challenge that feeling? What will you say to yourself the next time doubt creeps in?
?
If God's power is perfected by not doing what is logically contradictory, what does that reveal about your own expectations of God? Are you ready to trust that His refusals are not failures but perfections? Write down one specific disappointment you will now reinterpret in light of this truth.

Naming the Proof and Quranic Evidence
The author names the proof and cites Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the rational proof for divine unity is called Burhan al-Tamana' and is referenced in the Quran.

Core Idea
The Proof of Mutual Hindrance has both rational and scriptural foundations.

In this brief passage, the author gives a name to the argument we have been studying. He also connects it to a clear Quranic verse, showing that this rational proof is not merely philosophical but has divine sanction. This lesson establishes the identification and scriptural basis of Burhan al-Tamana'.

Guided Path

First Interpretation of 'Corruption': Non-Formation of Heavens and Earth
The author refutes the first interpretation of 'corruption' as non-formation of heavens and earth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the interpretation of 'corruption' as the non-formation of the heavens and earth through logical analysis of divine power.

Core Idea
If two gods existed, formation of the world would be logically impossible.

This lesson examines the first of two interpretations of 'corruption' in the Quranic verse. The author shows that even if corruption means the failure of the heavens and earth to come into being, the same tawḥīd argument can be made. By analyzing three logical possibilities for formation under multiple gods, each is shown to be impossible. This passage thus strengthens the proof for the oneness of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: the author first assumes a particular interpretation, then breaks down the possibilities.
Pay attention to the three options presented.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Find the three possibilities for how the heavens and earth could have been formed if there were two gods. Write them down.
They begin with 'إما لمجموع القدرتين أو بكل منهما أو بأحدهما'.
3
Understand the refutation of each
For each possibility, note the reason given for its invalidity. The first is rejected because each god is perfect. The others are rejected by reference to prior arguments.
The author says 'أما الأول فلأن من شأن الإله كمال القدرة' and 'وأما الآخران فلما مر'.
4
Connect to the larger tawḥīd argument
Reflect on how this interpretation, far from weakening the proof, actually reinforces the necessity of a single creator.
The impossibility of multiple gods creating the world is demonstrated regardless of how 'corruption' is defined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أريد بالفساد عدم التكون، فتقديره أنه لو تعدد الإله لم تتكون السماء والأرض، لأن تكونهما إما لمجموع القدرتين أو بكل منهما أو بأحدهما ، والكل باطل . أما الأول : فلأن من شأن الإله كمال القدرة . وأما الآخران فلما مرّ.

As for the first: because the nature of a god is perfection of power. And as for the other two: because of what has preceded.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains one possible interpretation of 'corruption' in the Quranic verse: that it means the failure of the heavens and earth to come into being. The author considers four possibilities: (a) formation by the combined power of two gods, (b) by both individually, (c) by one of them. He rejects the first because each god has perfect power, so he would not need help; the second leads to redundancy; the third implies the other is not truly divine. The last two are rejected by previous arguments (referring to earlier proofs). Thus this interpretation supports the proof.

Second Interpretation: Departure from Order and Harmony
The author explains the second interpretation of 'corruption' as departure from order, and deduces the impossibility of multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second interpretation of 'corruption' in the Quranic verse refers to departure from cosmic order, and how this leads to a proof for the oneness of God.

Core Idea
The universe's harmony refutes the possibility of multiple gods.

In this passage, the author presents the second meaning of 'corruption' in the verse 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both be corrupted.' This meaning focuses on the disruption of order and harmony. By examining what would happen if multiple gods existed, we can see why the world's observed coherence points to a single Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms like 'order', 'dispute', and 'cohesion'.
The passage is a single sentence but packs a logical argument.
2
Understand 'departure from order'
The author interprets 'corruption' not as mutual corruption but as deviation from the proper order. Consider what order means in the universe.
This interpretation relies on the observed harmony of creation.
3
Trace the argument's logic
Follow the conditional: if multiple gods existed, they would conflict, causing distinct works and loss of cohesion, which would disrupt the order necessary for species and causation. Conclude that multiple gods are impossible.
This is a logical deduction from the nature of multiple independent wills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أريد بالفساد الخروج عما هما عليه من النظام فتقديره أنه لو تعدد الإله لكان بينهما التنازع والتغالب، وتميز صنع كل عن صنع الآخر بحكم اللزوم العادي، فلم يحصل بين أجزاء العالم هذا الالتئام الذي باعتباره صار الكل بمنزلة شخص واحد، ويختل النظام الذي به بقاء الأنواع وترتب الآثار.
And if by 'corruption' is meant the departure from the order that they are upon, then its estimation is that if there were multiple gods, there would be dispute and mutual overcoming between them, and the work of each would be distinguished from the work of the other by virtue of ordinary necessity; then this cohesion among the parts of the world, by which the whole became like a single person, would not occur, and the order by which species endure and effects follow would be disrupted.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the second interpretation of 'corruption' in the Quranic verse: the disruption of the world's order and harmony. The author argues that if multiple gods existed, they would inevitably compete and overcome each other, leading to distinct and conflicting actions. The observed coherence and unity of the universe, where all parts function as a single organized whole, would be impossible. This order is essential for the continuation of species and causation. Hence this interpretation also supports the impossibility of multiple gods.

Sixth Argument: Agreement and Disagreement Both Impossible
The author presents the sixth argument using a dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument: the impossibility of two gods is shown through a dichotomy where both agreement and disagreement lead to absurdities.

Core Idea
Two gods: agreement = redundancy, disagreement = impotence.

This lesson presents a classic kalam argument: if two gods exist, whether they agree or disagree, the result is impossible. The argument uses a simple dichotomy to show the absurdity of polytheism. By examining both horns, we see that either redundancy or mutual hindrance necessarily follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Note the two conditional phrases: 'if they agree' and 'if they disagree'. The conclusion is that both lead to impossibility.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the two horns
Identify the two scenarios: agreement on every action leads to redundancy (tawārud), and disagreement leads to mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ), resulting in impotence or unjustified preponderance.
Redundancy means one god is unnecessary; mutual hindrance means neither can act.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
Both outcomes are absurd, so the assumption of two gods must be false. This argument is a powerful demonstration of tawhid using pure reason.
The term 'tarjīḥ bilā tarajjuḥ' is key: a preference without a reason is irrational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : لو وجد إلهان. فإن اتفقا على إيجاد كل مقدور لزم التوارد وإن اختلفا لزم مفاسد التمانع أعني عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما مع الترجيح بلا ترجح .
The sixth aspect: If there were two gods. Then if they agree on bringing into existence every possible thing, it necessitates redundancy (tawārud); and if they disagree, it necessitates the corruptions of mutual hindrance, meaning their impotence or the impotence of one of them, along with preponderance without a preponderator.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This is the sixth of a series of independent arguments against the possibility of two gods. The author presents a dichotomy: if both gods always agree on every action, then their duality is redundant (tawārud) because one would be sufficient. If they disagree, then mutual hindrance occurs, leading to either both being unable to act (impotence) or one being impotent, which requires a preponderator (murajjih) that is without a preponderator, which is absurd. Thus both horns lead to impossibility.

Key Terms
التوارد
التمانع
الترجيح بلا ترجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two gods always agree, there is no problem.
Correction: Agreement leads to redundancy (tawārud), meaning one god is superfluous, which is absurd for a necessary being.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what happens if two gods disagree?
A) They both act perfectly
B) It leads to redundancy
C) It leads to their impotence or one's impotence with unjustified preponderance
D) The universe becomes more orderly

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument uses a dichotomy to show that two gods are impossible because both agreement and disagreement lead to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the logical impossibility of two gods, does your heart acknowledge that the oneness of Allah is not just a belief but a necessity for the universe to exist as it is? If not, what doubts remain?

?

Seventh Argument: Distinction is Accidental and Removable
Seventh argument: distinction is accidental and removable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logic behind the seventh argument for the oneness of God: that any distinction between two gods must be accidental and removable, leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Distinction between gods must be accidental, not essential, so they could become one—absurd.

The seventh argument tackles a key assumption of polytheism: that multiple gods could exist. It uses a clever logical analysis: if two gods exist, they must be distinct, but that distinction cannot be part of their divinity (since both are divine), so it must be accidental and thus removable. This leads to an impossibility, proving that only one God can exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the compressed style of the argument.
The Arabic uses key terms like 'lawāzim' and 'ʿawāriḍ'.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument has two key premises: (1) both gods share divinity, so any distinguishing feature cannot be essential to divinity; (2) therefore, it must be an accident.
Essential attributes must be shared; accidental ones can differ.
3
Trace the logical chain to absurdity
If the distinguishing feature is an accident, it can be removed. If removed, the two gods would become one. The possibility of two becoming one is absurd (since they were distinct entities), so two gods cannot exist.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on why this proves tawhid
The argument shows that the existence of two gods is logically impossible. Thus, only one God (tawhid) can be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : لو تعدد الإله فما به التمايز لا يجوز أن يكون من لوازم الإلهية ضرورة اشتراكهما ، بل من العوارض فيجوز مفارقتها فترفع الاثنينية، فيلزم جواز وحدة الاثنين وهو محال .
The seventh aspect: If there were multiple gods, then that by which they are distinguished cannot be among the necessary concomitants of divinity (lawāzim al-ilāhiyya) due to the necessity of their sharing [in divinity], but rather it is among accidents (ʿawāriḍ), so it is possible for them to be separated from it, and thus the duality would be removed, and it would follow that the unity of the two is possible, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This is the seventh argument. The author reasons that if two gods exist, they must be distinct in some way. But that distinction cannot be essential to divinity, because both share the essence of divinity equally (otherwise one would not be divine). Therefore, the distinguishing factor must be an accident (ʿaraḍ) that can be separated. If it is removed, the two would become one, implying that the union of two distinct entities into one is possible—an absurdity. Thus two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms
اللوازم
العوارض
الاثنينية
الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two gods could be distinguished by essential attributes like power or knowledge, but if they are both divine, those attributes are essential to divinity and must be shared identically, so they cannot differentiate.
Correction: Essential attributes are common to all divine beings by definition; differentiation must be accidental, and accidents are removable, leading to logical absurdity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the distinguishing factor between two gods must be accidental?
A) Because essential attributes are always shared
B) Because they are both divine, so they must share all essential attributes of divinity
C) Because accidents are more numerous
D) Because the Quran says so

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument demonstrates that the existence of two gods is logically impossible because any distinction between them would be accidental and removable, leading to the absurd conclusion that two could become one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that any distinction between gods is accidental and removable. Consider the things you treat as 'gods' in your life—your career, relationships, or desires. What distinguishes them in your heart from the One God? Are those distinctions essential or removable? Could you imagine them being removed, and what would that leave? Challenge yourself: identify one such accidental attachment and commit to removing it this week.
?
If it is absurd for two gods to become one, what about your own heart's divisions? You claim to worship one God, but your heart runs after many masters. The proof dismantles multiplicity in divinity—can it also dismantle the multiplicity in your devotion? List three things you prioritize as if they were essential. Are they really necessary for your life, or are they accidents you could let go of? Choose one to detach from this week.
?
The argument uses pure logic to affirm tawhid. But does your belief in tawhid change how you live? When you face a conflict between your desires and Allah's command, do you act as if there is one God or many? Commit to one specific instance this week where you will choose obedience over desire, proving to yourself that He alone is worthy.

Two Meanings of Corruption in the Tawhid Argument
Explains two meanings of 'corruption' in the argument from universal order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two meanings of 'corruption' in the kalam argument against multiple gods and why each leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
The argument against multiple gods hinges on what we mean by 'corruption' — either non-formation or disorder.

This passage explores the second step of the classical argument for tawhid. After establishing the universe's order, theologians consider what would happen if there were two gods. They analyze the concept of 'corruption' in two ways, showing that both lead to absurdity. Let's study how Sanusi unpacks these meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the two distinct parts signaled by the phrases 'فإن أريد بالفساد عدم التكون' and 'وإن أريد بالفساد الخروج عما هما عليه من النظام'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Locate the two definitions of 'corruption': non-formation (عدم التكون) and deviation from order (الخروج عن النظام).
3
Follow the first interpretation
Understand the three possibilities for how two powers could create: combined, each individually, or one alone. See why each is absurd.
The phrase 'والكل باطل' sums up the refutation.
4
Follow the second interpretation
See how conflict and domination between two gods would lead to disorder and destruction of harmony.
Note the term 'الالتئام' (harmony) which makes the whole like a single person.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Both interpretations show the impossibility of multiple gods. Ask yourself why this strengthens the tawhid argument.
This passage is a key part of the burhān al-tamānu' (proof of mutual hindrance).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أريد بالفساد عدم التكون، فتقديره أنه لو تعدد الإله لم تتكون السماء والأرض، لأن تكونهما إما لمجموع القدرتين أو بكل منهما أو بأحدهما، والكل باطل. أما الأول : فلأن من شأن الإله كمال القدرة. وأما الآخران فلما مر. وإن أريد بالفساد الخروج عما هما عليه من النظام فتقديره أنه لو تعدد الإله لكان بينهما التنازع والتغالب، وتميز صنع كل عن صنع الآخر بحكم اللزوم العادي، فلم يحصل بين أجزاء العالم هذا الالتئام الذي باعتباره صار الكل بمنزلة شخص واحد، ويختل النظام الذي به بقاء الأنواع وترتب الآثار.
If by corruption is meant non-formation, then its supposition is that if there were multiple gods, the heavens and earth would not have come into being, because their coming into being is either by the combined two powers, or by each of them, or by one of them, and all are false. As for the first: because it is the nature of a god to have perfect power. And as for the other two: due to what has passed. And if by corruption is meant deviation from the order they are upon, then its supposition is that if there were multiple gods, there would be conflict and domination between them, and each one's work would be distinguished from the other's by virtue of habitual necessity, so there would not occur within the parts of the world this harmony by virtue of which the whole becomes like a single person, and the order by which species endure and effects follow would be disrupted.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage provides two interpretations of 'corruption' (فساد) in the argument against multiple gods. First, corruption is understood as non-formation (عدم التكون). If there were two gods, the universe would not exist because their combined action would be redundant, or each acting individually would be unnecessary, or only one acting would make the other superfluous. Each possibility is refuted. Second, corruption is understood as deviation from order (الخروج عن النظام). If there were two gods, they would conflict, leading to disorder and destruction of the harmony that makes the world function as a single organism. Both interpretations show the impossibility of multiple gods.

Key Terms
الفساد
عدم التكون
النظام
التنازع والتغالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against multiple gods only relies on observing order in the universe.
Correction: The argument also analyzes the meaning of 'corruption' to show that multiple gods would either prevent creation (non-formation) or disrupt order (deviation), both of which are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what would happen if two gods combined their powers to create?
A) The universe would be created perfectly.
B) Each power would be superfluous.
C) They would conflict.
D) The creation would be delayed.

Key Takeaway
The argument against multiple gods relies on two meanings of 'corruption' — non-formation and disorder — both of which lead to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire universe depends on the absolute oneness of its Creator to avoid corruption, what does that say about the things you treat as 'lords' in your life — your desires, your career, your reputation? How many gods do you serve without realizing it?
?
The argument shows that even a slight deviation from order would collapse the whole system. Think of one area of your life where you have allowed a 'second god' — something you obey besides Allah. How has that already begun to corrupt the harmony He designed for you?
?
When you witness the harmony of creation — your own body, the seasons, the stars — do you truly let that awe translate into exclusive worship? Or do you acknowledge His oneness with your tongue while your heart still bends to other masters?

The Logical Impossibility of Two Gods: Agreement or Disagreement
The sixth proof against two gods by division of agreement/disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hypothesis of two gods is rationally impossible by analyzing the two scenarios of their agreement or disagreement.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot exist because their actions would either be redundant or mutually exclusive.

Imagine two gods. What would happen if they both tried to create the same thing? Or if they disagreed? This short but powerful argument uses simple logic to show that either scenario leads to an absurdity, proving that only one God can exist. Let's examine the author's reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a hypothesis followed by two branches.
The passage uses the terms التوارد (concurrence) and التمانع (mutual hindrance).
2
Identify the two branches of the division
The author divides the possibilities for two gods into agreement or disagreement. Find where each branch is introduced and understand the consequence drawn.
The keywords 'فإن اتفقا' and 'وإن اختلفا' mark the two branches.
3
Analyze the consequence of agreement
If the two gods agree on every action, both are performing the same act. This is called التوارد (concurrence) – a redundancy that is unnecessary and therefore irrational for perfect beings.
4
Analyze the consequence of disagreement
If they disagree, each would oppose the other's will, leading to التمانع (mutual hindrance). This results in either both being incapable or one prevailing without a reason (الترجيح بلا ترجح) – both impossible for a god.
This branch actually contains two sub-possibilities: both incapable or one incapable with arbitrary preponderance.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Since both branches lead to absurdity, the original hypothesis (two gods) is false. Ask yourself: why is this argument considered a strong proof in Islamic theology?
This argument relies on the principle of non-contradiction and the perfection of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : لو وجد إلهان. فإن اتفقا على إيجاد كل مقدور لزم التوارد وإن اختلفا لزم مفاسد التمانع أعني عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما مع الترجيح بلا ترجح.
The sixth aspect: If there were two gods. If they agreed on bringing into existence every power, then concurrence would be necessary, and if they disagreed, then the evils of mutual hindrance would be necessary, meaning their incapability or the incapability of one of them with preponderance without a preponderating factor.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This is a proof by division. The author considers two possibilities for two hypothetical gods. First, if they agree on every action, then both are performing the same act, leading to 'concurrence' (التوارد) which is redundant and unnecessary, contradicting divine wisdom. Second, if they disagree, then each would hinder the other's will, resulting in mutual incapability (التمانع) or one prevailing without any reason (الترجيح بلا ترجح), which is irrational. Both outcomes are absurd, so the hypothesis of two gods is false.

Key Terms
التوارد
التمانع
الترجيح بلا ترجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two gods could possibly agree harmoniously and thus coexist.
Correction: The argument shows that agreement leads to redundant concurrence (both doing the same thing), which is absurd because it implies unnecessary repetition, contradicting divine wisdom and perfection.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if two gods disagree on a single action?
A) They both act independently.
B) One of them must be incapable.
C) The action is performed perfectly.
D) They reconcile.

Key Takeaway
The hypothesis of two gods is rationally impossible because it leads either to redundant concurrence or mutual hindrance, both absurd for divine beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as if there is another power that can ultimately oppose Allah's will? For example, when you fear people's opinions more than His command, you are effectively assuming another authority—a form of 'disagreement' that this proof shows is impossible. Will you repent from that subtle shirk today and seek His forgiveness?
?
The proof shows that two gods cannot exist even in theory. Yet, do you sometimes behave as if you yourself control outcomes independently of Allah? The Seventh Aspect: Distinguishing Attribute Must Be Accidental
Shows that what distinguishes two gods cannot be essential, leading to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute that distinguishes multiple gods cannot be essential, and how this leads to an absurd consequence that disproves polytheism.

Core Idea
What distinguishes gods cannot be essential — it must be accidental, and that's impossible.

This lesson presents a key argument from classical kalam against the possibility of multiple gods. Imam al-Sanusi shows that if there were two gods, their distinguishing feature would have to be accidental, which leads to a contradiction. Let's work through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the concise logical structure.
The passage is short but dense; each word matters.
2
Identify key terms
Learn the three key terms: التمايز (distinction), اللازم (necessary concomitant), العارض (accident). Understand their meanings in this context.
These terms are foundational in kalam logic.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument from 'if multiple gods' to 'impossible unity'. Write out the steps in your own words.
The chain is: premise → necessary vs accidental → accident separation → absurd conclusion.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Ask yourself: Why is 'unity of two' impossible? Because it violates the law of non-contradiction — two distinct things cannot be one.
This shows the argument relies on rational axioms.
5
Address a common misconception
Some might think the distinguishing attribute could be essential. The passage explains why it cannot: both gods share divinity, so any necessary concomitant would also be shared.
This misconception is directly refuted by the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع : لو تعدد الإله فما به التمايز لا يجوز أن يكون من لوازم الإلهية ضرورة اشتراكهما ، بل من العوارض فيجوز مفارقتها فترفع الاثنينية، فيلزم جواز وحدة الاثنين وهو محال.
The seventh aspect: If there were multiple gods, then that by which they are distinguished cannot be a necessary concomitant of divinity due to the necessity of their sharing it, but rather an accident, so it is possible for it to separate, thereby removing the duality, thus the unity of two becomes possible, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This proof argues that if two gods exist, they must be distinct in some attribute. This attribute cannot be a necessary concomitant (لازم) of divinity because both share divinity, so it must be an accident (عارض). Accidents are separable, meaning the attribute could cease, making the two gods identical, thus duality would collapse into unity. But unity of two is impossible, so the assumption of two gods is false.

Key Terms
التمايز
اللازم
العارض
الاثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinguishing attribute could be an essential property unique to each god.
Correction: If it were essential, both gods would share divinity, so any essential attribute must be common. Hence it cannot be essential — it must be accidental.

Checkpoint
Why can't the distinguishing attribute be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of divinity?
A) Because both gods share divinity.
B) Because accidents are separable.
C) Because necessity requires oneness.
D) Because the attribute is unknown.

Key Takeaway
If multiple gods were possible, their distinguishing attribute would have to be accidental, but accidents are separable, leading to the impossible conclusion that two could become one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before this lesson, did you ever imagine that there could be two gods who simply have different roles or specialties? Now that you see this logical proof, how does it expose the impossibility of that thought? Will you let this proof settle your heart when doubts about Allah's oneness arise?

If the distinguishing attribute between supposed gods must be accidental, then any 'god' that can lose its uniqueness is not truly God. Is there any part of your life where you act as though there are other 'gods' — things that you treat as essential and inseparable from your happiness, but which are actually accidents that could leave? How can you realign your reliance solely on Allah, who has no separable attributes?
?
This argument shows that two cannot become one. But in your own life, are there areas where you try to combine the worship of Allah with the pursuit of worldly approval or status? Realise that such a combination is impossible in the sight of the One God. What practical step will you take today to eliminate any 'duality' in your intentions?

The Eighth Aspect: Sufficiency of One God and Refutation of Objection
Argues one God is sufficient and refutes objection about pre-eternal proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that one God suffices for creation, and how kalam scholars respond to the objection that absence of proof in pre-eternity would also negate God.

Core Idea
One God suffices; a partner cannot have proof, so it must be denied.

This passage presents a key argument from the Ash'ari tradition affirming the oneness of God. It argues that since one God is sufficient, there is no need for a second divinity—and moreover, no rational proof could ever establish a partner. The passage addresses a common objection regarding the absence of proof in pre-eternity, offering two subtle responses that clarify the nature of rational evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the main claim
Read the opening sentence: the One is sufficient, no proof for a second, so it must be negated. Notice the warning about absurdities that would follow if a second existed.
2
Understand the objection
The objection says: God was in pre-eternity with no proof, so the same logic would require negating God. Identify the objection clearly.
The objection is a common challenge to the proof from contingency.
3
Analyze the two answers
There are two distinct answers. First: the rule applies to what we have no proof for, and we do have proof for God (through creation). Second: the rule applies to what cannot possibly have proof; God's proof is established in time, whereas a partner cannot have any proof at all.
Notice the subtle distinction between 'no proof for us' and 'impossible to have proof'.
4
Examine the hypothetical proof for a partner
The passage argues that any possible proof for a partner would be either pre-eternal (invalid) or temporal (circular). Reflect on why this refutes the possibility of a partner.
This section uses a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثامن : أن الواحد كاف ولا دليل على الثاني، فيجب نفيه، وإلا لزم جهالات لا تحصى مثل كل موجود تبصره اليوم غير الذي كان بالأمس ونحو ذلك فإن قيل : كان الله في الأزل ولا دليل عليه . وأجيب بأن المراد أن ما لا دليل لنا عليه يجب علينا نفيه . ولنا دليل على وجوده في الأزل. وقد يجاب : بأن المراد أن ما لا يمكن أن يقوم عليه دليل يجب نفيه . والله الواحد قد قام عليه الدليل فيما لا يزال، وإن لم يمكن في الأزل بخلاف الشريك، فإنه لو كان عليه دليل، فإما أزلي وهو باطل، لأنه لا يلزم افتقاره إلى المؤثر بل لا يجوز عند المتكلمين. وإما حادث وهو لا يستدعي مؤثراً ثانياً، ولا يخفى ضعفه بل ضعف هذا المأخذ.
The eighth aspect: That the One is sufficient and there is no proof for the second, so it is necessary to negate it, otherwise innumerable absurdities would follow, such as every existent that you see today is not the one that was yesterday and the like. If it is said: God was in pre-eternity and there is no proof for Him. The answer is that what is meant is that what we have no proof for, it is obligatory upon us to negate it. And we have proof for His existence in pre-eternity. And it has also been answered: that what is meant is that that for which a proof cannot possibly be established must be negated. And the One God has had proof established for Him in the future (what will be), even if not possible in pre-eternity, unlike the partner, because if there were a proof for it, either it is pre-eternal, which is false because it does not necessitate its need for an effect - rather it is impermissible according to the theologians - or it is temporal, and it does not require a second effect, and its weakness is not hidden, rather the weakness of this approach.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof and its defense. The core argument: one God suffices for creation; there is no evidence for a second, so we must deny it. The objection: in pre-eternity, there was no proof for God either, so the same logic would deny Him. Two answers are given. First: the rule applies to what we have no proof for (ما لا دليل لنا عليه), and we do have proof for God (through creation), though perhaps not in pre-eternity itself. Second: the rule applies to what cannot possibly have proof (ما لا يمكن أن يقوم عليه دليل). The one God's proof is established in time (through creation), even if not in pre-eternity, whereas a partner cannot have any proof: if a proof were possible, it would be either pre-eternal (invalid because it implies the partner needs an effect) or temporal (which would require a second effect, circular). Thus the partner must be denied.

Key Terms
كاف
دليل
نفي
الأزل
الشريك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That absence of proof for God in pre-eternity means God should be denied on the same grounds as a partner.
Correction: The rule for negation is either 'no proof for us' (and we have proof for God) or 'impossible to have proof' (God's proof is established in time, while a partner's proof is impossible).

Checkpoint
What is the first answer to the objection that there was no proof for God in pre-eternity?
A) Pre-eternity is not a valid time.
B) The rule applies only to what we have no proof for, and we have proof for God.
C) God does not need proof.
D) The objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The oneness of God is rationally necessary; a second divinity cannot have any rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Limitation of Number Argument
Argues that multiple gods cannot be limited to a specific number, then critiques the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth argument against multiple gods and why the author considers it weak due to a flawed premise.

Core Idea
Multiple gods cannot be limited to any number, but the proof relies on a flawed premise.

This lesson examines the ninth aspect presented in the text: that multiple gods cannot be confined to a specific number. The author presents an argument and then immediately notes its weakness, teaching us the importance of critical evaluation in theological reasoning. Understanding both the argument and its critique is essential for a balanced view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a conditional consequence, a reference to previous proofs, and a final critique.
The passage is compact—each phrase carries weight.
2
Understand the logical flow
The argument states: if there were multiple gods, they could not be limited to a specific number, because each would be equally ancient (no primacy). But then it says the consequence (unlimited number) is false because all existents are finite. So multiplicity is impossible.
The key link is that multiple co-eternal beings cannot be naturally bounded.
3
Note the author's critique
The author explicitly says 'its weakness has already been mentioned'—referring to a previous criticism that the proof that all existents are finite is itself weak. This shows that even classical theologians engaged in self-critique.
This is a valuable lesson: no argument is accepted uncritically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه التاسع : إنه لا أولية لعدد دون عدد فلو تعدد لم ينحصر في عدد واللازم باطل لما سبق من الأدلة على تناهي كل ما دخل تحت الوجود وقد سبق ضعفه.
The ninth aspect: There is no primacy for a number without a number, so if they were multiple, they would not be confined to a number, and the consequent is false due to the previous proofs on the finitude of everything that comes under existence, and its weakness has already been mentioned.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This proof argues that if there were multiple gods, they would not be limited to a specific number, because each would be equally ancient and there is no reason to stop at two. But all things that exist are finite (as argued elsewhere), so the claim that there can be an unlimited number of gods is false. However, the author notes that this proof's premise (the finitude of all existents) was previously shown to be weak, so the argument is not strong.

Key Terms
الأولية
الانحصار
التناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a decisive proof against multiple gods within Islamic theology.
Correction: The author explicitly says the proof's foundation (finitude of all existents) has been shown weak, so the argument is not considered strong. Theologians did not rely on it uncritically.

Checkpoint
Why does the author mention that this proof is weak?
A) Because the premise of finitude is already proven weak.
B) Because numbers are not real.
C) Because multiple gods are possible.
D) Because the proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument against multiple gods is presented but its reliance on a previously weakened premise makes it inconclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an argument that supports your existing belief, do you automatically accept it without checking its weaknesses, as the author of this passage does not do? Will you commit today to examine even your strongest held beliefs for logical flaws?
?
The author openly admits the weakness of a premise that could undermine a key proof. Is there an area of your faith where you are clinging to a weak argument because you fear the vulnerability of questioning it? What would it cost you to honestly confront that weakness?
