Proof from Prophethood and Transmitted Evidence
Argues that prophethood and miracles directly prove oneness and refutes a counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the truthfulness of prophets, established by miracles, provides an independent proof of God's oneness, and why the objection that it requires prior philosophical demonstration is mistaken.

Core Idea
Prophets are living proofs of oneness; knowledge does not require prior philosophical demonstration.

This lesson presents the tenth and final proof from the author, which argues that the sending of prophets and their truthfulness as indicated by miracles does not depend on prior rational proof of God's oneness. Therefore, transmitted evidences such as the consensus of all prophets on monotheism and definitive Quranic verses are valid direct arguments. The passage also addresses and refutes a common objection, clarifying a key distinction between existence and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a positive argument, then an objection, then a refutation.
The author uses a concise, dialectical style.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
Identify the author's main claim: prophethood and miracles can directly prove oneness. Then find the counter-argument that objections rely on.
The objection is that multiplicity implies possibility, so without knowing God is necessary, prophethood cannot be proven.
3
Understand the refutation
Analyze how the author refutes the objection. The key point is the distinction between the existence of a thing and knowledge of its existence.
The error is confusing ontological fact with epistemic requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه العاشر : أن بعثة الأنبياء عليهم السلام وصدقهم بدلالة المعجزات لا يتوقف على الوحدانية، فيجوز التمسك بالأدلة السمعية، كإجماع الأنبياء على الدعوة إلى التوحيد ونفي الشريك وكالنصوص القطعية من كتاب الله تعالى على ذلك. وما قيل : إن التعدد يستلزم الإمكان لما عرف من أدلة التوحيد وما لم يعرف أن الله تعالى واجب الوجود خارج عن جميع الممكنات لم يتأت إثبات البعثة والرسالة ليس بشيء، لأن غايته استلزام الوجوب الوحدة لا استلزام معرفيه معرفتها فضلاً عن التوقف، ومنشأ الغلط عدم التفرقة بين ثبوت الشيء والعلم بثبوته.
The tenth aspect: That the sending of prophets (peace be upon them) and their truthfulness as indicated by miracles does not depend on [the proof of] oneness, so it is permissible to rely on transmitted proofs, such as the consensus of the prophets on calling to monotheism and negating a partner, and such as the definitive texts from the Book of God on that. And what was said: that multiplicity entails [the attribute of] possibility due to what is known from the proofs of oneness, and as long as it is not known that God is necessary of existence, beyond all possible being, the establishment of prophethood and messengership cannot be achieved, is nothing, because its ultimate implication is that necessity entails oneness, not that knowledge of it entails knowledge of oneness, let alone dependence, and the source of the error is the failure to distinguish between the existence of a thing and the knowledge of its existence.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This final proof argues that the prophethood of messengers, established by miracles, does not require prior philosophical proof of God's oneness. Therefore, one can use transmitted evidences (الأدلة السمعية) like the consensus of all prophets on monotheism and clear Quranic verses. The author refutes a counter-argument: that multiplicity implies possibility, and without knowing God is necessary, prophethood cannot be proven. The refutation states that the necessary existence of God implies oneness ontologically, but our knowledge of that oneness is not required for prophethood to be established. The error is confusing the existence of a thing with knowledge of it. In short, we can know God's oneness through prophets even if we had not yet proven it philosophically.

Key Terms
بعثة الأنبياء
المعجزات
الأدلة السمعية
واجب الوجود
ثبوت الشيء والعلم به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That proving prophethood requires prior philosophical proof of God's oneness.
Correction: Prophethood and miracles are independent proofs; the error is confusing the existence of oneness with our knowledge of it.

Checkpoint
What is the main error in the counter-argument according to the author?
A) It assumes multiplicity is impossible.
B) It confuses the existence of a thing with knowledge of it.
C) It denies miracles.
D) It relies on transmitted proofs.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood, attested by miracles, stands as an independent proof of God's oneness, and the objection that it requires prior knowledge is a confusion of ontology and epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson says prophethood itself is a sufficient proof—do you truly rely on the message of the prophets as your foundation, or do you subconsciously demand philosophical certainty before you are convinced? Examine your heart: if all rational arguments were stripped away, would you still be certain of tawhid because the prophets spoke it?

The prophets unanimously called to tawhid, yet many people reject their message. Have you internalized their call so deeply that it transforms how you live every moment? Or do you treat prophethood as a historical fact rather than a living, authoritative witness? Today, will you let the testimony of the prophets override your own doubts and desires?
?
The author warns against confusing the existence of truth with your knowledge of it. Is your spiritual confidence based on the objective reality of God’s oneness, or on your subjective understanding and ability to defend it? If you discovered a flaw in your own reasoning, would your faith crumble? How can you anchor your certainty in the unchanging truth of prophethood rather than your fluctuating intellect?

Two answers on negating what has no proof
Two answers to an objection about proving God's oneness from eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two theological responses to the objection that God's oneness cannot be proven from pre-eternity, and the rationale behind each.

Core Idea
Proof for God's oneness exists in time, but negating the partner is still required.

Theologians faced an objection: if we cannot prove God's oneness from pre-eternity, how can we negate a partner? The author presents two distinct answers. The first focuses on having proof in pre-eternity; the second shifts to the possibility of proof. Let's examine both.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains two separate answers introduced by 'وأجيب' and 'وقد يجاب'.
2
Identify the two answers
Find the first answer (starting with 'وأجيب') and the second answer (starting with 'وقد يجاب'). Note how each answer defines the obligation to negate.
The first answer says we have proof in pre-eternity; the second says God is proven in time.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each answer
For the first answer, note that it asserts we have proof for God's existence in pre-eternity. For the second, it argues that a partner cannot be proven either eternally (because that would require no effect, which is rejected) or temporally (because that doesn't necessitate a second effect).
The author hints at the weakness of the second answer.
4
Reflect on the author's critique
Notice the author's comment about the weakness of the second approach. This shows scholarly debate within the tradition.
Consider why the first answer might be seen as stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأن المراد أن ما لا دليل لنا عليه يجب علينا نفيه ولنا دليل على وجوده في الأزل.
وقد يجاب بأن المراد أن ما لا يمكن أن يقوم عليه دليل يجب نفيه والله الواحد قد قام عليه الدليل فيما لا يزال وإن لم يمكن في الأزل بخلاف الشريك فإنه لو كان عليه دليل فإما أزلي وهو باطل لأنه لا يلزم افتقاره إلى المؤثر بل لا يجوز عند المتكلمين وإما حادث وهو لا يستدعي مؤثراً ثانياً ولا يخفى ضعفه بل ضعف هذا المأخذ.
And it is answered that the intended meaning is that whatever we have no proof for, we must negate it. And we have a proof for His existence in pre-eternity. And it may be answered that the intended meaning is that whatever cannot be proven by a proof must be negated. And the One God has been proven by proof in what does not cease, although it was not possible in pre-eternity, contrary to the partner, for if there were a proof for him, it would either be eternal, which is false because he would not necessarily need an effect—rather it is not permissible according to the theologians—or temporal, which does not require a second effect, and its weakness is not hidden, rather the weakness of this approach.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct answers to an objection that the oneness of God cannot be proven from eternity. The first answer states that the intended meaning is that anything for which we have no proof must be negated, and we do have proof for God's existence in pre-eternity. The second answer elaborates: anything that cannot be supported by a proof must be negated. God as one has been proven by proof in the temporal realm (what does not cease), even if not in pre-eternity. For a partner, if there were proof, it would either be eternal (which is false because a partner would not necessarily need an effect, and theologians reject that) or temporal, which would only require a single effect and not a second. The author notes the weakness of this second approach.

Key Terms
الدليل
النفي
الأزل
الشريك
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a proof is not available in pre-eternity, then God's oneness cannot be proven at all.
Correction: The author clarifies that proof for God's oneness exists in the temporal realm (ما لا يزال), even if not in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what must we do with something that has no proof?
A) Accept it as possible
B) Negate it
C) Suspend judgment
D) Seek a proof

Key Takeaway
The author provides two responses to the objection, ultimately affirming that God's oneness is provable even if not from pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face doubts about your faith, do you demand eternal, unassailable proof before believing, or do you accept temporal evidence as sufficient? How does this lesson challenge your standard of certainty?
?
In your daily life, are there beliefs or actions you negate without examining whether they can be proven? What would it take for you to apply the principle of this lesson—only negating what cannot be proven—to your own doubts?
?
This lesson presents two structured answers to an objection. Have you ever prepared a reasoned defense of your beliefs? What is stopping you from doing so, and what would change if you did?

The Ninth Argument: Multiplicity Cannot Be Limited
The ninth argument against polytheism: multiplicity cannot be limited

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ninth argument against polytheism, which contends that multiplicity of gods would lead to an unconfined number, contradicting the finitude of existence.

Core Idea
If multiple gods existed, they could not be limited to a finite number—but existence is finite.

This lesson examines the ninth argument presented in the text against the possibility of multiple gods. The argument builds on the idea that numbers require a starting point, so a multiplicity of gods would be infinite. However, the author acknowledges a prior criticism of this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the compact style; the argument is presented in a single sentence.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into premise (no beginning for a number → multiplicity cannot be limited) and conclusion (the consequent is false because existence is finite).
The author uses the term 'اللازم' (necessary consequent).
3
Reflect on the author's note
The passage ends with 'and its weakness has already preceded.' Consider why this argument might be considered weak despite its logical form.
The prior criticism may relate to the assumption that existence is finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه التاسع إنه لا أولية لعدد دون عدد فلو تعدد لم ينحصر في عدد واللازم باطل لما سبق من الأدلة على تناهي كل ما دخل تحت الوجود وقد سبق ضعفه.
The ninth aspect: There is no beginning for a number without a number. So if there were multiplicity, it would not be confined to a number. And the consequent is false due to what preceded of the proofs on the finitude of everything that falls under existence, and its weakness has already preceded.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument for oneness. It states that there is no 'firstness' for a number apart from number; that is, any number requires a starting point. If there were multiple gods, they would form a set that cannot be limited to a specific number (since any finite number would be arbitrary). But everything that exists is finite, as proven earlier. Therefore, multiplicity is impossible. The author notes that the weakness of this argument has already been mentioned (referring to a prior critique).

Key Terms
العدد
التعدد
التناهي
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the necessary consequence (اللازم) that the author identifies as false?
A) That multiplicity would be infinite
B) That multiplicity would not be confined to a number
C) That numbers have no beginning
D) That everything falls under existence

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument claims that multiple gods would be infinite, contradicting the finitude of existence, but the author notes its weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this argument is weak and even the author admits its weakness, on what do you base your certainty in Allah's oneness? Is your belief built on a collection of arguments, each possibly flawed, or on a deeper certainty from faith? What would you do if you found that one of your foundational beliefs about God relied on a weak argument?
?
The author points out the weakness of this argument. Are you willing to critically examine the reasons for your own beliefs, even if it means discarding a favorite proof? How would that affect your relationship with Allah?
?
This argument tries to prove God's oneness through logical deduction. But does your conviction in tawheed come from logic alone, or from something more direct? If your logical proofs were shown to be weak, would your faith remain? Why?

The Meaning of Tawheed: Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna
The author clarifies the reference of tawheed and distinguishes the emphases of Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common core of tawheed agreed upon by Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, despite their differing emphases.

Core Idea
Tawheed centers on the unity of the Necessary Being, not on sectarian differences.

In this passage, the author clarifies what tawheed really means: the oneness of the Necessary Being in essence. He then compares the emphases of two major theological schools—Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna—and shows that despite their different focal points, they agree on the fundamental truths. This lesson will help you see past apparent disagreements to the shared foundation of Islamic monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the compact style: each sentence adds a distinct point.
Pay attention to the key terms: التوحيد, الواجب لذاته, القديم, الخالق.
2
Identify the three parts
The passage has a clear structure: first a general definition of tawheed, then the specific emphases of Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, and finally their common ground. Try to divide the passage into these three parts mentally.
The word 'ف' (fa) introduces each new statement.
3
Analyze the agreements and differences
Reflect on why the Mu'tazila focus on negating multiplicity of the eternal, while Ahl al-Sunna focus on negating multiplicity of the creator. What does each emphasis protect against?
The Mu'tazila were concerned with any other eternal beings (like the Quran), while Ahl al-Sunna were concerned with any other creators.
4
Summarize the shared core
The final sentence lists three things all agree on: the Necessary Being, the one worthy of worship, and the creator of bodies. This is the essence of tawheed. Write it down in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمرجع التوحيد عندهم إلى وحدة الواجب لذاته لا غير. فالمعتزلة إنما يبالغون في نفس تعدد القديم. وأهل السنة في نفي تعدد الخالق. والكل متفقون على نفي تعدد الواجب والمستحق للعبادة، والموجد للجسم.
For the reference of tawheed according to them is to the unity of the Necessary Being in itself, nothing else. For the Mu'tazila only exaggerate in [negating] multiplicity of the eternal. And Ahl al-Sunna [exaggerate] in negating multiplicity of the creator. And all are agreed on negating multiplicity of the Necessary Being, the one worthy of worship, and the creator of the body.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of tawheed (monotheism) and shows how different theological schools express it. The author begins by stating that the reference (مرجع) of tawheed according to them (i.e., the theologians) is the unity of the Necessary Being (الواجب لذاته) in itself, nothing else. Then he explains that the Mu'tazila place great emphasis on negating any multiplicity in the eternal (القديم), meaning they reject any other eternal entity besides God. Ahl al-Sunna, on the other hand, focus on negating multiplicity of the creator (الخالق), meaning they reject any other creator. Both groups, however, agree on negating multiplicity of the Necessary Being, the one deserving worship, and the one that brings bodies into existence.

Key Terms
التوحيد
الواجب لذاته
القديم
الخالق
المستحق للعبادة
الموجد للجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It might be thought that Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna disagree fundamentally on tawheed.
Correction: The passage shows that they agree on the core: the Necessary Being is one, worthy of worship, and creator. Their differences are about which aspect of that unity to emphasize, not about the essence of tawheed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what do both the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna agree upon?
A) That God is the only creator of bodies
B) That the eternal is one
C) That the Necessary Being is one and alone worthy of worship
D) That the creator is one

Key Takeaway
Tawheed, at its core, is the affirmation that Allah alone is the Necessary Being, worthy of worship, and creator, despite differences in emphasis between theological schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly worship Allah as the Necessary Being, or do you sometimes associate partners with Him in your heart by relying on created means as ultimate causes? Commit to examining one attachment today that you treat as necessary.
?
Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna agreed on core tawheed despite differences. Where in your life do you let secondary disputes distract you from the essential unity of purpose in worshipping Allah alone? What step can you take to refocus?
?
If tawheed means affirming Allah's unique necessity, what does that imply about your own self-sufficiency? In which area of your life are you living as if you are the necessary being? Repent and realign.

The Dualists' Argument and Its Refutation
The author presents and refutes the dualist argument for two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dualists' argument that good and evil require two separate origins and the author's refutation by denying the entailment and properly defining the terms 'good' and 'evil'.

Core Idea
Good and evil can come from one source if we properly understand the terms.

In this passage, the author addresses the dualist and Magian belief in two gods—one for good and one for evil. He presents their argument and then refutes it by clarifying the nature of attributing good and evil. An objection is also raised, though its full answer may require additional context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dualist and Magian beliefs
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the dualist claim that light/good and darkness/evil come from separate origins.
The Magians name the gods Yazdan and Ahriman.
2
Examine the dualists' argument
They argue that one origin cannot be both good and evil. Why do they think this is impossible?
3
Study the refutation
The author denies the entailment by defining 'good' as one whose good predominates, and 'evil' as one whose evil predominates. Thus the same being can be described as good even if it creates evil.
The refutation uses 'منع اللزوم'.
4
Consider the objection
The objection (عورض) raises a dilemma: if the good being cannot stop evil, it is weak; if it can but doesn't, it is evil. The author does not answer here; think about possible responses.
The Mu'tazila argument about Iblis is mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشركون فمنهم الثنوية القائلون بأن للعالم الهين نور هو مبدأ الخيرات، وظلمة هو مبدأ الشرور. ومنهم المجوس القائلون بأن مبدأ الخيرات هو (يزدان) ومبدأ الشرور هو (أهرمن) واختلفوا في أن (أهرمن) أيضاً قديم أو حادث من (يزدان) وشبهتهم أنه لو كان مبدأ الخير والشر واحد لزم كون الواحد خيراً وشريراً وهو محال . والجواب : منع اللزوم إن أريد بالخير من غلب خيره، وبالشر من غلب شره، ويمنع اللازم إن أريد خالق الخير وخالق الشر في الجملة . غاية الأمر أنه لا يصلح إطلاق الشرير لظهوره فيمن غلب شره . وعورض بأن الخير إن لم يقدر على دفع الشرير أو الشرور فعاجز، وإن قدر ولم يفعل فشرير، وإن جعل إبقاؤها خيراً لما فيه من الحكم والمصالح الخفية كما تزعم المعتزلة في خلق إبليس وذريته وأقداره وتمكينه من الإغواء، فلعل نفس خلق الشرور والقبائح أيضاً، كذلك، فلا يكون شراً وسفهاً، ومنهم عبدة الملائكة، وعبدة الكواكب وعبدة الأصنام، أما عبدة الملائكة والكواكب فيمكن أنهم اعتقدوا كونها مؤثرة في عالم العناصر مدبرة لأمور قديمة بالزمان ، شفعاء العباد عند الله تعالى مقربة إياهم إليه تعالى وأما الأصنام فلا خفاء في أن العاقل لا يعتقد فيها شيئاً من ذلك .
And as for the polytheists, among them are the Dualists who say that there are for the world two gods: light which is the origin of good, and darkness which is the origin of evil. And among them are the Magians who say that the origin of good is Yazdan and the origin of evil is Ahriman, and they disagreed whether Ahriman is also eternal or originated from Yazdan. And their argument is that if the origin of good and evil were one, it would necessitate that the one be good and evil, which is impossible. And the answer: denial of the entailment if by 'good' is meant one whose good predominates, and by 'evil' one whose evil predominates, and the necessary consequence is denied if what is meant is the creator of good and the creator of evil in general. The upshot is that the term 'evil' is not applicable because it is used for one whose evil predominates. And it was objected: if the good is not able to repel the evil or evils, then it is incapable, and if it is able but does not act, then it is evil, and if it makes their preservation good because of the hidden wisdoms and benefits as the Mu'tazila claim regarding the creation of Iblis and his progeny and his decrees and his empowerment to mislead, then perhaps the very creation of evils and abominations is likewise, and then it would not be evil and foolish. And among them are the worshippers of angels, and the worshippers of stars, and the worshippers of idols. As for the worshippers of angels and stars, it is possible that they believed that they are effective in the world of elements and managers of affairs, ancient in time, intercessors for servants with God, bringing them near to Him. And as for the idols, there is no concealment that the rational person does not believe any of that regarding them.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist polytheism. The author first lists the groups among polytheists: the Dualists (الثنوية) who posit two gods, light and darkness, and the Magians (المجوس) who posit Yazdan and Ahriman. They argue that if one source produced both good and evil, that source would have to be both good and evil, which is impossible. The response is twofold: first, the entailment is denied if by 'good' is meant one whose good predominates, and by 'evil' one whose evil predominates; second, the necessary consequence is denied if what is meant is the creator of good and evil in general. The term 'evil' is not applied to one whose good predominates. Then an objection (عورض) is raised: if the good being cannot repel evil, it is incapable; if it can but does not, it is evil. The Mu'tazila's example of Iblis is cited, where God's allowing Iblis to mislead is considered good due to hidden wisdoms. The objection suggests that the same could be said for the creation of evils, so they would not be evil. The passage then transitions to discussing other polytheists: angel-worshippers and star-worshippers, who believed these beings are effective in the world and intercessors. Finally, the author notes that no rational person would attribute such powers to idols.

Key Terms
الثنوية
المجوس
يزدان
أهرمن
الخير
الشر
اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that one being cannot create both good and evil without being contradictory.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'good' and 'evil' are applied based on predominance; a single being can be good even if it creates evil, as long as its good predominates.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the dualists' argument that one source cannot be both good and evil?
A) By claiming that the dualists misunderstood the nature of good and evil
B) By denying the entailment and explaining that 'good' means one whose good predominates
C) By arguing that there are actually three origins
D) By quoting scriptures

Key Takeaway
The dualists' argument is refuted by denying the entailment and redefining the terms 'good' and 'evil' based on predominance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly felt that evil in your life comes from a source other than Allah, as if God is not fully in control? How does this passage's refutation of dualism challenge you to reaffirm tawheed even in hardship? What will you do this week when a difficult situation tempts you to think otherwise?
?
When you see evil in the world, does your faith in Allah's goodness waver? This passage teaches that terms like 'good' and 'evil' are defined by predominance and hidden wisdoms. Is there an area of your life where you are judging Allah's actions as 'evil' because you can't see the hidden good? Will you repent from that judgment and trust His wisdom today?
?
The dualists saw a contradiction in one God creating both good and evil. Are there any contradictions in your understanding of Allah that you have not resolved? This passage shows that apparent contradictions can be resolved by refining definitions and trusting divine wisdom. What step can you take this week to deepen your understanding of Allah's attributes instead of falling into confusion?

Other Polytheists: Angel-Worshippers, Star-Worshippers, and Idol-Worshippers
The author lists other polytheist groups and comments on their beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beliefs of angel-worshippers, star-worshippers, and idol-worshippers, and why the author considers idol-worship to be irrational.

Core Idea
Only angels and stars can be considered intercessors, but idols are clearly powerless.

After refuting the dualists, the author turns to other polytheistic groups who worship angels, stars, and idols. He explains the possible rationale behind the first two groups but dismisses idol-worship as irrational.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups
Find the three groups mentioned: عبدة الملائكة, عبدة الكواكب, عبدة الأصنام.
2
Understand the beliefs of angel and star worshippers
The author says they believed these beings are effective in the elemental world, manage affairs, are ancient, and serve as intercessors with God.
This is presented as a plausible belief, not necessarily rejected.
3
Consider the case of idol-worshippers
The author states that no rational person would believe such things about idols. Why?
Idols are man-made objects, clearly powerless.

Source Text

?

?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
No Union or Indwelling in the Necessary Being
First proof against indwelling with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against indwelling (ḥulūl) in the Necessary Being and how the author refutes the objection about mixture.

Core Idea
To be dependent is to be contingent; the Necessary Being depends on nothing.

After establishing that the Necessary Being cannot be united with another, the author turns to the more subtle claim of indwelling. The first proof argues that anything dwelling in something else is dependent on it, which contradicts the self-sufficiency required for necessary existence. An objection about mixture is answered, showing it ultimately implies dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two topics
Notice that the author first dismisses union (ittiḥād) briefly, then turns to indwelling (ḥulūl) with a proof.
The dismissal of union references an earlier proof.
2
Understand the core of the first proof against indwelling
The proof states that a dweller is dependent on its locus. Dependence (iftiqār) on another negates necessity (wujūb).
The author lists several types of indwelling to show the general principle.
3
Examine the objection and the author's response
The objection suggests mixture (e.g., water in rose-water) as a counterexample. The author replies that mixture is a property of bodies and implies divisibility, reducing to body-in-place indwelling, which still involves dependence.
This reinforces the principle that all indwelling implies dependence.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is important: it protects the transcendence of the Necessary Being from any form of dependence on creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : الواجب لا يتحد بغيره، ولا يحل فيه. أما الاتحاد فلما سبق من امتناع اتحاد الاثنين ولأنه يلزم كون الواجب هو الممكن والممكن هو الواجب وذلك محال بالضرورة وأما الحلول فلوجوه : الأول : أن الحال في الشيء يفتقر إليه في الجملة. سواء كان حلول جسم في مكان ، أو عرض في جوهر أو صورة في مادة كما هو رأي الحكماء، أو صفة في موصوف كصفات المجردات والافتقار إلى الغير ينافي الوجوب . فإن قيل : قد يكون حلول امتزاج كالماء في الورد. قلنا : ذلك من خواص الأجسام ومفضي إلى الانقسام، وعائد إلى حلول الجسم في المكان.
The third investigation: The Necessary Being cannot be united with another nor dwell in it. As for union, [it is impossible] due to what preceded about the impossibility of the union of two [things] and because it would necessitate that the Necessary Being is the possible and the possible is the Necessary Being, and that is necessarily absurd. As for indwelling [it is impossible] due to [several] aspects: The first: that which dwells in a thing is in some way dependent upon it, whether it be the indwelling of a body in a place, or an accident in a substance, or a form in matter as is the view of the philosophers, or an attribute in a described entity like the attributes of incorporeal beings, and dependence upon another contradicts necessity. If it is said: Indwelling may be by mixture like water in rose-water. We say: That is a property of bodies and leads to division, and reduces to the indwelling of a body in a place.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage defines the third major topic (al-mabḥath al-thālith) of this section: that the Necessary Being (al-wājib) cannot be united with another nor can It dwell in something (ḥulūl). The author briefly dismisses union (ittiḥād) by referencing a previous proof that union of two would make the Necessary and the possible interchangeable, which is absurd. Then he turns to indwelling (ḥulūl) and presents the first of several proofs. The proof states that anything that dwells in something else is necessarily dependent (muftaqir) on that locus, whether as a body in place, an accident in substance, a form in matter (as per the philosophers), or an attribute in a subject (like the attributes of incorporeal beings). Dependence on another contradicts the self-sufficiency required for necessary existence (wujūb). An objection is raised: what about mixture like water in rose-water? The author replies that mixture is a property of bodies and implies divisibility, ultimately reducing to body-in-place indwelling, which still involves dependence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mixture like water in rose-water is a case of indwelling without dependence.
Correction: The author shows that mixture is a property of bodies and implies divisibility, ultimately reducing to body-in-place indwelling, which still involves dependence.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given in the first proof against indwelling?
A) The Necessary Being cannot be united with another because that would imply two necessary beings.
B) Anything that dwells in something is dependent on it, which contradicts the self-sufficiency of the Necessary Being.
C) Indwelling always implies spatial occupation, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.
D) The philosophers' view of indwelling is inconsistent with divine unity.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against indwelling establishes that any form of dwelling in another necessitates dependence, which is incompatible with the self-sufficiency of the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage shows that anything that dwells in another is dependent. Is there any area of your life where you are 'dwelling' in someone or something other than God, seeking security or identity? What would it mean to relocate your dependence solely on the Necessary Being?
?
If God is beyond all indwelling, then He is not contained by any place or time. How does this affect the way you think about His closeness to you? Are you comfortable with a God who is near without being 'inside' you?

The Second Proof Against Indwelling: A Dilemma
Sanusi presents the second proof against indwelling as a dilemma and then records two objections to each horn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second proof against indwelling (ḥulūl) and the initial objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Indwelling in a locus either makes God dependent or changes His independence—both impossible.

Sanusi presents the second proof against indwelling, structured as a dilemma: if God's indwelling in something is necessary, then He depends on that locus, implying contingency; if it is contingent, then He would be independent of it, but the nature of a dweller demands dependence, leading to a contradiction. Two objections challenge each horn of the dilemma, questioning whether dependence necessarily follows.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma's two horns
Read the proof carefully: either indwelling is necessary (first horn) or contingent (second horn). Note the consequences drawn for each: dependence and contingency on the first, and change from independence to dependence on the second.
The proof aims to show both options lead to absurdities for the Necessary Being.
2
Examine the first objection
The first objection argues that on the necessary horn, dependence does not follow because God's essence could necessitate the locus without needing it, just as a cause necessitates its effects without needing them.
This objection targets the inference from 'necessitating' to 'needing'.
3
Examine the second objection
The second objection denies that a dweller is always dependent, using the analogy of a body dwelling in a space without being essentially dependent on that space.
This challenges the premise that 'the dweller must be dependent on the locus'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لو حل في محل فإما مع وجوب ذلك وحينئذ يفتقر إلى المحل ويلزم إمكانه، وقدم المحل، بل وجوبه ، لأن ما يفتقر إليه الواجب أولى بأن يكون واجباً، وإما مع جوازه وحينئذ يكون غنياً عن الحل . والحال يجب افتقاره إلى المحل فيلزم انقلاب الغني عن الشيء محتاجاً إليه ، هكذا قرره الإمام رحمه الله . ثم اعترض بأنه على التقدير الأول لا يلزم الافتقار لجواز أن توجب ذاته ذلك المحل والمحل الحلول، أو توجب ذاته المحل، والحلول جميعاً، ووجوب اللوازم والآثار عند المؤثر لا يوجب احتياجه إليها . وعلى التقدير الثاني لا يلزم الانقلاب لان لا نسلم أن الحال في الشيء يكون محتاجاً إليه كالجسم المعين بحل في الحيز المعين مع عدم احتياجه في ذاته إليه . وقد تقرر بأنه إذا كان مستغنياً بالذات لزم إمكانه وقدم المحل وهو ظاهر. واعترض : بأن عدم الاستغناء بالذات لا يستلزم الاحتياج بالذات، ليلزم إمكانه فيقدم المحل الجواز أن يكون كل من الغنى والاحتياج عارضاً بحسب أمر خارج . وأجيب بأن عدم مجرد الاستغناء بالذات يستلزم الإمكان (لأن الواجب مستغني بالذات ضرورة ولا حاجة إلى توسيط الاحتياج بالذات، وقد يقرر بأنه إن كان محتاجاً بالذات لزم إمكانه ، وإلا امتنع حلوله . ورد : بأن عدم الاحتياج الذاتي لا ينافي عروض الاحتياج، فلا ينافي الحلول.
The second [aspect]: that if He were to dwell in a locus, then either [the indwelling is] necessary, and in that case He would be dependent on the locus and His contingency would follow, and the priority of the locus, indeed its necessity, because what the Necessary Being depends on is more deserving of being necessary; or [the indwelling is] contingent, and in that case He would be independent of the indwelling. But the dweller must be dependent on the locus, so a change from being independent of the thing to being in need of it would follow. Thus the Imām (may God have mercy on him) formulated it. Then [someone] objected that on the first supposition, dependence does not necessarily follow, because it is possible that His essence necessitates that locus and the locus [necessitates] the indwelling, or His essence necessitates both the locus and the indwelling together, and the necessitation of concomitants and effects by the efficient cause does not entail its need for them. And on the second supposition, the change does not follow because we do not concede that the dweller in a thing is in need of it, like a particular body dwelling in a particular space without being in need of it in its essence. And it was established that if it is independent by essence, its contingency and the priority of the locus follow, and that is apparent. And [someone] objected: that absence of self-sufficiency by essence does not entail need by essence, so that its contingency and the priority of the locus do not follow; rather, it is possible that both self-sufficiency and need are accidental according to an external matter. And it was answered: that mere absence of self-sufficiency by essence entails contingency (because the Necessary Being is self-sufficient by essence necessarily, and there is no need to mediate need by essence). And it may be formulated: if He is needy by essence, His contingency follows; otherwise, His indwelling is impossible. And [the objection] was refuted: that absence of essential need does not conflict with accidental need, so it does not conflict with indwelling.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof against indwelling (ḥulūl) in the Necessary Being, followed by a series of objections and responses. The proof is a dilemma: if God's indwelling in something is necessary, then He depends on that locus, implying contingency (imkān) and that the locus is prior to Him, even necessary; if indwelling is contingent, then God would be independent of it, but the nature of a dweller (ḥāl) demands dependence, so He would change from independent to dependent. The Imām (likely al-Rāzī) formulated this. The first objection argues that on the first horn, necessity does not imply dependence because God's essence could necessitate the locus and the indwelling without needing them—just as a cause necessitates its effects without needing them. The second objection denies that a dweller is always dependent, giving the example of a body dwelling in a space without being essentially dependent on that space. The author then reiterates that if God were independent by essence (mustaghanī bi-l-dhāt), that would imply contingency and locus priority, which is absurd. Another objection says that lack of essential self-sufficiency does not entail essential need, so the conclusion does not follow. The answer asserts that mere lack of essential self-sufficiency entails contingency because the Necessary Being is essentially self-sufficient. Finally, the objection that absence of essential need does not preclude accidental need is dismissed as irrelevant, since accidental need would still conflict with necessary existence.

Key Terms

المحل
الحال
الوجوب
الإمكان
الافتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God's essence necessitates the locus, then He is dependent on it.
Correction: The objection clarifies that necessitating does not entail need; a cause necessitates effects without needing them.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence drawn from the first horn (necessary indwelling)?
A) God would be independent.
B) God would be dependent on the locus, leading to contingency.
C) The locus would be contingent.
D) Indwelling would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The second proof uses a dilemma to show that indwelling (ḥulūl) in God leads to contradictions, but objections challenge the logical steps.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument insists that God cannot depend on anything, even as a dweller in a locus. In your daily life, do you ever act as if God's actions depend on your circumstances or your worthiness? How can you realign your thinking to recognize His absolute independence?
?
The first objection shows that a cause does not need its effects. God causes your existence and your actions. Do you live as though your obedience is needed by God, or do you understand that He is utterly self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized that?
?
The dilemma drives home that God cannot be contained in any place. When you pray or make du'a, do you imagine God in a particular direction or space, or do you address the One who is beyond all spatial limitations? How can your prayer reflect this truth starting today?

Defending the Proof: Self-Sufficiency and Need
Sanusi presents a restatement, an objection about self-sufficiency, the response, and a final refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection regarding essential self-sufficiency is refuted and why accidental need does not affect the proof.

Core Idea
Lack of essential self-sufficiency entails contingency, even without essential need.

After presenting the initial objections, Sanusi continues with a clarification that if God were independent by essence, that would imply contingency and locus priority. An objection is raised that absence of self-sufficiency does not entail need by essence. The response affirms that mere lack of essential self-sufficiency implies contingency, because the Necessary Being is essentially self-sufficient. Finally, a refutation of another objection shows that accidental need does not conflict with essential independence, but it still cannot apply to the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the restatement of the proof
Read the passage beginning 'وقد تقرر' which reaffirms that if God is independent by essence, that would imply contingency and the priority of the locus. This sets up the next objection.
This is a recap before the new objection.
2
Analyze the objection about self-sufficiency
The objection claims that lack of essential self-sufficiency does not entail essential need, so the inference to contingency is invalid. The contingency may be avoided if self-sufficiency and need are accidental.
The key is: does absence of self-sufficiency necessarily imply need?
3
Study the response
The answer states that mere absence of essential self-sufficiency entails contingency because the Necessary Being is necessarily self-sufficient by essence. There is no need to mediate via essential need.
The response bypasses the need for essential need, using a direct inference.
4
Examine the final refutation
The last part dismisses the objection that absence of essential need does not preclude accidental need. The refutation shows that accidental need still conflicts with necessity, so it does not affect the proof.
This closes the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third and Fourth Proofs Against Indwelling
Sanusi presents the third and fourth proofs against indwelling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments against the doctrine of indwelling (ḥulūl) based on the perfection of God and the impossibility of spatial localization for the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Indwelling either implies imperfection or dependence – either way, it is impossible for God.

After establishing that indwelling (ḥulūl) is impossible because it would entail contingency, the author now presents two more direct proofs. The third proof uses a disjunction: either indwelling is not a perfection (so it must be negated from God) or it is a perfection (so God would be perfected by another, which is absurd). The fourth proof argues that indwelling necessarily means spatial localization, which is impossible for the non-corporeal Necessary Being. Together, these proofs reinforce the transcendence of Allah from any form of indwelling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that each proof is introduced with an ordinal (third, fourth).
2
Analyze the third proof's disjunction
The third proof is a simple either/or: either indwelling is not a perfection (so it must be negated from God) or it is a perfection (so God would be perfected by another, which is impossible). Identify both halves and think about why each leads to the same conclusion.
The term 'istithmār' (perfection) is key.
3
Analyze the fourth proof's definition of indwelling
The fourth proof argues that indwelling, by universal rational consensus, means obtaining in a spatial locus (taḥayyuz). Since the Necessary Being cannot be localized, indwelling is impossible. Notice how the author relies on the agreed-upon meaning of the term.
The phrase 'al-maʿqūl min al-ḥulūl' indicates the universally understood meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الحلول في الغير إن لم يكن صفة كمال، وجب نفيه عن الواجب، وإن كان لزم كون الواجب مستكملاً بالغير وهو باطل وفاقاً. الرابع : أنه لو حل في شيء لزم تحيزه . لأن المعقول من الحلول باتفاق العقلاء هو حصول الفرض في الحيز تبعاً لحصول الجوهر.
The third [aspect]: that indwelling in another, if it is not a perfection, then its negation from the Necessary Being is obligatory, and if it is [a perfection], then it would necessitate the Necessary Being being perfected by another, and that is false by agreement. The fourth: that if He were to dwell in something, it would necessitate His being localized. Because the intelligible meaning of indwelling according to the consensus of rational people is the obtaining of a ‘farḍ’ in a space following the obtaining of a substance.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the third and fourth proofs against indwelling (ḥulūl). The third proof is a simple disjunction: either indwelling is not a perfection (kamāl), in which case it must be negated from the Necessary Being because imperfection is impossible for Him; or it is a perfection, in which case the Necessary Being would acquire perfection from another, implying dependence and imperfection in His own essence, which is universally rejected (bāṭil ittifāqan). The fourth proof argues that indwelling necessarily entails being localized (taḥayyuz), because the universally understood meaning of indwelling is the occurrence of an individual (farḍ) in a spatial locus (ḥayyiz) consequent upon the occurrence of a substance. Since the Necessary Being is not a body and cannot be localized, indwelling is impossible. This proof relies on the consensus of rational people (al-ʿuqalāʾ) that indwelling implies spatial occupation.

Key Terms
الحلول
الكمال
الواجب
الاستكمال بالغير
التحيز
الحيز
الجوهر
الفرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that indwelling could be a perfection without implying need.
Correction: The third proof shows that even if indwelling were a perfection, God would still be perfected by another, contradicting His self-sufficiency and thus is false.

Checkpoint
What is the central claim of the fourth proof against indwelling?
A) Indwelling implies that God is imperfect because He needs a place.
B) Indwelling, by universal consensus, means occupying a spatial locus, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.
C) Indwelling is only possible for bodies, and God is not a body.
D) Indwelling leads to multiplicity in the divine essence.

Key Takeaway
Indwelling is impossible for the Necessary Being because it either implies imperfection or dependence, and it entails spatial localization which contradicts His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine Allah as being 'in' the sky or 'in' your heart in a literal, spatial way? After reading this lesson, how does realizing that indwelling implies localization challenge that imagination? What does this mean for how you should conceive of Allah's closeness (ma‘iyya) without spatial containment?
?
The third proof shows that if indwelling were a perfection, Allah would be perfected by another—which is impossible. Reflect on your acts of worship: have you ever thought that your prayer or good deeds add to Allah's glory? How does this proof compel you to purify your intention and recognize that Allah is already absolutely perfect, needing nothing from you?
?
The fourth proof relies on the consensus of rational people that indwelling means spatial occupation. In a time when many spiritual traditions describe divine presence in material forms, how does this lesson guard you against falling into anthropomorphic concepts of God? Commit to correcting any mental images that confine Allah to a place.

The Doctrine of Absolute Existence (Wahdat al-Wujud)
Author presents claims of wahdat al-wujud doctrine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key claims of the monistic doctrine of existence (wahdat al-wujud) as presented by the author.

Core Idea
Wahdat al-wujud reduces all multiplicity to illusion.

In this passage, the author presents the second doctrinal deviation: the claim that only absolute existence is real, and all diversity is merely relational and apparent. Understanding this doctrine is essential before encountering its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the doctrine
Read the Arabic passage describing the doctrine. Note the central claim that the Necessary Being is absolute existence, one without any multiplicity.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'وجود مطلق', 'إضافات', 'تعينات', and 'مظاهر'. Understand how they function in the doctrine.
These terms are central to the monistic worldview.
3
Understand the denial of multiplicity
Notice that the doctrine says multiplicity exists only in relationships and determinations, which are like illusions. Everything in reality is one.
4
Recognize the implications for hulul and ittihad
Because there is no real multiplicity, there can be no true indwelling (hulul) or union (ittihad) since both require duality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الواجب هو الوجود المطلق ، وهو واحد لا كثرة فيه أصلاً، وإنما الكثرة في الإضافات والتعينات التي هي بمنزلة الخيال والسراب، إذ الكل في الحقيقة واحد يتكرر على مظاهر لا بطريق المخالطة، وبتكثره في النواظر، لا بطريق الانقسام، فلا حلول ها هنا، ولا اتحاد لعدم الاثنينية والغيرية وكلامهم في ذلك طويل خارج عن طريق العقل والشرح . وقد أشرنا في بحث الوجود إلى بطلانه ولكن من يضلل الله فما له من هاد.
The second: that the Necessary Being is absolute existence, and He is one with no multiplicity at all; rather, multiplicity is only in the relationships and determinations that are like illusion and mirage, for everything in reality is one, repeated in manifestations not by way of mixing, and multiplied in the viewers not by way of division. So there is no indwelling here, nor union, because there is no duality or otherness. Their discourse on this is lengthy, outside the way of reason and revelation. And we have already pointed out its falsehood in the discussion of existence. But whomever God leads astray, there is no guide for him.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
The author describes the second doctrine: wahdat al-wujud. It claims that only absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) is real; all multiplicity is merely relational and illusory. Since there is no real multiplicity, there is no subject for hulul or ittihad. The author dismisses this view as not only false but outside the purview of both reason ('aql) and sacred law (shar'). He notes he has already refuted it in his earlier discussion on existence, and ends with a Quranic verse implying that those who persist are led astray by God.

Key Terms

إضافات
تعينات
مظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the doctrine of wahdat al-wujud, why is there no hulul (indwelling) or ittihad (union)?
A) Because God and creation are separate.
B) Because there is no real multiplicity to allow for union.
C) Because union is impossible.
D) Because the doctrine denies God's existence.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of wahdat al-wujud asserts that only absolute existence is real; all distinctions are illusory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
According to this passage, your very existence as a distinct being is merely an 'illusion and mirage.' Have you ever considered that your most private moments of worship—your tears, your whispered pleas—are directed not to a separate Lord but to a reality that includes you? How does that thought make you feel, and what does your feeling reveal about your belief in the absolute distinction between God and His creation?
?
The doctrine denies 'indwelling' and 'union' because 'there is no duality or otherness.' If you were to truly accept this, would it change the way you ask Allah for help? Would you still feel need, or would you realize that the one asking and the one being asked are the same? Tonight, test your heart: when you say 'You alone we worship,' do you truly feel that He is wholly Other?

Refutation of Wahdat al-Wujud
Author refutes wahdat al-wujud and dismisses it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of wahdat al-wujud and his categorical dismissal of it.

Core Idea
The author rejects wahdat al-wujud as false and outside reason and revelation.

After presenting the doctrine, the author offers a decisive rebuttal. He declares that such discourse is outside the domain of both rational inquiry and sacred law, and he reminds the reader of a previous refutation. A Quranic warning underscores the gravity of this error.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage

Identify the author's verdict
The author says the doctrine is 'خارج عن طريق العقل والشرح' — outside the way of reason and revelation. This is a strong rejection.

He mentions that he already pointed out its falsehood in the discussion of existence, indicating this is not a new argument.
4

The final phrase 'لكن من يضلل الله فما له من هاد' is a Quranic verse, implying that those who persist in this error have been led astray by God.

The Mu'tazila's Two Proofs Against Divine Power
Presents the Mu'tazila claim and their two proofs against divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments the Mu'tazila used to deny that God has power (qudra) to create bodies.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that if God had power, it would be just like human power—impotent to create.

The Mu'tazila challenge the orthodox view that God is omnipotent by arguing that power (qudra) is a single reality; if human power cannot create bodies, then any power, including God's, cannot. They present two proofs based on analogy and common causality. Let's explore these arguments carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
Notice the term 'علة مشتركة' (common cause) appears multiple times.
2
Identify the first proof
The first proof argues that the unsuitability of human power to create bodies is a common judgment that must have a common cause, which is 'being power'. If God has power, it shares this cause, so it too would be unsuitable.
3
Identify the second proof
The second proof uses an analogy: God's power either resembles human power or differs. If it resembles, it shares the same judgment; if it differs, the difference is not greater than differences among human powers, which remain incapable. Hence, God's power also cannot create bodies.
Look for the contrast between 'تماثل' and 'مخالفة'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The Mu'tazila conclude that God cannot have power. Consider why this argument would be problematic for orthodox theology.
This forms the basis for the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعاء المعتزلة نفي القدرة عن الله تعالى

قال : ولهم في نفي القدرة.
أنه لو كانت له قدرة لما تعلقت بخلق الأجسام لأن قدرة الشاهد ليست كذلك إلا لعلة مشتركة هي كونها قدرة ولأنها إما أن تماثل قدرة الشاهد أو تخالفهـا بقدر تخالفها ) . قلنا : لعل العلة أخص والمخالفة أشد) .

قال : ولهم في نفي القدرة تمسك المعتزلة في امتناع كون البارى تعالى قادراً بالقدرة بأنه لو كان كذلك لما كان قادراً على خلق الأجسام، واللازم باطل وفاقاً ببيان الملازمة من وجهين : أحدهما : أن عدم صلوح قدرة العبد لخلق الأجسام حكم مشترك ، لا بد له من علة مشتركة، وما هي إلا كونها قدرة ، فلو كان للباري أيضاً قدرة، لكانت كذلك. وثانيهما : أن قدرة الباري على تقدير تحققها، إما أن تكون مماثلة لقدرة العباد فيلزم أن لا تصلح لخلق الأجسام، لأن حكم الأمثال واحد، وإما أن تكون مخالفة لها ، وليست تلك المخالفة أشد من مخالفة قدرة العباد بعضها للبعض ، ومع ذلك لا يصلح شيء منها لخلق الأجسام، فكذا التي تخالفها هذا القدر من المخالفة والجواب : أنا لا نسلم أنه لا بد للحكم المشترك من علة مشتركة. بل يجوز أن يعلل بعلل مختلفة، إذ لا يمتنع اشتراك المختلفات في لازم واحد، فها هنا يجوز أن يعلل عدم صلوح قدرة العباد لخلق الأجسام بخصوصياتها .
ولو سلم، فلا نسلم أنه لا مشترك بينها سوى كونها قدرة الجواز أن تكون أمراً أخص من ذلك بحيث تشمل قدرة العباد، ولا تشمل قدرة الباري. ولا نسلم أن مخالفة قدرة الباري لقدرة العباد ليست أشد من مخالفتها فيما بينها لجواز أن تنفرد بخصوصية لا توجد في شيء منها، فتصلح لخلق الأجسام دونها .
The Mu'tazila's claim of negating power from God Almighty. He said: And for them in negating power: That if He had power, it would not be connected to creating bodies, because the power of the witnessed [i.e., human] is not so except due to a common cause which is its being power. And because it either resembles the power of the witnessed or differs from it by the extent of its difference. We said: Perhaps the cause is more specific and the difference is greater. He said: And for them in negating power: The Mu'tazila adhere to the impossibility of the Creator Almighty being powerful by power, because if it were so, He would not be powerful to create bodies, and the consequent is false by consensus. Explanation of the entailment from two aspects: First, that the unsuitability of the slave's power for creating bodies is a common judgment; it must have a common cause, and it is nothing other than its being power. So if the Creator also had power, it would be the same. Second, that the power of the Creator, assuming its realization, either is similar to the power of servants, then it would follow that it is not suitable for creating bodies, because the judgment of similars is one; or it differs from it, and that difference is not greater than the difference among the powers of servants among themselves, yet none of them is suitable for creating bodies. So likewise that which differs by this amount of difference. The answer: We do not accept that a common judgment must have a common cause. Rather, it is permissible to explain it by different causes, since it is not impossible for different things to share a single necessary consequent. So here it is permissible to explain the unsuitability of the servants' powers for creating bodies by their particularities. Even if accepted, we do not accept that there is no commonality among them besides their being power; it is possible that it be something more specific than that, which includes the power of servants and does not include the power of the Creator. And we do not accept that the difference of the Creator's power from the servants' power is not greater than the difference among them [the servants' powers], because it is possible that it is characterized by a particularity not found in any of them, so it is suitable for creating bodies unlike them.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila argument that God cannot have power (qudra) because if He did, it would be incapable of creating bodies, similar to human power. The argument is presented in two proofs: (1) the unsuitability of human power for creating bodies is a common property derived from its being power, so God's power would also be unsuitable; (2) God's power would either be similar to human power (hence unsuitable) or differ, but the difference is no greater than differences among human powers, which still are all unsuitable. The author responds by denying that a common judgment requires a single common cause; different causes can produce the same effect. He also suggests that the common cause could be something more specific than 'being power' that excludes divine power. Additionally, God's power may have a unique particularity making it suitable for creating bodies, unlike any human power. The refutation thus preserves God's omnipotence by rejecting the analogy with human power.

Key Terms
قدرة
علة مشتركة
خلق الأجسام
تماثل
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that all powers, including God's, are fundamentally the same in nature and capacity.
Correction: The Mu'tazila assume that power (qudra) is a univocal concept, but the author later refutes that by showing that different causes can produce similar effects, and divine power may have a unique particularity.

Checkpoint
What is one of the two ways the Mu'tazila argue that God's power cannot create bodies?
A) God's power is unlike human power in every way
B) The unsuitability of human power for creation is due to its being power, so God's power would share that
C) Human power sometimes creates bodies, so it is not a common cause
D) The difference between God's power and human power is greater than among human powers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument hinges on the assumption that all powers share the same nature, which they claim makes creation of bodies impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously measure God's power against your own limitations? What would it mean to truly believe that God's power is unlike anything you know?
?
Reflect on a time you doubted God's ability to do something. Did that doubt come from assuming God operates within the same constraints as humans? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If the Mu'tazila are wrong about the nature of power, what does that say about other assumptions you may hold about God? Are there places in your life where you treat God as if He were limited like you?

Refuting the Mu'tazila: Defending Divine Omnipotence
Presents the author's refutation of the Mu'tazila's two proofs, consisting of three sub-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key refutations of the Mu'tazila's argument, particularly the denial that a common judgment must have a single common cause.

Core Idea
A common effect can arise from different causes; divine power is unique.

The author counters the Mu'tazila by challenging their core assumption: that a shared property (unsuitability for creation) must come from a single cause. He argues that different particularities can produce the same effect, and God's power may have a unique particularity enabling creation. Let's examine the refutation in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'والجواب' and its translation. Note the author's initial denial.
The response begins with 'أنا لا نسلم' (we do not accept).
2
Understand the first refutation
The author denies that a common judgment necessarily implies a single common cause. He says different causes can produce the same effect, so the unsuitability of human powers could be due to their particularities, not a shared essence.
This breaks the link between 'being power' and inability.
3
Understand the second and third refutations
Even if a common cause existed, it could be something more specific that excludes divine power. Also, the difference between God's power and human power could be far greater than any human differences, allowing for a unique particularity that enables creation.
These responses undermine the analogy argument.
4
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how the author preserves God's omnipotence by rejecting the Mu'tazila's assumption of univocal power.
This is a classic Ash'ari response using the concept of 'خصوصية' (particularity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعاء المعتزلة نفي القدرة عن الله تعالى

What is the author's first response to the Mu'tazila's argument?
A) He accepts that a common judgment needs a common cause

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever limit God's power in your heart because you compare Him to human limitations? What specific area of your life do you treat as if it's beyond His ability to change? Commit to praying for that area today, acknowledging His unique power.
?
When you hear arguments that say God cannot do something because it contradicts human logic or experience, how do you respond? Is your trust in God's omnipotence based on His revelation or on human reasoning? Write down one instance where you accepted a human argument over God's power and repent.
?
If God's power is entirely unique and not bound by analogies to creation, how should that transform your prayers? Are you praying small, safe prayers that fit within your understanding of what is possible? Challenge yourself to pray for something that seems impossible to you, trusting in His exclusive particularity (خصوصية) that enables what no one else can do.

intermediate
5 min
Proof of God's Power (Qudra)
Sanusi enumerates three ways to complete the proof of God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power (qudra) is proven from the fact that He is a pre-eternal Maker who produces temporal effects, and the three ways to complete this proof.

Core Idea
God's power is proven because temporal acts cannot come from a necessitating cause.

In this passage, Sanusi lays the foundation for proving God's power (qudra). He argues that if God were a necessitating cause, the universe would have to be eternal along with Him, but since the universe is temporal, God must act by power and will. He then outlines three ways to establish this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the clear argument structure.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that the foundational principle for proving God's power is that He is a pre-eternal maker with a temporal making.
3
Understand the three ways
The passage lists three methods to complete the proof: the theologians' approach, the opponent's agreement, and refutation of the philosophers' claim.
These correspond to the three conditions for attributing temporal events to God directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل المعول عليه في باب إثبات قادرية الباري أنه صانع قديم له صنع حادث، وصدور الحادث عن القديم إنما يتصور بطريق القدرة دون الإيجاب. وإلا فيلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام علته، حيث وجدت في الأزل العلة دون المعلول، ولا يتم هذا إلا بعد إثبات أن شيئاً من الحوادث يستند إلى الباري بلا واسطة، وذلك بأن يبين أنه قديم بذاته وصفاته وأن العالم حادث بجميع أجزائه على ما قرره المتكلمون، أو يبين امتناع أن يكون موجباً بالذات، ويكون في سلسلة معلولاته لأنه قديم مختار يستند إليه الحوادث، وهذا مما وافقنا عليه الخصم، أو حركة سرمدية يكون جزئياته الحادثة شروطاً ومعدات في حدوث الحوادث على ما زعمت به الفلاسفة. وقد سبق في بحث التسلسل بيان وجود ما لا نهاية لها مجتمعة كانت أو متعاقبة. وفي بحث حدوث العالم بيان استحالة أزلية الحركة. قال إمام الحرمين رحمه الله تعالى: دخول حوادث لا نهاية لها لإمدادها على التعاقب في الوجود معلوم البطلان بأوائل العقول. وكيف ينصرم بالواحد على أثر الواحد ما انتفت عنه النهاية، كالدورات التي قبل هذه الدورة التي نحن فيها على ما تزعم الملاحدة من أن العالم لم يزل على ما هو عليه، ولم تزل دورة قبل دورة إلى غير أول، ووالد قبل ولد، وبذر قبل زرع، ودجاجة قبل بيضة. وهذا بخلاف إثبات حوادث لا آخر لها، كنعيم الجنان، فإنه ليس قضاء بوجود ما لا يتناهى. وهكذا كما إذا قال: لا أعطيك درهماً إلا أعطيك قبله ديناراً، أو لا أعطيك ديناراً إلا أعطيك قبله درهماً، فلم يتصور أن يعطيه على حكم شرطه درهماً ولا ديناراً. بخلاف ما إذا قال: لا أعطيك درهماً إلا أعطيك بعده ديناراً، ولا أعطيك ديناراً إلا أعطيك بعده درهماً.
Then the foundational principle relied upon in the section of proving the power of the Creator is that He is a pre-eternal Maker with a temporal making, and the emanation of the temporal from the pre-eternal is only conceivable by way of power, not necessitation. Otherwise, it would necessitate the effect's failure to follow from its complete cause, since the cause existed in pre-eternity without the effect. And this is only complete after proving that some temporal events are attributed to the Creator without mediation. This is done by showing that He is pre-eternal in essence and attributes and that the world is temporal in all its parts, as the theologians established, or by showing the impossibility that He be a necessitating essence while being in the chain of His effects, because He is a pre-eternal willing agent to which temporal events are attributed—this is something we agree with the opponent upon—or that there be an eternal motion whose temporal particulars are conditions and preparations in the occurrence of temporal events, as the philosophers claimed. And it has preceded in the discussion of infinite regress the demonstration of the existence of what has no end, whether simultaneous or successive. And in the discussion of the temporal origination of the world, the demonstration of the impossibility of the pre-eternity of motion. Imam al-Haramayn, may God have mercy on him, said: The occurrence of events with no end supplying one after another into existence is known to be false by the first principles of the intellects. How can what has no end be exhausted by one after another, like the cycles before this cycle we are in, as the heretics claim that the world has never ceased being as it is, and there has never ceased a cycle before a cycle with no beginning, and a parent before a child, and a seed before a crop, and a hen before an egg. This is different from proving events with no end, like the bliss of Paradise, for it is not a decree of existence of what is infinite. Likewise, if someone says, 'I will not give you a dirham unless I give you a dinar before it,' or 'I will not give you a dinar unless I give you a dirham before it,' then it is inconceivable that he should give him, on the basis of his condition, either a dirham or a dinar. This is contrary to if he says, 'I will not give you a dirham unless I give you a dinar after it,' and 'I will not give you a dinar unless I give you a dirham after it.'
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the key proof for God's power (qudra) based on His being a pre-eternal agent producing temporal acts. The author argues that if God were a necessitating cause (mūjib), the effect would have to be co-eternal with the cause, but since the world is temporal, God must be a powerful agent who acts by will. The author states that this proof requires establishing that some temporal events are directly from God without intermediary. This can be achieved by showing that God is pre-eternal and the world is temporal (as the theologians proved), or by showing that God cannot be a necessitating cause because He is a willing agent to whom events are attributed (which even the opponents accept), or by refuting the philosophers' claim of eternal motion whose temporal parts are conditions for further events. The author references earlier discussions on the impossibility of an infinite regress and the pre-eternity of motion. Then a quote from Imam al-Haramayn illustrates the impossibility of an infinite series of past events: if there were an infinite number of cycles before the present, it would be impossible to traverse one after another, like the example of cycles, parents, seeds, and hens. He contrasts this with an infinite future (like Paradise) which is not actualized all at once, but is successive. The example of conditional giving of a dirham and dinar shows that a condition of pre-existence leads to an impossible regress, while a condition of post-existence is possible because future events can succeed indefinitely.

Key Terms
قادرية
بارئ
صانع قديم
صنع حادث
إيجاب
تخلف المعلول
حركة سرمدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be a necessitating cause (mūjib) and still create a temporal world.
Correction: The passage argues that if God were a necessitating cause, the effect (the world) would have to be co-eternal with the cause, contradicting its temporality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would necessarily follow if God were a necessitating cause (mūjib) instead of a powerful agent?
A) The world would be temporal.
B) The effect would be co-eternal with the cause.
C) God would not be pre-eternal.
D) The world would not be temporal.

Key Takeaway
God's power is proven from the fact that He is a pre-eternal maker producing temporal effects, which cannot be explained by necessitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's act were necessitated, you would be a mere puppet. But this lesson proves He acts by power and will. When you sinned last, did you truly believe God could have prevented it but chose not to? How does that change your repentance? This week, confess to God that you acknowledge His absolute power over your actions.
?
Sanusi says temporal events are attributed to God without mediation. Think of the last 'coincidence' in your life. Did you attribute it to chance or to God's direct power? Starting today, write down one event each day that you can only trace back to God's hand, and thank Him for His direct involvement.
?
The refutation of the philosophers shows that God's power is not limited by secondary causes. Are there areas of your life where you rely more on natural laws than on God's power? Identify one situation where you will consciously ask God to intervene directly, beyond means.

Refutation of Infinite Past Events
Imam al-Haramayn refutes infinite past events and contrasts them with infinite future events using analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series of past events is impossible, while an infinite future series is possible, using the arguments of Imam al-Haramayn and the analogy of conditional giving.

Core Idea
An infinite past is impossible because it would require completion of an endless chain.

After establishing God's power, Sanusi turns to refute the possibility of an infinite number of past events, a claim made by materialists and philosophers. He presents the argument that an endless series of successive events cannot be exhausted, contrasting it with the infinite future of Paradise. The analogy of conditional giving illustrates the logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the quote from Imam al-Haramayn and the examples.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage asserts that an infinite series of past events is impossible because it would require completing an endless sequence.
3
Contrast past and future infinity
Understand why infinite future (like Paradise) is possible while infinite past is not.
Future events never complete, but past events have been completed successively.
4
Analyze the analogies
Examine the cycles example and the dirham-dinar analogy to see how a condition of pre-existence leads to impossibility.

?

?

?
