God as a Free Agent, Not a Necessary Cause
Author argues God is a direct, free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimūn argue that God must be a free agent (mukhtār) rather than a necessary cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt) for the existence of originated events.

Core Idea
God's direct action requires freedom, not necessity.

The argument for God's existence often hinges on explaining how originated events come to be. The mutakallimūn emphasize that if God were merely a necessary cause, the world would be eternal, contradicting its originated nature. In this passage, the author outlines two approaches—either demonstrating God's eternality and the world's origination, or showing the impossibility of God's being a necessary cause—and concludes that an eternal free agent is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the two methods introduced with 'أو' (or).
The passage is part of a larger argument.
2
Identify the two approaches
Find the phrases describing Method A (showing God eternal and world originated) and Method B (showing impossibility of God as necessary cause).
Method B is emphasized more.
3
Understand the key contrast
Contrast 'mūjib bi al-dhāt' (necessary cause) with 'mukhtār' (free agent). The author says if God were a necessary cause, events would be eternal, but since events are originated, God must be a free agent.
The opponent agrees with this conclusion.
4
Reflect on the agreement
Note that the author says the opponent (philosophers) agrees that God is a free agent. This is a point of common ground.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستند إلى الباري بلا واسطة، وذلك بأن يبين أنه قديم بذاته وصفاته وأن العالم حادث بجميع أجزائه على ما قرره المتكلمون أو يبين امتناع أن يكون موجباً بالذات، ويكون في سلسلة معلولاته لأنه قديم مختار يستند إليه الحوادث، وهذا مما وافقنا عليه الخصم، أو حركة سرمدية يكون جزئياته الحادثة شروطاً ومعدات في حدوث الحوادث على ما زعمت به الفلاسفة. وقد سبق في بحث التسلسل بيان وجود ما لا نهاية لها مجتمعة كانت أو متعاقبة. وفي بحث حدوث العالم بيان استحالة أزلية الحركة.
It is based on the Necessary Being (al-Bāri) without intermediate, by showing that He is eternal in essence and attributes, and that the world is originated in all its parts as the mutakallimūn established, or by showing the impossibility of His being a necessary cause in the chain of His effects, because He is an eternal free agent to whom originated things are attributed—and this is what the opponent agrees with us on—or [by showing the impossibility of] an eternal perpetual motion whose originated parts are conditions and preparatory causes for the occurrence of originated things according to what the philosophers claimed. It has preceded in the discussion of infinite regress (al-tasalsul) the proof of the existence of what has no end, whether collectively or sequentially. And in the discussion of the originatedness of the world, the proof of the impossibility of the eternity of motion.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is part of a larger argument establishing God's power and the world's originatedness. The author states that the proof relies on showing either that God is eternal and the world is originated (the mutakallimūn's approach) or that God cannot be a necessary cause in the chain of His effects. He explains that if God were a necessary cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt), then originated things would have to be eternal, which is impossible. Instead, God is an eternal free agent (qadīm mukhtār) to whom originated events are attributed. The opponent agrees on this point. Alternatively, the philosophers claim that there is an eternal perpetual motion (ḥaraka sarmadiyya) whose originated parts serve as conditions and preparatory causes for the occurrence of originated events. This view is rejected because it entails an infinite regress of events without a beginning. The author reminds us that earlier discussions on infinite regress (tasalsul) and the originatedness of the world have already demonstrated the impossibility of an eternal motion.

Key Terms
باري (al-Bārī)
قديم (qadīm)
حادث (ḥādith)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi al-dhāt)
مختار (mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power is like a natural cause, necessary and automatic.
Correction: The mutakallimūn emphasize that God's agency is voluntary, not necessary, because the world is originated and cannot be the necessary effect of an eternal cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second method in the passage?
A) To prove that God is a necessary cause
B) To prove that God is a free agent directly causing events
C) To refute the mutakallimūn
D) To prove the eternity of motion

Key Takeaway
God's agency is free and direct, not necessary; otherwise the world would be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's action is direct and free, not mediated by any necessary cause, then what does that mean for the 'causes' you rely on daily – your job, health, relationships? Are they truly independent, or is God the only real agent behind them? Will you consciously attribute every outcome to His direct will today?
?
The passage says even the philosophers agree that God cannot be a necessary cause. Yet many people live as if the world runs on autopilot – natural laws, habits, routines – forgetting that each moment is sustained directly by God's free choice. Is there a part of your life where you act as if God is bound by a 'system' rather than being absolutely free? What would it look like to surrender that area to His immediate control?
?
God brings about events directly without intermediaries. But you often blame intermediaries for your frustrations – a person, a circumstance, yourself. When you fail or suffer, do you truly trace the cause back to God as the one who willed it? Or do you attribute agency to created things? What would change in your heart if you saw every event as a direct divine appointment?

Refutation of Perpetual Motion
Author refutes philosophers' perpetual motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' concept of an eternal perpetual motion (ḥaraka sarmadiyya) is impossible according to the mutakallimūn.

Core Idea
Eternal motion leads to infinite regress, which is impossible.

The philosophers proposed that an eternal perpetual motion serves as an intermediate between God and events, with its originated parts acting as conditions for new events. The mutakallimūn refute this by invoking earlier proofs: the impossibility of an actual infinite and the impossibility of an eternal motion. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the part of the passage describing the philosophers' view of perpetual motion (ḥaraka sarmadiyya).
They claim its originated parts are conditions for events.
2
Identify the refutation sources
The author references two earlier discussions: tasalsul (infinite regress) and ḥudūth al-ʿālam (origination of the world).
These provide the logical basis for rejection.
3
Understand the problem of infinite regress
If motion is eternal, its parts form an infinite series. The proof of tasalsul shows that an actual infinite (whether simultaneous or sequential) is impossible.
4
Connect to the eternity of motion
The second reference shows that an eternal motion is impossible because the world is originated.
This reinforces the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in the discussion of the originatedness of the world, the proof of the impossibility of the eternity of motion.

The author reminds us that earlier discussions on infinite regress (tasalsul) and the originatedness of the world have already demonstrated the impossibility of an eternal motion.

Key Terms
حركة سرمدية (ḥaraka sarmadiyya)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
أزلية الحركة (azaliyyat al-ḥaraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an eternal motion could be possible as a chain of events without a beginning.
Correction: The mutakallimūn argue that an actual infinite series is impossible (proof of tasalsul) and that motion cannot be eternal because the world is originated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the discussion of tasalsul demonstrate?
A) That an infinite regress is possible
B) That something with no end exists whether collectively or sequentially
C) That the eternity of motion is possible
D) That the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
Eternal motion is impossible because it entails an actual infinite and contradicts the world's origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever live as if the world runs on its own momentum, forgetting that every motion, breath, and event is directly created by Allah from nothing? What practical step can you take today to break that illusion?
?
Consider your deepest attachments—your career, relationships, or health. Do you treat them as independent causes in your life, like the philosophers' perpetual motion? How would your heart change if you saw them purely as Allah's direct creation at every moment?
?
The passage shows that an eternal chain of events is impossible. What does this imply about your own reliance on 'secondary causes'? Are there patterns where you depend on created things as if they were self-sustaining? What is one concrete change you can make to rely solely on Allah?

Objection and Simplified Proof of God's Power
Objection and initial response on the simplified proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the simplified four-case argument for God's power and why the author considers it to add unnecessary premises.

Core Idea
Simplified arguments often hide hidden assumptions.

The passage begins with an objection: the proof for God's power can be simplified to four possibilities without needing previous details. The author responds that this formulation, though common, actually adds unnecessary premises (three conditionals). This lesson examines that objection and initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. It presents four cases that would follow if God were not powerful: no events, uncaused event, infinite regress, or delayed effect.
Notice the fourth case involves an eternal being causing an event without intermediate.
2
Read the author's response
The author says this is just the well-known argument with extra premises. Identify the 'three conditionals' mentioned.
3
Reflect on unnecessary premises
Consider why the author considers these premises unnecessary. The response indicates that the discussion is about the capability of the eternal being, and the first two cases have identical antecedents and consequents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : قد يمكن تقرير هذا الاستدلال بحيث لا يفتقر إلى أحد الأمرين المذكورين، كما ذكر في المواقف من أنه لو لم يكن قادراً لزم إما نفي الحادث أو عدم استناده إلى المؤثر، أو التسلسل، أو تخلف الأثر عن المؤثر التام لأنه إن لم يوجد حادث أصلاً فهو الأمر الأول. وإن وجد فإن لم يستند إلى مؤثر فهو الثاني، وإن استند فإن لم ينته إلى قديم فهو الثالث، وإن انتهى فلا بد من قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة دفعاً للتسلسل وهو الرابع، لأنا نقول هذا أيضاً تقرير الاستدلال المشهور بزيادة مقدمات لا حاجة إليها وهي الشرطيات الثلاث. الأول : لأن الكلام في قادرية القديم الذي إليه ينتهي الكل، مع أن التالي في كل من الأولين عين المقدم، ولذا عدل عنه وقال : وإن شئت قلت : أي في تقرير هذا الاستدلال، لو كان الباري موجباً بالذات لزم قدم الحادث، إذ لو حدث لتوقف على شرط حادث وتسلسل، ثم إنه لا يتم إلا بما ذكرنا على ما اعترف به حيث قال: واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال يغني على التقديرين لا يتم إلا إن تبين حدوث ما سوى الله تعالى، وامتناع قيام حوادث متعاقبة لا نهاية لها بذاته أو تبين في الحادث اليومي أنه لا يستند إلى حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية محفوظاً بحركة دائمة، وذلك لأنه لو لم يتبين ما ذكر لم تصح الشرطية الرابعة من التقرير الأول، ويلزم المحال المذكور في التقرير الثاني لجواز أن تنتهي الحوادث إلى قديم يوجب قديماً يستند إليه الحوادث بطريق الاختيار دون الإيجاب، فلا يلزم التخلف ولا التسلسل، وأن لا يثبت قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة، بل يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بآخر من غير بداية كما هو رأيهم في الحركات ولا يكون هذا من التسلسل المسلم استحالته، أعني ترتب العلل والمعلولات لا إلى نهاية فلا بد من بيان استحالة النوع الآخر من التسلسل أعني كون كل حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية ليتم به الاستدلال.
It cannot be said: This argument can be formulated in a way that does not need either of the two mentioned matters, as mentioned in al-Mawāqif—that if He were not powerful, then either there would be no originated event, or it would not be traced to an effect, or there would be infinite regress, or the effect would be delayed from the complete cause. For if there is no originated event at all, that is the first case. If there is, and it is not traced to an effect, that is the second case. If it is traced but does not end with an eternal being, that is the third case. If it ends, then there must be an eternal being that causes an originated event without an intermediate to avoid infinite regress, which is the fourth case. We say: This is also a formulation of the well-known argument but with unnecessary premises—three conditionals. First, because the discussion concerns the capability (qādariyya) of the eternal being to which everything ultimately ends, whereas the consequent in each of the first two is identical to the antecedent. Therefore he (i.e., the author of al-Mawāqif) departed from it and said: If you wish, you can say: In the formulation of this argument, if the Necessary Being (al-Bārī) were a necessary cause, then the originated would be eternal, because if it occurred, it would depend on an originated condition and thus infinite regress. Then it is not complete except with what we have mentioned, as he acknowledged where he said: Know that this argument, on both suppositions, is not complete unless it is established that everything other than God is originated, and that it is impossible for an infinite series of sequential originated events to subsist in His essence, or it is established that the daily originated event does not trace back to an originated event preceded by another without end, preserved by a perpetual motion. That is because if what was mentioned were not established, then the fourth conditional in the first formulation would be invalid, and the absurdity mentioned in the second formulation would follow—since it is possible that originated events end with an eternal being that causes an eternal thing to which originated events are traced by choice (ikhtiyār) rather than necessitation (ījāb), so that neither delay nor infinite regress would be necessitated; and that an eternal being causing an originated event without intermediate is not established, but rather every originated event is preceded by another without a beginning, as is their view regarding motions. And this is not the kind of infinite regress whose impossibility is accepted, namely the ordering of causes and effects without end. Therefore, it is necessary to establish the impossibility of the other type of infinite regress—namely, every originated event being preceded by another without end—to complete the argument.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objection proposes a simplified version of the argument from al-Mawāqif: if God is not powerful, then we get four possibilities: (1) no originated events, (2) originated events without a cause, (3) an infinite regress, or (4) delay of effect from the complete cause. The objection claims this does not require the earlier mentioned details. The author responds that this formulation still has unnecessary premises (three conditionals) and does not avoid the need to prove the impossibility of an infinite regress of events without a beginning. He further explains that the argument's completion requires establishing two things: (a) that everything other than God is originated, and (b) that it is impossible for an infinite series of sequential originated events to subsist in God's essence, or that the daily originated event does not trace back to a chain without beginning. If these are not established, then the fourth conditional might be invalid because one could conceive of an eternal being that causes an eternal thing from which originated events proceed by choice (ikhtiyār), not by necessitation (ījāb), thus avoiding the standard infinite regress of causes but still allowing for an infinite regress of events without a beginning. This latter type of regress (every event preceded by another without beginning) is a different kind of infinite regress whose impossibility must be proven separately. Thus, the objection's simplified proof is insufficient.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi al-dhāt)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
علة (ʿilla)
معلول (maʿlūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a simplified argument avoids all difficulties.
Correction: The author shows that even the simplified argument adds unnecessary premises and does not avoid the need for deeper analysis.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the simplified argument?
A) It is completely new and avoids all problems
B) It is the well-known argument with three unnecessary conditionals
C) It proves God's power without any assumptions
D) It is invalid because it leads to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The objection's simplified proof is merely the familiar argument with added conditionals, not a genuine simplification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a simplified proof for a profound truth about Allah, do you accept it readily, or do you pause to examine the hidden assumptions? What might you miss by settling for a surface-level understanding?
?
The objection here claims to present a more efficient argument, but the author shows it is redundant. Are there areas of your faith where you have accepted 'simplified' explanations without checking their soundness? How can you cultivate intellectual rigor without losing sincerity?
?
This passage deals with the attribute of power (qādir). Reflect on a time when you doubted Allah's power in a specific situation. Did you have an underlying assumption that blocked your trust? How does understanding the logical necessity of Allah's power challenge that assumption today?

Two Types of Infinite Regress in Proving God's Power
The author refines the proof and distinguishes two types of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of infinite regress and why the argument for God's power requires establishing the impossibility of an infinite regress of events without a beginning.

Core Idea
Not all infinite regresses are equal; causal regress and event regress require separate proofs.

The author now refines the argument. He explains that the proof is not complete unless two things are established: that everything other than God is originated, and that an infinite regress of sequential originated events cannot subsist in God's essence. He also distinguishes between the impossible causal infinite regress (cause-effect chain without end) and another type of regress where every event is preceded by another without a beginning, which requires separate proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative formulation
The author presents another way to state the argument: if the Necessary Being were a necessary cause, then the originated would be eternal, leading to infinite regress. Notice the condition about a 'originated condition' (sharṭ ḥādith).
2
Identify the two required establishments
The argument is incomplete without two things: (1) everything other than God is originated, (2) the impossibility of an infinite series of sequential originated events subsisting in God's essence, OR that the daily originated event does not trace back to an infinite chain.
These are parallel requirements.
3
Understand the two types of infinite regress
The author distinguishes between the classical infinite regress of causes (tarattub al-ʿilal wa al-maʿlūlāt lā nihāya) and another type: every originated event preceded by another without beginning (kull ḥādith mawāqif bi-ākhar lā nihāya). The latter is not automatically impossible and must be proven separately.
This is crucial for completing the argument.

Source Text

The Objection and the Unnecessary Premises
Taftazani presents an objection with four cases and its initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of an objection to the proof of God's power and why adding extra premises is unnecessary.

Core Idea
The four-case objection is just the well-known argument with extra baggage.

The author begins by stating that contingency contradicts the negation of priority but not of posteriority. Then he presents an objection that claims the proof can be simplified into four possibilities if God were not powerful. He immediately responds that this is simply the standard argument with three unnecessary conditional premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial statement and objection
Start with the author's claim about contingency, then read the objection listing four possibilities if God were not powerful.
2
Identify the four possibilities
List the four cases: no contingent, contingent without agent, regress, or effect failure. Notice how they are derived.
3
Understand the author's response
The author says this is just the well-known argument with extra conditional premises. Grasp why he considers them unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالحدوث ينافي نفي الأولية، ولا ينافي نفي الآخرية.
لا يقال : قد يمكن تقرير هذا الاستدلال بحيث لا يفتقر إلى أحد الأمرين المذكورين، كما ذكر في المواقف من أنه لو لم يكن قادراً لزم إما نفي الحادث أو عدم استناده إلى المؤثر، أو التسلسل، أو تخلف الأثر عن المؤثر التام لأنه إن لم يوجد حادث أصلاً فهو الأمر الأول. وإن وجد فإن لم يستند إلى مؤثر فهو الثاني، وإن استند فإن لم ينته إلى قديم فهو الثالث، وإن انتهى فلا بد من قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة دفعاً للتسلسل وهو الرابع ، لأنا نقول هذا أيضاً تقرير الاستدلال المشهور بزيادة مقدمات لا حاجة إليها وهي الشرطيات الثلاث.
الأول : لأن الكلام في قادرية القديم الذي إليه ينتهي الكل، مع أن التالي في كل من الأولين عين المقدم، ولذا عدل عنه وقال : وإن شئت قلت : أي في تقرير هذا الاستدلال، لو كان الباري موجباً بالذات لزم قدم الحادث، إذ لو حدث لتوقف على شرط حادث وتسلسل، ثم إنه لا يتم إلا بما ذكرنا على ما اعترف به حيث قال: واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال يغني على التقديرين لا يتم إلا إن تبين حدوث ما سوى الله تعالى، وامتناع قيام جوادث متعاقبة لا نهاية لها بذاته أو تبين في الحادث اليومي أنه لا يستند إلى حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية محفوظاً بحركة دائمة، وذلك لأنه لو لم يتبين ما ذكر لم تصح الشرطية الرابعة من التقرير الأول، ويلزم المحال المذكور في التقرير الثاني لجواز أن تنتهي الحوادث إلى قديم يوجب قديماً يستند إليه الحوادث بطريق الاختيار دون الإيجاب، فلا يلزم التخلف ولا التسلسل، وأن لا يثبت قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة، بل يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بآخر من غير بداية كما هو رأيهم في الحركات ولا يكون هذا من التسلسل المسلم استحالته، أعني ترتب العلل والمعلولات لا إلى نهاية فلا بد من بيان استحالة النوع الآخر من التسلسل أعني كون كل حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية ليتم به الاستدلال.
In sum, contingency (al-ḥudūth) contradicts the negation of priority (al-awwaliyya), but it does not contradict the negation of posteriority (al-ākhiariyya). It should not be said: It is possible to formulate this argument such that it does not depend on either of the two mentioned things, as mentioned in al-Mawāqif: that if He were not powerful (qādiran), then either the negation of the contingent (al-ḥādith) would follow, or its lack of attribution to an agent (al-muʼaththir), or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), or the effect's failure to follow from a complete cause, because if there is no contingent at all, that is the first case; if there is but it is not attributed to an agent, that is the second; if it is attributed but does not end with an eternal being, that is the third; and if it ends, then there must be an eternal being that necessitates a contingent without intermediary to prevent regress—that is the fourth. For we say: This too is a formulation of the well-known argument with an addition of premises that are unnecessary, namely the three conditionals. The first: because the discussion concerns the power (qādariyya) of the eternal being to which everything ends, while the consequent in each of the first two is identical to the antecedent, so he turned away from it and said: 'And if you wish, you may say'—i.e., in formulating this argument: if the Creator were a necessitator (mūjiban) by essence, the eternity of the contingent would follow, for if it originated it would depend on a contingent condition and regress. Then it is not complete except by what we mentioned, as he admitted when he said: 'Know that this argument, despite being sufficient in both scenarios, is not complete unless it is shown that everything besides God is contingent (ḥudūth mā siwā Allāh), and the impossibility of successive contingents without end subsisting in His essence, or it is shown in the case of the daily contingent that it does not depend on a contingent preceded by another without end, preserved by a perpetual motion.' That is because if what was mentioned were not shown, the fourth conditional in the first formulation would not be valid, and the absurdity mentioned in the second formulation would follow—since it is possible that contingents end with an eternal being that necessitates an eternal being, to which the contingents are attributed by way of choice (al-ikhtiyār) without necessitation (al-ījāb), so neither failure nor regress follows, and that it is not established that there is an eternal being that necessitates a contingent without intermediary; rather, every contingent is preceded by another without beginning, as is their view regarding motions, and this is not the type of regress whose impossibility is accepted, namely the arrangement of causes and effects without end. Therefore, it is necessary to demonstrate the impossibility of the other type of regress, namely that every contingent is preceded by another without end, in order for the argument to be complete.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against a standard proof of God's power (qudra). The author first makes a general statement: contingency (ḥudūth) contradicts the negation of priority but not the negation of posteriority. Then he presents an objection: one might argue that the proof can be formulated without needing either of the two mentioned requirements, citing a formulation from al-Mawāqif. That formulation states that if God were not powerful, four possibilities arise: either no contingent exists, or a contingent exists without an agent, or it ends with an eternal being but with regress, or it ends with an eternal being that necessitates a contingent without intermediary. The objection claims this is a simpler formulation. The author responds by saying that this is still the same well-known argument but with unnecessary added premises—namely the three conditionals (al-sharṭiyyāt al-thalāth). He explains: the discussion concerns the power of the eternal being to which everything ends, yet in the first two possibilities the consequent is identical to the antecedent, so the objector turned away from that. Then the author presents an alternative formulation: if the Creator were a necessitator by essence, the contingent would be eternal, because if it originated it would require a contingent condition leading to regress. But this formulation is incomplete unless one establishes certain things: that everything besides God is contingent, and the impossibility of successive contingents without end subsisting in His essence, or that each daily contingent is not dependent on another contingent preceded by another ad infinitum, preserved by a perpetual motion. The reason is that without these, the fourth conditional in the first formulation fails, and the absurdity in the second formulation arises because it is possible that contingents end with an eternal being that necessitates an eternal being by choice rather than necessitation, so no failure or regress occurs, and it is not proven that there is an eternal being necessitating a contingent without intermediary. Instead, every contingent could be preceded by another without beginning, as the philosophers claim for motions—this is a type of regress whose impossibility is not automatically accepted (unlike the regress of causes and effects). Therefore, one must also demonstrate the impossibility of this other type of regress (every contingent preceded by another without end) to complete the argument.

Key Terms
حدوث (ḥudūth)
قادر (qādir)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, which of the following is a consequence if God were not powerful?
A) The world is eternal
B) No contingent exists
C) God is a necessitator by essence
D) The effect necessarily follows from the complete cause

Key Takeaway
The objection's four cases are not a new proof but a restatement of the standard argument with added conditionals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's power, do you find yourself adding unnecessary conditions or doubts? How can you simplify your trust in His absolute power, as this passage reminds you that the proof needs no extra premises?
?
The four cases in the objection all presuppose the absence of God's power. Which of these possibilities, if true, would most shake your faith? Are you able to affirm with certainty that none of them can be true because God is al-Qādir?

Completing the Proof: The Need to Refute Temporal Regress
Taftazani refines the proof and explains its necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof of God's power requires establishing the impossibility of an infinite temporal regress of contingents.

Core Idea
The argument fails unless we prove that every contingent is not preceded by an infinite series.

After dismissing the objection, the author provides a refined formulation of the proof and explains the conditions needed to complete it. He shows that unless we establish the impossibility of an infinite regress of contingents (temporal regress), the argument remains incomplete. This involves distinguishing between two types of regress: causal and temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the refined formulation
Read the alternative formulation: if the Creator were a necessitator, the contingent would be eternal; if it originated, regress would follow.
2
Identify the necessary conditions
Note the two conditions: proving everything besides God is contingent, and the impossibility of an infinite series of contingents subsisting in God's essence or an infinite chain of daily contingents preserved by perpetual motion.
3
Understand the distinction between regress types
Recognize that causal regress (causes without end) is impossible, but temporal regress (events without beginning) is not automatically impossible and must be disproven separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالحدوث ينافي نفي الأولية، ولا ينافي نفي الآخرية.
لا يقال : قد يمكن تقرير هذا الاستدلال بحيث لا يفتقر إلى أحد الأمرين المذكورين، كما ذكر في المواقف من أنه لو لم يكن قادراً لزم إما نفي الحادث أو عدم استناده إلى المؤثر، أو التسلسل، أو تخلف الأثر عن المؤثر التام لأنه إن لم يوجد حادث أصلاً فهو الأمر الأول. وإن وجد فإن لم يستند إلى مؤثر فهو الثاني، وإن استند فإن لم ينته إلى قديم فهو الثالث، وإن انتهى فلا بد من قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة دفعاً للتسلسل وهو الرابع ، لأنا نقول هذا أيضاً تقرير الاستدلال المشهور بزيادة مقدمات لا حاجة إليها وهي الشرطيات الثلاث.
الأول : لأن الكلام في قادرية القديم الذي إليه ينتهي الكل، مع أن التالي في كل من الأولين عين المقدم، ولذا عدل عنه وقال : وإن شئت قلت : أي في تقرير هذا الاستدلال، لو كان الباري موجباً بالذات لزم قدم الحادث، إذ لو حدث لتوقف على شرط حادث وتسلسل، ثم إنه لا يتم إلا بما ذكرنا على ما اعترف به حيث قال: واعلم أن هذا الاستدلال يغني على التقديرين لا يتم إلا إن تبين حدوث ما سوى الله تعالى، وامتناع قيام جوادث متعاقبة لا نهاية لها بذاته أو تبين في الحادث اليومي أنه لا يستند إلى حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية محفوظاً بحركة دائمة، وذلك لأنه لو لم يتبين ما ذكر لم تصح الشرطية الرابعة من التقرير الأول، ويلزم المحال المذكور في التقرير الثاني لجواز أن تنتهي الحوادث إلى قديم يوجب قديماً يستند إليه الحوادث بطريق الاختيار دون الإيجاب، فلا يلزم التخلف ولا التسلسل، وأن لا يثبت قديم يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة، بل يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بآخر من غير بداية كما هو رأيهم في الحركات ولا يكون هذا من التسلسل المسلم استحالته، أعني ترتب العلل والمعلولات لا إلى نهاية فلا بد من بيان استحالة النوع الآخر من التسلسل أعني كون كل حادث مسبوق بآخر لا إلى نهاية ليتم به الاستدلال.
In sum, contingency (al-ḥudūth) contradicts the negation of priority (al-awwaliyya), but it does not contradict the negation of posteriority (al-ākhiariyya). It should not be said: It is possible to formulate this argument such that it does not depend on either of the two mentioned things, as mentioned in al-Mawāqif: that if He were not powerful (qādiran), then either the negation of the contingent (al-ḥādith) would follow, or its lack of attribution to an agent (al-muʼaththir), or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), or the effect's failure to follow from a complete cause, because if there is no contingent at all, that is the first case; if there is but it is not attributed to an agent, that is the second; if it is attributed but does not end with an eternal being, that is the third; and if it ends, then there must be an eternal being that necessitates a contingent without intermediary to prevent regress—that is the fourth. For we say: This too is a formulation of the well-known argument with an addition of premises that are unnecessary, namely the three conditionals. The first: because the discussion concerns the power (qādariyya) of the eternal being to which everything ends, while the consequent in each of the first two is identical to the antecedent, so he turned away from it and said: 'And if you wish, you may say'—i.e., in formulating this argument: if the Creator were a necessitator (mūjiban) by essence, the eternity of the contingent would follow, for if it originated it would depend on a contingent condition and regress. Then it is not complete except by what we mentioned, as he admitted when he said: 'Know that this argument, despite being sufficient in both scenarios, is not complete unless it is shown that everything besides God is contingent (ḥudūth mā siwā Allāh), and the impossibility of successive contingents without end subsisting in His essence, or it is shown in the case of the daily contingent that it does not depend on a contingent preceded by another without end, preserved by a perpetual motion.' That is because if what was mentioned were not shown, the fourth conditional in the first formulation would not be valid, and the absurdity mentioned in the second formulation would follow—since it is possible that contingents end with an eternal being that necessitates an eternal being, to which the contingents are attributed by way of choice (al-ikhtiyār) without necessitation (al-ījāb), so neither failure nor regress follows, and that it is not established that there is an eternal being that necessitates a contingent without intermediary; rather, every contingent is preceded by another without beginning, as is their view regarding motions, and this is not the type of regress whose impossibility is accepted, namely the arrangement of causes and effects without end. Therefore, it is necessary to demonstrate the impossibility of the other type of regress, namely that every contingent is preceded by another without end, in order for the argument to be complete.

?

Two Arguments from Contingency and Necessitation
Taftazani presents the first two arguments for God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two rational arguments Taftazani uses to prove that God is powerful: the argument from the termination of contingents and the argument against necessitation in creating the world.

Core Idea
God must be powerful because contingency ends in a Necessary Being and creation cannot be by blind necessitation.

Taftazani begins his enumeration of proofs for God's power with two closely related arguments. The first builds on the established conclusion that all contingent events must terminate in a Necessary Being. The second examines the nature of God's creative act, showing it cannot be a form of necessitation. Together, they lay the foundation for understanding God as a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the two arguments
Read the Arabic passage for the first and second arguments, then their translation. Notice the logical structure: each argument presents an impossibility if God were not powerful.
The first argument uses the impossibility of delay (takhalluf) and infinite regress (tasalsul).
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument says: since contingents end with the Necessary Being, He must be powerful. If not, then either He would necessitate a contingent without an intermediary (leading to delay) or not (leading to regress). Both are impossible.
Key terms: takhalluf (delay of effect) and tasalsul (regress).
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument considers God's effect on the world. If it were by necessitation, it would require an eternal intermediary (making the world eternal) or a contingent one (leading to regress). Since the world is contingent, God must be a free agent.
This argument also refutes the view that God is a mere necessitating cause.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
These arguments are the first steps in a series. Notice how they set up the need for free will (ikhtiyār) in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولنعد من الأدلة عدة .
الأول: لما ثبت انتهاء الحوادث إلى الواجب لزم كونه قادراً وإلا فإما أن يوجب حادثاً بلا وسط فيلزم التخلف أو لا فيلزم التسلسل .
الثاني : تأثيره في وجود العالم إن كان بطريق الإيجاب فإما بلا وسط أو بوسط قديم فيلزم قدم العالم، وإما بوسط حادث فتتسلسل الحوادث .
الثالث : اختلاف الأجسام بعوارضها ليس للجسمية ولوازمها لكونها مشتركة وإلا لعوارض أو ذاتيات أو أجسام لها نوع اختصاص لامتناع التسلسل وبتعين الفاعل المختار، لأن نسبة الموجب إلى الكل على السواء.
الرابع : لو كان موجد العالم موجباً لزم من ارتفاعه ارتفاعه لأن ارتفاع الملزوم من لوازم ارتفاع اللازم لكن ارتفاع الواجب محال .
الخامس : اختصاص الكواكب والأقطاب بمحالها، والأفلاك بأماكنها لو لم يكن بإرادة القادر، لزم الترجح لأن نسبة الموجب إلى الكل على السواء.
السادس : فاعل أعضاء الحيوان وأشكاله إن كانت طبيعة أو مبدأ موجباً لزم كونه كرات مجردة أو متغامة فتعين القادر المختار وقد يتمسك بالأدلة السمعية من الإجماع وغيره، وبأن القدرة وغيرها صفات كمال وأضدادها سمات نقص، وبأن صانع العالم على أحكامه وانتظامه لا يكون إلا عالماً قادراً بحكم الضرورة، وهذه الوجوه ع ما فيها من مجال المناقشة ربما تفيد باجتماعها اليقين.
He said: Let us return to several arguments. The first: Since it is established that contingents end with the Necessary Being (al-wājib), it follows that He is powerful (qādiran); otherwise, either He would necessitate a contingent without an intermediary, leading to delay (al-takhalluf) [of effect], or not, leading to infinite regress (al-tasalsul). The second: His effect on the existence of the world—if it is by way of necessitation (al-ījāb), then either without an intermediary or with an eternal intermediary, leading to the eternity of the world (qidam al-‘ālam); or with a contingent intermediary, leading to regress of contingents. The third: The difference of bodies in their accidents (a‘rāḍ) is not due to corporeality (al-jismiyya) and its necessary concomitants (lawāzimuhā) because they are common; otherwise [it would be due] to accidents, or essential properties (dhātiyyāt), or bodies having a specific kind of specialization (ikhtiṣāṣ) due to the impossibility of regress, and this determines the free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār), because the relation of the necessitator (al-mūjib) to all is equal. The fourth: If the originator of the world (mūjid al-‘ālam) were a necessitator, then from His removal, its removal would follow, because the removal of the necessary condition (al-malzūm) is among the concomitants of the removal of the necessary consequent (al-lāzim), but the removal of the Necessary Being is impossible. The fifth: The specialization of planets and poles in their places, and the spheres in their positions—if it were not by the will of the Powerful (irādat al-qādir), then preponderance without a preponderator (al-tarajjuḥ) would follow, because the relation of the necessitator to all is equal. The sixth: The agent of the animal's organs and their shapes—if it were a nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating principle, then they would be pure spheres (kurāt mujarrada) or blurry (mutaghāmma), so the powerful free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) is determined. And one may also appeal to transmitted proofs (al-adilla al-sam‘iyya) such as consensus (al-ijmā‘) and others, and that power and other [attributes] are perfections and their opposites are defects, and that the Maker of the world, given its perfection and order, cannot be other than knowing and powerful by necessity (bi-ḥukm al-ḍarūra). These points, despite having room for objection (majāl al-munāqasha), when combined perhaps yield certainty (al-yaqīn).
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage enumerates six rational arguments (adilla) proving that God is powerful (qādir). The author begins with 'He said: Let us return to several arguments.' The first argument: Since we have established that contingent beings (al-ḥawādith) terminate at the Necessary Being (al-wājib), He must be powerful. If He were not, then either He would necessitate a contingent without an intermediary—but this would lead to delay of the effect (takhalluf), which is impossible—or He would not necessitate it, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is also impossible. Thus, He must be powerful. The second argument: God's effect on the existence of the world (al-‘ālam) cannot be by necessitation (ījāb). If it were by necessitation, then either there is no intermediary—which would make the world eternal (since a necessary cause with no barrier necessitates its effect immediately)—or there is an eternal intermediary, again leading to the world's eternity. Or there is a contingent intermediary, leading to an infinite regress of contingents. Since all these are impossible, God must act by choice (ikhtiyār), i.e., be powerful. The third argument: Bodies (ajsām) differ in their accidents (a‘rāḍ). This difference cannot be due to corporeality (jismiyya) itself or its necessary concomitants, because these are common to all bodies. It cannot be due to other accidents or essential properties, as that would lead to regress. It must be due to a specific specialization (ikhtiṣāṣ) from a free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār), because a necessitator (al-mūjib) has an equal relation to all things and cannot produce variation without a preponderator (murajjiḥ), which itself must be a free chooser. The fourth argument: If the originator of the world were a necessitator, then from His removal the world's removal would necessarily follow (since the removal of the cause necessitates removal of the effect). But the removal of the Necessary Being is impossible, so the world could never cease. Yet the world is contingent and subject to change—so the originator must be powerful, capable of acting or not. The fifth argument: The special positions of planets, poles, and spheres cannot be due to necessitation. If they were, their relation to all positions would be equal, so preponderance without a preponderator would follow. Therefore, they must be placed by the will of a powerful agent. The sixth argument: The design of animal organs and shapes cannot be the result of blind nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating principle (mabda’ mūjib). If it were, the organs would be uniform spheres or blurry forms. Since they are intricate and varied, the agent must be powerful and free. The author then mentions that one may also add transmitted proofs (al-adilla al-sam‘iyya) like consensus (ijmā‘), and rational arguments that power and its like are perfections (ṣifāt kamāl) while their opposites are defects (nuqṣ), and that the Maker of such a perfectly ordered world must be knowing and powerful by necessity (bi-ḥukm al-ḍarūra). The author concludes that although each of these arguments may have room for objection (majāl al-munāqasha), when combined they can yield certainty (al-yaqīn).

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
واجب (wājib)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
تخلف (takhalluf)
إيجاب (ījāb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God is necessary, He must produce the world necessarily, like a cause necessitating its effect.
Correction: This passage shows that necessary existence does not imply necessitation; rather, God acts by will and power, as the impossibility of necessitation here demonstrates.

Checkpoint
In the first argument, what two impossibilities does Taftazani mention if God were not powerful?
A) Delay and regress
B) Eternity and regress
C) Necessitation and free will
D) Causality and contingency

Key Takeaway
The first two arguments establish that God must be powerful and cannot be a mere necessitating cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were only a necessitating cause, your life would be mechanical and purposeless. Do you truly live as though you were created by a free, powerful God who chose you for a purpose? How does that truth change the way you face your daily trials? Will you remind yourself of this when you feel like a victim of circumstances?
?
The second argument demolishes the idea that the world could be eternal—it must have a beginning. Have you pondered that your own life, too, had a beginning and will have an end? How does that urgency shape your priorities today? Will you seize this moment to repent and draw closer to the Powerful One?
?
Both arguments hinge on the impossibility of infinite regress. Yet your heart often seeks security in infinite chains—money, status, relationships—as if they were ultimate. Are you relying on secondary causes as if they were necessary? Will you declare today that only the Necessary Being is your true sustainer, and break the chains of false dependencies?

Two Arguments from Variation and Necessity
Taftazani presents the third and fourth arguments for God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how variations in bodies and the impossibility of a necessary cause ceasing demonstrate that God is a powerful free agent.

Core Idea
Variation in bodies and the impossibility of God's removal prove He acts by choice, not necessity.

Taftazani's third and fourth arguments address two different aspects: first, why bodies differ in their accidents, showing this cannot be from a necessitator; second, why the originator of the world cannot be a necessitator, otherwise the world would be eternal. Both point to a God who acts freely.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic for the third argument and its translation. Notice how it addresses the variation in bodies and leads to the conclusion of a free agent.
The argument involves the impossibility of regress and the equal relation of a necessitator.
2
Analyze the third argument's reasoning
The difference in bodies' accidents cannot come from corporeality (shared by all bodies) nor from other accidents or essential properties (would lead to regress). Hence, it must come from a specific specialization by a free agent, since a necessitator would produce uniformity.
Key term: ikhtiṣāṣ (specialization).
3
Read the fourth argument
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth argument. It uses the concept of necessary condition (malzūm) and consequent (lāzim).
4
Understand the fourth argument's logic
If God were a necessitator, His removal would necessitate the world's removal. But God's removal is impossible, so the world would be eternal, which is false. Therefore, God is not a necessitator but a powerful free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولنعد من الأدلة عدة .
الأول: لما ثبت انتهاء الحوادث إلى الواجب لزم كونه قادراً وإلا فإما أن يوجب حادثاً بلا وسط فيلزم التخلف أو لا فيلزم التسلسل .
الثاني : تأثيره في وجود العالم إن كان بطريق الإيجاب فإما بلا وسط أو بوسط قديم فيلزم قدم العالم، وإما بوسط حادث فتتسلسل الحوادث .
الثالث : اختلاف الأجسام بعوارضها ليس للجسمية ولوازمها لكونها مشتركة وإلا لعوارض أو ذاتيات أو أجسام لها نوع اختصاص لامتناع التسلسل وبتعين الفاعل المختار، لأن نسبة الموجب إلى الكل على السواء.
الرابع : لو كان موجد العالم موجباً لزم من ارتفاعه ارتفاعه لأن ارتفاع الملزوم من لوازم ارتفاع اللازم لكن ارتفاع الواجب محال .
الخامس : اختصاص الكواكب والأقطاب بمحالها، والأفلاك بأماكنها لو لم يكن بإرادة القادر، لزم الترجح لأن نسبة الموجب إلى الكل على السواء.
السادس : فاعل أعضاء الحيوان وأشكاله إن كانت طبيعة أو مبدأ موجباً لزم كونه كرات مجردة أو متغامة فتعين القادر المختار وقد يتمسك بالأدلة السمعية من الإجماع وغيره، وبأن القدرة وغيرها صفات كمال وأضدادها سمات نقص، وبأن صانع العالم على أحكامه وانتظامه لا يكون إلا عالماً قادراً بحكم الضرورة، وهذه الوجوه ع ما فيها من مجال المناقشة ربما تفيد باجتماعها اليقين.
He said: Let us return to several arguments. The first: Since it is established that contingents end with the Necessary Being (al-wājib), it follows that He is powerful (qādiran); otherwise, either He would necessitate a contingent without an intermediary, leading to delay (al-takhalluf) [of effect], or not, leading to infinite regress (al-tasalsul). The second: His effect on the existence of the world—if it is by way of necessitation (al-ījāb), then either without an intermediary or with an eternal intermediary, leading to the eternity of the world (qidam al-‘ālam); or with a contingent intermediary, leading to regress of contingents. The third: The difference of bodies in their accidents (a‘rāḍ) is not due to corporeality (al-jismiyya) and its necessary concomitants (lawāzimuhā) because they are common; otherwise [it would be due] to accidents, or essential properties (dhātiyyāt), or bodies having a specific kind of specialization (ikhtiṣāṣ) due to the impossibility of regress, and this determines the free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār), because the relation of the necessitator (al-mūjib) to all is equal. The fourth: If the originator of the world (mūjid al-‘ālam) were a necessitator, then from His removal, its removal would follow, because the removal of the necessary condition (al-malzūm) is among the concomitants of the removal of the necessary consequent (al-lāzim), but the removal of the Necessary Being is impossible. The fifth: The specialization of planets and poles in their places, and the spheres in their positions—if it were not by the will of the Powerful (irādat al-qādir), then preponderance without a preponderator (al-tarajjuḥ) would follow, because the relation of the necessitator to all is equal. The sixth: The agent of the animal's organs and their shapes—if it were a nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating principle, then they would be pure spheres (kurāt mujarrada) or blurry (mutaghāmma), so the powerful free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) is determined. And one may also appeal to transmitted proofs (al-adilla al-sam‘iyya) such as consensus (al-ijmā‘) and others, and that power and other [attributes] are perfections and their opposites are defects, and that the Maker of the world, given its perfection and order, cannot be other than knowing and powerful by necessity (bi-ḥukm al-ḍarūra). These points, despite having room for objection (majāl al-munāqasha), when combined perhaps yield certainty (al-yaqīn).
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage enumerates six rational arguments (adilla) proving that God is powerful (qādir). The author begins with 'He said: Let us return to several arguments.' The first argument: Since we have established that contingent beings (al-ḥawādith) terminate at the Necessary Being (al-wājib), He must be powerful. If He were not, then either He would necessitate a contingent without an intermediary—but this would lead to delay of the effect (takhalluf), which is impossible—or He would not necessitate it, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is also impossible. Thus, He must be powerful. The second argument: God's effect on the existence of the world (al-‘ālam) cannot be by necessitation (ījāb). If it were by necessitation, then either there is no intermediary—which would make the world eternal (since a necessary cause with no barrier necessitates its effect immediately)—or there is an eternal intermediary, again leading to the world's eternity. Or there is a contingent intermediary, leading to an infinite regress of contingents. Since all these are impossible, God must act by choice (ikhtiyār), i.e., be powerful. The third argument: Bodies (ajsām) differ in their accidents (a‘rāḍ). This difference cannot be due to corporeality (jismiyya) itself or its necessary concomitants, because these are common to all bodies. It cannot be due to other accidents or essential properties, as that would lead to regress. It must be due to a specific specialization (ikhtiṣāṣ) from a free agent (al-fā‘il al-mukhtār), because a necessitator (al-mūjib) has an equal relation to all things and cannot produce variation without a preponderator (murajjiḥ), which itself must be a free chooser. The fourth argument: If the originator of the world were a necessitator, then from His removal the world's removal would necessarily follow (since the removal of the cause necessitates removal of the effect). But the removal of the Necessary Being is impossible, so the world could never cease. Yet the world is contingent and subject to change—so the originator must be powerful, capable of acting or not. The fifth argument: The special positions of planets, poles, and spheres cannot be due to necessitation. If they were, their relation to all positions would be equal, so preponderance without a preponderator would follow. Therefore, they must be placed by the will of a powerful agent. The sixth argument: The design of animal organs and shapes cannot be the result of blind nature (ṭabī‘a) or a necessitating principle (mabda’ mūjib). If it were, the organs would be uniform spheres or blurry forms. Since they are intricate and varied, the agent must be powerful and free. The author then mentions that one may also add transmitted proofs (al-adilla al-sam‘iyya) like consensus (ijmā‘), and rational arguments that power and its like are perfections (ṣifāt kamāl) while their opposites are defects (nuqṣ), and that the Maker of such a perfectly ordered world must be knowing and powerful by necessity (bi-ḥukm al-ḍarūra). The author concludes that although each of these arguments may have room for objection (majāl al-munāqasha), when combined they can yield certainty (al-yaqīn).

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
موجب (mūjib)
مختار (mukhtār)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
اختصاص (ikhtiṣāṣ)
ملزوم (malzūm)
لازم (lāzim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that variation in bodies could arise from matter itself without an external agent.
Correction: Taftazani argues that since corporeality is common to all bodies, it cannot account for specific differences; a free agent is needed to assign specific accidents.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude if the originator of the world were a necessitator?
A) The world would be contingent
B) The world would be eternal
C) The world would have a beginning
D) The world would be created in time

Key Takeaway
Variation in bodies and the contingency of the world prove God acts by free choice, not necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the fact that God acts by free choice—not necessity—change the way you pray to Him? Do you treat your requests as if they are automatically answered by a 'law' or do you genuinely appeal to His will?
?
If God were a necessitator, your existence would be compulsory—like a shadow following its object. Does that thought make you uncomfortable? Why does knowing that God chose to create you—and could have not—make your existence more precious?
?
The third argument says that the diversity in creation cannot come from a mere physical law; it requires a specific, free grant from God. Look at one unique aspect of your own body or life: how does acknowledging that it came from God's specific choice—not blind chance—affect your gratitude and responsibility?

Two Arguments from Design and the Certainty of Combination
Taftazani presents the fifth and sixth arguments and concludes with remarks on certainty through multiple proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the specific positions of celestial bodies and the intricate design of animal organs prove God's power, and why combining all arguments yields certainty.

Core Idea
Design in the heavens and living beings requires a powerful free agent; many weak arguments together can yield certainty.

The final two arguments focus on design: the precise placement of planets and spheres, and the complex shapes of animal organs. Both point to a powerful free agent. Taftazani then mentions additional proofs and notes that while each argument alone may be open to objection, their combination produces certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the fifth argument
Read the Arabic and translation for the fifth argument. It deals with the specific positions of celestial bodies.
The argument uses the principle of preponderance without a preponderator (tarajjuḥ bilā murajjiḥ).
2
Understand the fifth argument
Planets, poles, and spheres have specific locations. A necessitator would have equal relation to all positions, so without a will, any specific placement would be preponderance without a preponderator—impossible. Hence, the placement is by the will of a powerful agent.
3
Read the sixth argument
Read the Arabic and translation for the sixth argument. It concerns the design of animal bodies.
4
Understand the sixth argument and conclusion
Animal organs have intricate shapes. If they came from nature or a necessitating principle, they would be uniform spheres or blurry forms. Their complexity points to a powerful free agent. Taftazani then adds that transmitted proofs and the perfection argument support this, and that despite possible objections, the accumulation of arguments can yield certainty.
Key phrase: 'ربما تفيد باجتماعها اليقين' (perhaps their combination yields certainty).

Source Text

?

?
Taftazani admits that each argument can be objected to, yet the combination gives certainty. What is that one objection about God's existence that keeps nagging at you? Are you willing to step back and see the whole picture of arguments, not just the weak point? What would it take for you to let the cumulative evidence bring you to certainty?
?
God’s power is shown in the intricate design of your own body—your eyes, hands, and organs. If He is the powerful free agent behind your physical form, then haven't you been living as if you own your body and its abilities? What is one practical change in your daily routine that would acknowledge that He is the true agent of every movement you make?

Certainty from Design for the Truth Seeker
A concise statement on the teleological argument's yield of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the argument from design provides decisive certainty for the sincere seeker of truth, not mere probability.

Core Idea
The design in creation yields certainty, not probability, for the truthful seeker.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses a common objection: that the argument from design only yields probability. The author insists that for a sincere seeker, the observed order of the heavens and earth leads to decisive certainty. We will explore the distinction between the philosopher's view and the seeker's attainment of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the contrast between the philosopher's claim and the sincere seeker's result.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the key terms: الواجب, معلولاته, الفلسفي, القطع واليقين.
2
Identify the two attributions
The passage mentions two ways the observed order is attributed: directly to the Necessary Being or through some of His effects. Recognize this distinction.
The philosopher holds the second view, but the author does not endorse it.
3
Reflect on the certainty claim
Consider why the sincere seeker can achieve 'cutting proofs and certainty' (القطع واليقين) without probability, despite the philosophical complication.
The sincerity of the seeker is key; the argument itself is sufficient for an unbiased mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ما يشاهد من أمر السماء والأرض مستند إلى الواجب بلا واسطة إلى بعض معلولاته على ما يزعم الفلسفي ، لكن من كان طالباً للحق غير هائم في أودية الضلال ربما يستفيد من هذه لوجوه القطع واليقين بلا احتمال .
That what is observed of the affair of the heavens and the earth is attributed to the Necessary Being without intermediary or to some of His effects, according to what the philosopher claims, but whoever is a seeker of truth, not wandering in the valleys of misguidance, may benefit from these [proofs] to [attain] aspects of decisive certainty without probability.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a concise statement about the teleological argument (argument from design). It acknowledges two views: either the observed order in the heavens and earth is directly attributed to the Necessary Being (God) without any intermediary, or it is attributed to some of His effects (i.e., secondary causes) as the philosophers (specifically those influenced by Aristotelian or Neoplatonic philosophy) claim. The author then states that despite this philosophical complication, a sincere seeker of truth who is not lost in misguidance can still benefit from this evidence, arriving at 'cutting proofs and certainty' (القطع واليقين) rather than mere probability. This highlights that the argument from design is sufficient to produce certain knowledge for an unbiased mind.

Key Terms
الواجب (the Necessary Being)
معلولاته (His effects)
الفلسفي (the philosopher)
القطع واليقين (decisive certainty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from design only provides probability, not certainty.
Correction: The author asserts that a sincere seeker can attain decisive certainty (القطع واليقين) from observing the order of the heavens and earth, contrary to the philosopher's claim that it leads only to probability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the orderly arrangement of the heavens and earth demonstrate?
A) It demonstrates mere probability about the existence of God.
B) It demonstrates decisive certainty (القطع واليقين) for the truth seeker.
C) It demonstrates that the philosopher's view is correct.
D) It demonstrates that the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
A sincere seeker of truth can derive decisive certainty from the design in the universe, without falling into probability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the heavens and the earth, do you perceive mere probability of a Creator, or do you allow the evidence to produce decisive certainty in your heart? What blocks you from moving beyond probability?
?
In what specific area of your life are you 'wandering in the valleys of misguidance,' clinging to doubts about Allah's existence or attributes? Will you repent from that wandering and seek truth as a sincere seeker today?
?
If you were to die tonight, would you face Allah with certainty or with lingering doubts? What step will you take right now to transform your intellectual acknowledgment into unwavering faith?

Proof of God's Knowledge from the Order of Creation
The author presents a rational proof for God's knowledge using two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's knowledge (عالم) based on the perfect order and arrangement of the universe, as presented by the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
The perfect order of creation necessarily implies a knowing Creator.

This passage presents a foundational proof used by theologians to establish God's attribute of knowledge. It is based on a simple observation: whenever we see a perfectly arranged piece of work, we know with certainty that its maker possessed knowledge. The author applies this principle to the entire universe, demonstrating that its intricate order points directly to a knowledgeable Creator, and that relying solely on scripture for this is circular.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the author states the conclusion, then gives the proof with two premises.
The passage begins with 'المبحث الثالث في أنه عالم' and ends with 'وإلى ما يقول'.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof has a major premise (الكبرى) and a minor premise (الصغرى). Find each one and note how the author supports them.
The major premise is that a perfectly solid act implies a knowledgeable agent. The minor is that God created the universe with perfect order.
3
Understand the major premise
The author says the major premise is known by necessity (بالضرورة). Observe the example: beautiful lines or eloquent words indicate a knowing author. Think of examples in everyday life.
This is a rational intuition, not something learned from scripture.
4
Understand the minor premise
The minor premise is that God created the heavens, elements, minerals, plants, and animals with harmony and perfection. The author cites specific sciences (astronomy, anatomy) that reveal this order.
The sciences witness the intricate design that man cannot fully comprehend.
5
Reflect on the conclusion and the role of scripture
From the two premises, the conclusion follows necessarily: God is knowing. Note the author's remark that relying on scripture alone (التمسك بالسمعيات) is circular.
This proof is rational, not dependent on revelation, making it universally accessible.

المبحث الثالث في أنه تعالى عالم . قال : المبحث الثالث في أنه عالم . أما عندنا فإنه صانع للعالم على انتظامه، وأحكامه، ولأنه قادر مختار لما مر . وما يشاهد من بعض الحيوانات لو صح أنه فعلها لدل على عملها، وأما التمسك بالسمعيات فدور). : اتفق عليه جمهور العقلاء، والمشهور من استدلال المتكلمين وجهان الأول : أنه فاعل فعلاً محكماً متقناً، وكل من كان ذلك فهو عالم ، أما الكبرى فالبضرورة ونبه عليه أن من رأى خطوطاً مليحة، أو سمع ألفاظاً فصيحة، تنبىء عن معادن دقيقة ، وأغراض صحيحة علم قطعاً أن فاعلها عالم ، وأما الصغرى فلما ثبت من أنه خالق للأفلاك (1) والعناصر بما فيها من الأعراض والجواهر، وأنواع المعادن والنبات، وأصناف الحيوانات على اتساق وانتظام، واتقان، وإحكام تحار فيها العقول والأفهام، ولا تعفى بتفاصيلها الدفاتر والأقلام على ما يشهد بذلك علم الهيئة (۲) ، وعلم التشريح ، وعلم الاثار العلوية والسفلية، وعلم الحيوان أن الإنسان لم يؤت من العلم إلا قليلاً، ولم يجد إلى الكنه سبيلاً. والنبات، مع فكيف إذا رقي إلى عالم الروحانيات من الأرضيات والسماويات، وإلى ما يقول
The third investigation: concerning that He (Exalted) is knowing. He said: The third investigation: concerning that He is knowing. As for according to us, He is the Maker of the world in its order and its perfect solidity, and because He is powerful, choosing, as has passed. And what is observed from some animals—if it were established that He did it, it would indicate His action. As for clinging to the scriptural evidences, it is circular. It is agreed upon by the majority of the rational people. The well-known from the argumentation of the mutakallimun is two aspects. The first: that He is an agent of a perfectly solid and accurate act, and everyone who is like that is knowing. As for the major premise, it is by necessity. And he drew attention to it: that whoever sees beautiful lines or hears eloquent words that indicate precise meanings and correct purposes knows with certainty that their agent is knowing. As for the minor premise, it is because it is established that He is the Creator of the celestial spheres (1) and the elements including what is in them of accidents and substances, the types of minerals and plants, and the species of animals, in a harmonious and regular, accurate and solid arrangement that bewilders intellects and understandings, and the books and pens cannot encompass their details, as is witnessed by the science of astronomy (2), the science of anatomy, the science of the upper and lower effects, and the science of animals—that man has not been given of knowledge except a little, and he has not found a way to the essence. And the plants, how?—when one ascends to the world of spiritualities from the earthly and heavenly things, and to what He says.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for God's knowledge (عالم) from the order and perfection of creation. It begins by stating that according to us (the Ash'ari school), God is the Maker of the world with its order and solidity, and He is powerful and choosing. The proof is agreed upon by most rational people. The well-known argument of the mutakallimun has two premises (وجهان). The first premise (major) is that whoever produces a perfectly solid and accurate act is knowledgeable (عالم). This is known by necessity: when one sees beautiful lines or hears eloquent words that convey precise meanings, one knows with certainty that the agent is knowledgeable. The second premise (minor) is that God is the Creator of the celestial spheres, elements, minerals, plants, and animals—all in a harmonious, regular, accurate, and solid arrangement that bewilders the intellect. Even books cannot encompass their details, as evidenced by astronomy, anatomy, the study of celestial and terrestrial phenomena, and zoology. The author notes that man has been given little knowledge and cannot reach the essence of things. He then rhetorically asks how much more baffling it would be to ascend to the spiritual realms. From these two premises, the conclusion necessarily follows: God is knowing. The author also mentions that relying solely on scriptural evidence is circular and thus not the primary proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof for God's knowledge is based on scripture, not reason.
Correction: The author explicitly states that clinging to scriptural evidences (التمسك بالسمعيات) is circular, and the proof he presents is rational, based on necessity and observation.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise (الكبرى) in this proof?
A) The universe is perfectly ordered
B) Every agent of a perfectly solid act is knowing
C) The Creator is powerful and choosing
D) Scriptural evidence is sufficient

Key Takeaway
The ordered universe necessitates a knowing Creator, a truth known by rational necessity, not dependent on scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the intricate order of your own body—the 'science of anatomy' mentioned in this proof—do you really see it as undeniable evidence of a Knowing Creator, or have you become desensitized? What one step can you take today to reawaken that certainty in your heart?
?
The author says that man has been given only a little knowledge and cannot grasp the essence of things. How does this humble assessment of your own intellect change the way you approach rational proofs like this one? Are you willing to submit to the rational necessity it demands, or do you hold back?
?
If the proof of God's knowledge is as clear as recognizing a skilled calligrapher from beautiful writing, why do you still sometimes live as if God does not see or know your actions? What specific habit or thought pattern do you need to change today to align your life with this truth?
